1 
 
Molecular Views on Fischer-Tropsch Synthesis 
Konstantijn Tom Rommens,† and Mark Saeys*,† 
†Laboratory for Chemical Technology, Ghent University, Technologiepark 125, 9052 Gent, Belgium 
Corresponding author’s e-mail address: Mark.Saeys@UGent.be 
Abstract 
For nearly a century, the Fischer-Tropsch (FT) reaction has been subject of intense debate. Various 
molecular views on the active sites and on the reaction mechanism have been presented, both for Co- 
and Fe-based FT. In the last 15 years, the emergence of a surface-science- and molecular-modeling-
based bottom-up approach has brought this molecular picture a step closer. Theoretical models 
provided a structural picture of the Co catalyst particles. Recent surface science experiments and 
density functional theory (DFT) calculations highlighted the importance of realistic surface coverages, 
which can induce surface reconstruction and impact the stability of reaction intermediates. For Co-
based FT, detailed microkinetic simulations and mechanistic experiments are moving towards a 
consensus about the active sites and the reaction mechanism. The dynamic phase evolution of Fe-
based catalysts under reaction conditions complicates identification of the surface structure and the 
active sites. New techniques can help tackle the combinatorial complexity in these systems. 
Experimental and DFT studies have addressed the mechanism for Fe-based catalysts; the absence of a 
clear molecular picture of the active sites, however, limits the development of a molecular view of the 
mechanism. Finally, direct CO2 hydrogenation to long-chain hydrocarbons could present a sustainable 
pathway for the FT synthesis.

3 
 
1 Introduction 
In 1923, Franz Fischer and Hans Tropsch discovered that a mixture of synthetic oil compounds (Synthol) 
formed from syngas (CO + H2) over an alkalized iron catalyst1. After further development, the process 
was optimized for Co- and Fe-based catalysts and coal-derived syngas was successfully converted into 
hydrocarbons, resulting in the submission of a patent in the summer of 19252,3. 
Since then, Fischer-Tropsch Synthesis (FTS) has become the largest-scale catalytic process, with single 
plants producing up to 8 Mton per year, and with an increasing number of commissioned industrial 
plants. The industrial relevance of FTS has motivated both academic and industrial research. While FTS 
will soon celebrate its centennial, a molecular view of the dominant reaction mechanism and of the 
active sites remains elusive. Independent of the detailed reaction mechanism, FTS follows a surface 
polymerization mechanism, resulting in a distribution of hydrocarbons that follows a typical Anderson-
Schulz-Flory distribution4-7. In situ formation of the monomers from the reactants CO and H2, often 
termed “initiation”, is followed by chain-growth and chain length independent termination to form the 
olefin, paraffin and/or oxygenate products. In addition, oxygen is removed as H2O or CO2, depending 
on the catalyst and the reaction conditions. When considering these three families of reactions for 
hydrocarbon chains of length 1 up to 40, the detailed mechanism for FTS easily consists of more than 
10 000 elementary reaction steps. Since the discovery of FTS in 1923, various reaction pathways have 
been proposed, with the three most prominent the carbide mechanism, the CO-insertion mechanism 
and the Enol/Hydroxycarbene/Oxymethylene-mechanism (Figure 1)1,2,6,8,9. 

4 
 
 
Figure 1. Reaction scheme of Fischer-Tropsch synthesis, summarizing the 3 most common chain-growth mechanisms: a) 
Carbide mechanism, b) CO-insertion mechanism and c) Hydroxycarbene mechanism. In the carbide mechanism and the 
hydroxycarbene mechanism the monomer is also the chain-initiator. 
Fischer and Tropsch originally proposed that a metal carbide reacts with H2 to produce methylene 
species (CH2*), which act as the chain-growth monomers2. This mechanism, aptly termed the carbide 
mechanism, has been supported by various experiments10-14 and is still widely used, albeit in a slightly 
modified form15-19. Later, C* and CH* have been proposed as possible monomers, with alkylidynes 
(RC*) instead of alkyls (RCH2*) as the growing chains17,20. In the carbide mechanism, the CHx* 
monomers are formed by CO activation followed by hydrogenation while termination forms olefins, 
paraffins and oxygenates. In the CO insertion mechanism, CO acts as the monomer which inserts in the 
growing chain, followed by hydrogenation and C-O cleavage. The CO insertion mechanism hence easily 
accounts for the formation of oxygenates. It differs from the carbide mechanism mainly by the 
sequence of C-C bond formation and C=O bond cleavage; in the CO insertion mechanism, the C-C bond 
forms first; in the carbide mechanism, the C=O bond cleaves first. 
Inspired by homogeneous catalysis, the CO insertion mechanism was originally proposed by Pichler 
and Schulz8. Experimental support comes from the work of Schweicher et al.21, while Zhuo et al. 
presented theoretical evidence22. On cobalt terraces (Co(111) and Co(0001)), which are abundant on 
catalytic particles, CO insertion has a lower barrier than direct CO dissociation16,22-24. Instead of CO 

5 
 
insertion, CO* can be first hydrogenated to form HCO*24,25. Further hydrogenation forms an 
hydroxycarbene (HCOH*). In the hydroxycarbene mechanism, HCOH* undergoes condensation 
polymerization with a growing RCOH* chain6,9. Experimental evidence for this mechanism is limited18. 
To unravel the mechanisms of the FT reaction, a variety of surface science studies, computational 
studies and more recently microkinetic models have been developed18,26,27. The absence of a detailed 
molecular movie of the FTS mechanism stimulates intense debate28-30. In this review, we summarize 
recent insights in the molecular mechanism and in the nature and structure of the active sites. 
1.1 Brief history of Fischer-Tropsch synthesis 
To understand the waves of interest in FTS throughout the 20th century and the revival during the last 
20 years, it is interesting to look at the drivers. After the discovery of the reaction in 1923, research 
was mainly limited to the lab of Franz Fischer at the Kaiser-Wilhelm-Institut für Kohlenforschung in 
Germany31. Interest was driven by the abundance of coal, and the potential to convert coal to liquid 
fuels. The first patent was filed in 1925 for a reaction over cobalt and iron catalysts at atmospheric 
pressure and below 300 °C, followed by a patent in 1930 which describes metallic cobalt as the active 
component3,32,33. The first industrial plant came on stream already in 1935 and by 1945 produced 600 
kton of liquid fuel per year from coal31,34,35. After World War II, increasing demand for liquid fuels and 
less optimistic oil forecasts promoted interest in FTS outside Germany. The Carthage Hydrocol Fischer-
Tropsch plant is an example of an early high-temperature Fe-based process (HTFT) with a fluidized bed 
reactor31,35. This plant was discontinued due to unfavorable economics and technical challenges. The 
‘iron medium-pressure synthesis’ was invented by Fischer and Pichler36 and commercialized by Sasol 
in South Africa in 195531,34,37. This plant, Sasol I, integrated HTFT in a circulating fluidized bed reactor 
developed by the Kellogg company and Low-Temperature FT (LTFT, ARGE process) in a fixed bed 
reactor. The plant has undergone various plant design changes since its start-up (e.g., a transformation 
to a gas-to-liquid (GTL) plant in 2004)35. 

6 
 
During the ‘oil age’, global research interest in FTS plummeted, with the exception of South Africa. The 
oil crisis in the 70s and 80s revived FTS research in Europe, US and Japan, especially coal-based FT 
processes31. During this time, several studies focused on unraveling the complicated reaction 
mechanisms that govern the FT reaction38. In the 80s, two additional Sasol plants were commissioned 
at Secunda in South Africa (Sasol II and III), employing the circulating fluidized bed technology (Synthol) 
demonstrated in Sasol I, increasing FT production to 6 Mton per year37. Since 1993, a new GTL plant at 
Mossel Bay in South Africa is operated by PetroSA, employing the HTFT Synthol technology (1 Mton 
per year)35,37. At the same time, the first Co-based GTL plant by Shell started production in Bintulu, 
Malaysia, based on cobalt medium-pressure synthesis developed by Pichler and Schulz (500 kton per 
year), using fixed bed reactors36,39. In the late 90s, the Sasol II and III reactors were replaced by Sasol 
Advanced Synthol (SAS) reactors, using conventional fluidized bed technology40. Development of Co- 
and Fe-based LTFT slurry bed reactor technology led to the implementation of Fe-based slurry bed 
reactors in Sasol I in 199341. 
At the turn of the century, FTS experienced a new surge due to the interest in converting stranded gas. 
This led to the development of GTL plants and to the transformation of existing coal-to-liquid (CTL) 
plants31. The Sasol Oryx GTL plant using LTFT cobalt-based technology was commissioned in 2006, 
followed shortly by the Shell Pearl GTL process based on the Shell Middle Distillate Synthesis 
technology35. In China, the primary energy source remains coal, and progress has been made in 
upscaling FT technology for CTL plants, with the Yitai and Luan demonstration CTL plants as 
exponents42. The largest industrial project to date is the Shenhua-Ningmei plant, which was 
commissioned in 2017 and has an estimated production of 4 Mton/year. This plant employs the 
Medium Temperature FT process developed by Synfuels China, similar to the Shanxi Luan High Sulphur 
Coal Co-production project (1.1 Mton/year). In 2015, the Yankuang Shaanxi Yulin project was 
commissioned (1 Mton/year). China has an installed CTL capacity of 8 Mton/year, which generates a 
carbon footprint of more than 60 Mton/year43. Significant efforts are necessary to make these 
processes more carbon-neutral. 

7 
 
Environmental concerns and efforts to reduce fossil fuel dependency are driving the development of 
biomass-based FTS processes. Very recently, research is shifting towards CO2-FT, where CO2 is used 
instead of CO, in combination with blue or green hydrogen. Both integrated processes employing 
tandem catalysis and two-stage processes combining electrochemical syngas production with 
conventional FTS are being studied18,44. 
1.2 Fischer-Tropsch catalysts 
While Fe-based catalysts were the first to be used in the FTS process, cobalt, ruthenium, rhodium and 
nickel also catalyze CO hydrogenation. Already in 1902, Sabatier and Senderens synthesized methane 
by reacting a mixture of COx and H2 over cobalt and nickel catalysts45. Since Co, Ru, Rh, and Ni are 
mostly metallic under reaction conditions, they have been somewhat easier to study using surface 
science and computational catalysis, and a molecular view of the catalyst structure and of the reaction 
mechanism is emerging. We will therefore begin this review by discussing the structure of cobalt 
catalysts and the corresponding FTS mechanisms. Fe-based catalysts are more complex due to the co-
existence of several iron carbide and iron oxide phases along with metallic iron under FT conditions. 
Fe-based catalysts are moreover often heavily promoted31,46-48 and the selectivity of Fe-based FT 
catalysts can be tuned46. Their water-gas shift (WGS) activity makes Fe-based catalysts a good choice 
for CO2-based FT processes44,49. Recent extensive research in Fe-based FT, in combination with the 
advent of new experimental and computational techniques, is beginning to enhance our molecular 
understanding of the active phases, sites and mechanisms for Fe-based FT to the level of cobalt and 
ruthenium50. 
Recently, molybdenum carbides have been explored for CO hydrogenation. These catalysts display a 
high resistance to carbon and sulfur poisoning51. Alkali earth metal promotors have been found to 
increase chain growth and activity,52 while selectivity to alcohols and olefins can be tuned by alkali 
promoters (e.g., potassium)53. Similarly, mixed K–Mo sulfides were proposed to increase olefin and 
alcohol selectivities54. Santos et al. proposed that alkoxy species play an important role in alcohol 

8 
 
formation and K promotion was suggested to stabilize alkoxy species and modify the electronic 
structure of MoS255. While initial results for these Mo-based catalyst systems are promising, a 
molecular view of the active sites and of the reaction mechanism remains to be developed.  
This review focuses on molecular level insights in the structure and the activity of FT catalysts gained 
over the past 15 years. The bulk of the review deals with Co-based catalysts due to the extensive 
progress from surface science and theoretical studies. Iron will be treated as well, although less 
extensively. We discuss mechanistic studies performed with density functional theory (DFT) 
calculations, backed up with experimental surface science studies. We begin the review by discussing 
the particle size effect on the activity of Co- and Ru-based FT catalysts. Several atomistic particle 
models have been constructed to describe the particle shape and structure under reaction conditions, 
but the nature of the active sites remains controversial. For Fe-based catalysts, a similar approach will 
be taken, where the different active phases will be discussed with an additional focus on the effect of 
promoters. The last section of the review briefly discusses recent progress on CO2 hydrogenation to 
long-chain hydrocarbons (CO2-FT). To conclude, the future of Fischer-Tropsch synthesis is discussed, 
including the advent of CO2-based processes and the emergence of new computational techniques, 
such as machine learning, that sharpen our molecular view of the FT reaction.  
 

9 
 
2 Molecular views of the active site and structure of Co- and Ru-based 
FT catalysts 
In this chapter, the structure of the catalyst under Fischer-Tropsch conditions will be discussed. First, 
we will discuss the effect of the particle size on the activity and selectivity for cobalt and ruthenium. 
By modeling nanoparticles, a better understanding of the active sites present under FT conditions can 
be developed. Wulff structures are a first method to model large nanoparticles, but recently atomistic 
models are appearing, which can describe nanoparticles more realistically. Finally, we will discuss how 
different intermediates can affect the surface structure (e.g., formation of nanoislands, CO overlayers). 
A molecular picture of the catalytic surface under reaction conditions is a first step to elucidate the 
observed experimental trends and the mechanism of FTS, which will be discussed in the following 
chapter. 
2.1 Effect of particle size on activity and selectivity 
New catalyst preparative techniques have allowed careful control over the metal particle size and 
dispersion. In combination with advanced supports, very small monodisperse nanoparticles can be 
created56 and highly loaded catalysts can be obtained57. These studies have shown a significant 
increase in turnover frequency (TOF) for cobalt and ruthenium particles from 1 to 10 nm. Above 10 
nm, the activity per surface atom remains constant. This unusual structure sensitivity has been 
supported and reproduced by many researchers58-63. 
Using inert carbon nanotubes as a support, Bezemer et al. reported a 10-fold increase in the TOF for 
Co-based catalysts when particle size increases from 3 to 10 nm58. Together with the increase in TOF, 
an increase in C5+ selectivity from 51 to 85 wt.% was observed. Concurrently, CH4 selectivity decreased 
for larger particles. This dramatic change in activity and selectivity was at the time unexpected since 
earlier work by Iglesia had shown that the activity and selectivity of Co-FT is independent of particle 
size for particles between 10 and 200 nm64. Since then, the size-dependence of the Co-FT TOF and 

10 
 
selectivity has been confirmed by several groups (Figure 2)56,58,59,61-63,65-74. Typically, TOFs on the order 
of 10-2 s-1 are observed for large particles under typical FT conditions. 
 
Figure 2. Turnover frequency (TOF) as a function of cobalt particle size as reported in 56,58,61,62,65,69,71-75. The TOF is scaled due 
to the varying process conditions. This figure is inspired by van de Loosdrecht et al. 48 and Pestman et al.48,76 
Steady-state isotopic transient kinetic analysis (SSITKA) is a unique experimental technique to quantify 
the concentration and reactivity of surface reaction intermediates. Using SSITKA, Den Breejen et al. 
attributed the reduced TOF for 6 nm cobalt particles to blocking of active edge/corner sites by 
irreversibly adsorbed CO* and to a lower intrinsic activity of the terraces59. The increase in CH4 
selectivity for smaller particles was linked to a higher H* coverage. Using SSITKA, Holmen et al. also 
attributed the lower TOF for small particles to site blocking, but proposed adsorbed C* and O* as site 
blocking species. They suggested that the site-based activity and the kinetically relevant steps did not 
change for particles from 4 to 15 nm, but that a decrease in the available active sites was responsible 
for the decrease in activity for smaller particles62. Kinetic studies of CO methanation on Co/SiO2 also 
showed a decrease in activity for particles below 10 nm, yet the apparent activation energy is 
independent of particle size, indicating that the rate-limiting steps do not change with particle size62,65. 
Hydrogen-deuterium isotopic exchange experiments however show a decrease in HD production with 
decreasing particle size. Herranz et al. therefore proposed that reduced H2 dissociation rates cause the 

11 
 
lower rates for smaller particles65. X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) studies by the same group 
showed that CO dissociation is facilitated by hydrogen through a COH* or CHxO* intermediate, 
indicating that H2 dissociation is key for FT activity66. Prieto et al. prepared a series of highly reducible 
10 wt.%Co/ITQ-2 model catalysts by combining a reverse micellar synthesis with a surface silylated 
ITQ-2 delaminated zeolite56. Again, an increase in TOF was measured when the size of cobalt particles 
increased from 5 to 11 nm. Above 11 nm, the TOF was independent of particle size. 
The effect of the particle size and of the support on the C5+ selectivity was studied by Holmen et al.67,68 
For Co/γ-Al2O3, a maximum in C5+ selectivity was found for 7-8 nm particles. The C5+ selectivity was 
further influenced by the choice of the support, and α-Al2O3 supports gave higher C5+ selectivity for all 
particle sizes67. In an attempt to probe the number of active sites under reaction conditions, Yang et 
al. proposed to use in situ CO chemisorption63. This method provides a more realistic measure of the 
number of active sites than ex situ techniques such as H2 chemisorption, X-ray diffraction (XRD) and 
Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM). The cobalt particle size index was introduced as the inverse 
of the total number of active sites obtained by H2 chemisorption or by in situ CO chemisorption and is 
proportional to the particle diameter. A correlation between the TOF and cobalt particle size index 
(i.e., particle size) was again found for a wide range of supports, resulting in a 3-fold increase in TOF 
for larger particles. 
The group of Somorjai reported a similar increase in the methane selectivity, especially at higher 
temperatures, for smaller cobalt particles supported on mesoporous silica (MCF-17)74,77. At 250°C, a 
maximal C5+ selectivity of more than 85% was obtained for nanoparticles of 11 nm, while the C5+ 
selectivity decreases strongly for smaller particles. This size dependence of the selectivity was less 
obvious at lower temperature. An increase in TOF with particle size was again observed, but was less 
pronounced at higher temperatures. 
In an attempt to explain the observed size dependence of the TOF, Ralston et al. performed Chemical 
Transient Kinetics (CTK) for cobalt particles of 4.3 nm and 9.5 nm75. Tracking the carbon coverage with 

12 
 
online MS and offline GC-MS, it was observed that carbon initially accumulates until a coverage of 0.5 
ML for the 4.3 nm particle and up to 2 ML for the larger 9.5 nm particles. The higher carbon coverage 
and increasing TOF for larger particles was suggested to be caused by the higher concentration of 
defect sites on larger particles. Pestman et al. also linked the higher concentration of defect sites, and 
in particular so-called B5 sites, on larger particles to the increase in TOF76. The proposed increase in the 
fraction of defect sites with particle size is somewhat counter-intuitive since the fraction edge and 
corner sites typically decreases with particle size. In the next section, we discuss atomistic particle 
models that try to address this issue. 
Recently, Cheng et al. controlled the size of Co-based FTS catalysts by embedding the cobalt oxide 
precursor inside a mesoporous silica support72. A narrow particle size distribution could be obtained 
through the spatial confinement, which also inhibits aggregation of smaller cobalt particles. Again, the 
TOF increased with particle size. In this work, the small particles (7.2 nm) showed a high selectivity 
towards diesel-range (C10-C20) products (66.2%), while the larger particles (11 nm) were more selective 
towards gasoline-range (C5-C10) products (62.4%). This seemingly larger chain-growth probability for 
smaller particles was attributed to a higher CHx* coverage on smaller particles and to spatial 
confinement72 and contrasts with the increase in chain-growth probability with particle size observed 
in other studies58,69. 
For Ru-based catalysts, a decrease of the TOF for smaller particles has been reported as well. Carballo 
et al. attributed the reduced activity to stronger CO adsorption and concomitant partial blocking of 
active sites for smaller particles78. This was supported by SSITKA studies, where an increase in the CO 
residence time and coverage was found for smaller particles. The higher CO* coverages are suggested 
to inhibit CO dissociation. Very small, unsupported ruthenium particles of 2 nm were prepared by Xiao 
et al. and tested for aqueous phase FT, exhibiting very high activity79. Quek et al. found that these small 
unsupported nanoparticles exhibit high oxygenate selectivity80. The lower step-edge concentration of 
small particles is postulated to decrease CO dissociation rates and hence decrease hydrocarbon 

13 
 
selectivity. A follow-up study suggested that the increase in TOF for larger particles is due to the 
stabilization of step-edge sites on the larger nanoparticles, which increases the rate of CO 
dissociation81. In another study, Kang et al. reported an increase in TOF with particle size for Ru 
supported on carbon nanotubes, up to 6 nm, followed by a slight decrease for larger particles82. An 
optimum size of 7 nm was found for the C5+ selectivity. 
At this point, the reduced TOF on cobalt and ruthenium particles below ~10 nm is generally accepted, 
while a nearly constant TOF for particles above 10 nm is observed. In addition, smaller cobalt particles 
show a higher methane selectivity. Some studies point to an optimum C5+ selectivity for particles of 8-
9 nm, while others find that the C5+ selectivity is constant for particles above 10 nm. Industrially, the 
optimal cobalt particles size is hence 8-10 nm to maximize the cobalt site time yield. Various 
propositions have been put forward to explain the particle size effect on the TOF, from site blocking to 
surface restructuring. In this context, it is important to note that the effective activation energy does 
not change with particle size. 
2.2 Modeling the metallic nanoparticles 
To identify the active sites for FT, a molecular scale picture of the catalyst particles and surfaces under 
reaction conditions is an important starting point. In 1969, Van Hardeveld and Hartog presented the 
first structural models to describe cuboctahedral nanoparticles83. Their model continues to influence 
structural models and suggestions for the nature of the active site. Similarly, surface energy 
calculations have been used to construct thermodynamic Wulff particle shapes84-87. While Wulff theory 
is only valid for large particles, it provides an indication of possible surface structures and sites84. In 
recent years, the development of machine learning potentials opened the door to simulate realistic 
cobalt particles, containing 1000s of atoms88-90. 
One way to predict the shape of a nanoparticle is the Wulff construction, which minimizes the surface 
energy at a fixed volume91. Surface free energies for different surface facets at different temperatures, 
pressures and coverage can then be used to predict equilibrium shapes. Typically, bulk cobalt adopts 

14 
 
a hexagonally close-packed (HCP) structure at ambient conditions. A temperature-induced phase 
transformation to a face-centered cubic (FCC) phase occurs at 400-450 °C 77,92. This transformation can 
also occur at much milder conditions when the particle size is reduced. Cobalt nanoparticles smaller 
than 20 nm are typically in the FCC phase, while particles larger than 40 nm will be in the HCP phase93. 
Therefore Liu et al. constructed Wulff equilibrium shapes for both HCP and FCC particles using DFT-
PBE computed surface energies (Figure 3)86. On the HCP particle, the close-packed (0001) surface only 
covers 18% of the surface, while the more open surfaces comprise the rest of the surface. The FCC 
nanoparticle on the other hand is dominated by the close-packed (111) surface, which comprises 70% 
of the surface. The more open facets constitute the rest of surfaces, with (100) covering 12% of the 
surface. The Wulff equilibrium shape of an FCC nanoparticle by Van Helden et al. also shows both (111) 
and (100) facets dominating the surface85. The (110) facet contributes less than 25% to the particle 
surface. These simulations were done for clean surfaces and did not account for surface coverages. 
 
Figure 3. Wulff equilibrium shape of (left) HCP Co and (right) FCC Co based on DFT calculations by Liu et al.86 
A combined operando TEM and computational catalysis study showed the effect of CO* coverage on 
the shape of cobalt and platinum nanoparticles87. By calculating the equilibrium CO* coverage at 
different conditions (via the chemical potential of CO, μCO), the Gibbs free surface energy as a function 
of the CO partial pressure and temperature was calculated and Wulff equilibrium shapes were 
constructed. While platinum nanoparticles respond dynamically to the changing CO partial pressure, 
FCC cobalt nanoparticles exhibit no structural shape evolution under a CO atmosphere. This 

15 
 
remarkably different behavior was attributed to two factors. First, the cobalt surface energy is more 
sensitive to the coordination number than the platinum surface energy, making high-index surfaces 
less stable on cobalt compared to platinum. Second, the stabilization by CO adsorption is stronger for 
the high-index facets of platinum than for cobalt87. The cobalt FCC Wulff particle is very similar to the 
earlier proposed particles by Liu et al. and Van Helden et al., with the (111) and (100) surface facets 
dominating. Similarly, the effect of CO* coverage on the shape of FCC and HCP ruthenium particles 
was studied by Zhao et al. by constructing Wulff equilibrium shapes based on DFT calculated surface 
energies94. The Gibbs free energy of CO adsorption for different coverages, pressures and 
temperatures was used to calculate the surface free energy under different conditions. For both HCP 
and FCC nanoparticles, more terrace-like facets (e.g., (100) and (111) for FCC and (0001) for HCP) are 
observed at higher CO* coverages. It should be noted that Wulff shapes are only valid for sufficiently 
large particles (> 10 nm) and do not account for the structure of the interfaces between different 
surface facets. Additionally, they cannot describe possible surface restructuring. 
Atomistic models, which are becoming more common, give a more detailed view of the structure of a 
nanoparticle (e.g., including atomic scale surface restructuring) and can differentiate better between 
different sizes of nanoparticles. Seminal work by Van Hardeveld and Hartog used a hard sphere packing 
approach to represent all surface atoms with a specific coordination number for ideal nanocrystal 
structures83. An active site is then defined as an ensemble of surface atoms with specific coordination 
numbers. Using this approach, a few simple ensembles consisting of n (‘Bn’) surface atoms were 
proposed: B2, B3, B4, B5-A, B5-B, and B6 (Figure 4a). B2 is an ensemble of 2 surface atoms, which 
corresponds to a bridge site. B3 and B4 sites lead to 3-fold and 4-fold sites, and some combinations of 
these two sites lead to the formation of B5-A (combination of B3 and B4 sites) and B5-B (combination of 
2 B3 sites) sites. B6 sites can be identified as corner sites, with 6 surface cobalt atoms making up the 
active site. By using ideal crystal models, the size-dependent surface structure of a particle was 
determined. This model is only valid for symmetrical, ideal nanoparticles, with an ideal surface packing. 
In this way, a particle maximizing the B5 concentration is constructed by the addition of an extra Co 

16 
 
layer on top of the terraces of ideal particles, which results in the formation of step-edges containing 
B5 sites. A maximum B5-A and B5-B concentration for particles of 3 nm was observed, followed by a 
decrease in the B5 site fraction for larger sizes. The predicted proportion of B4 sites, present on the 
(100) surface, converges to about 20%, which is similar to the results obtained for the Wulff particles. 
The decrease in the fraction of B5 sites can probably be attributed to the very specific, ideal subset of 
particles, defined to maintain very specific surface facet ratios85. 
a) 
 
 
b) 
 
c) 
 
Figure 4. a) An FCC Co nanoparticle (~4 nm), with the different possible Bn sites as proposed by Van Helden et al.85, exhibiting 
the reactive step-edge (B5) sites and the terrace sites (2B3). b) Surface concentration of the B5-A sites as a function of particle 
diameter. The thick blue curves are fitted regression functions. Thin blue lines indicate the 95% confidence interval of the fit. 
Reproduced with permission from ref.85, Copyright 2016 Elsevier. c) A cobalt nanoparticle (~6 nm), illustrating the possible 
sites. Reproduced with permission from ref.90, Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society under the CC BY-NC-ND 4.0 license 
(https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/). 

17 
 
In 2016, Van Helden et al. developed a similar approach based on a broken bond model, to establish 
the shape of the energetically most stable nanoparticles85. To represent the nanoparticle, a discretized 
space, represented by a 3D FCC lattice, was used. This results in fixed bond lengths and fixed angles. 
Similar site configurations as used by Van Hardeveld and Hartog were proposed. The unique kink site, 
resembling step sites exposed by Co(321), is defined as a B6 site. The different sites are illustrated in 
Figure 4. The energy of the nanoparticle was modeled using the coordination number of the individual 
atoms, with a correction of the bias towards low-coordinated atoms:

] ∙𝐸𝑐𝑜ℎ∙𝑁𝑖
