(1) 
with i the coordination number, CB the bulk coordination number (CB = 12 for FCC), Ecoh the cohesive 
energy, m the scaling factor and Ni the number of atoms with coordination number i. While the 
energies of this simplified model are not completely accurate, the model can still be used to calculate 
the relative stability of various particles. Using an ‘atom-by-atom’ growth model followed by a 
stochastic hill-climbing local optimization algorithm, a set of particles of the same size were found at 
local minima. Statistical averaging and site identification then showed the site composition of particles 
of 0.5 nm to 8.25 nm. In line with the results of Van Hardeveld and Hartog83, particles larger than 2 nm 
were similar to truncated octahedrons, containing an increasing fraction of defect sites. B4 sites start 
appearing for particles sizes of 1 nm and stabilize to a value of 18% at 8 nm. For an 8 nm particle size, 
approximately 15% of the surface consists of B5 and B6 sites, which start appearing for particle sizes of 
1 nm and converge at particle sizes of ~8 nm (Figure 4b). Both B5-A and B5-B are not explicitly found in 
the Wulff equilibrium shapes, but Co(311) and Co(110) surfaces, which are present on the Wulff 
equilibrated particles, exhibit a similar structure to, respectively, B5-A and B5-B sites. B6 sites are absent 
in Wulff shapes. 
In the last 5 years, several groups have proposed procedures to model nanoparticles88,90,94-100. Using 
artificial neural networks, Artrith et al. predicted thermodynamic equilibrium shapes of Cu-Au 
nanoparticles using artificial neural networks88. Rahm et al. developed an algorithm, based on 

18 
 
atomistic simulations in a constrained thermodynamic ensemble, to model changes in the equilibrium 
shapes of nanoparticles of 1-7 nm96. By combining DFT with Boltzmann statistics to describe ensembles 
of nanoparticles, Cheula et al. obtained different morphologies of rhodium nanoparticles under 
reaction conditions98. In this way, the importance of a statistical representation of catalyst particle 
morphologies in structure-sensitive reactions was highlighted. Agrawal et al. employed an embedded 
atom model for interatomic interactions to perform molecular dynamics simulations of FCC and HCP 
cobalt nanoparticles95. They concluded, similarly to the results of Van Helden et al.85, that the step/kink 
site fraction increases when particle size increases from 4 to 15 nm, with a significant increase of the 
B5 site fraction from 10% to more than 30%. 
In 2021, van Etten et al. developed a force field trained on DFT data of particles up to 55 atoms90. To 
model the nanoparticles (1-9 nm), simulated annealing molecular dynamics (MD) was used, without 
imposing any crystal structure a priori. Common neighbor analysis was used to identify the specific 
topological arrangements, or active sites (Figure 4c). Below 2.5 nm, low-coordinated atoms are very 
prominent. With increasing particle size (1-6 nm), the fraction of step-edges with the (110) and (211) 
orientation increases, before stabilizing for larger particles. A detailed analysis of the surface showed 
that this increase is related to nanoisland formation. The constant fraction of step-edge sites above 6 
nm is related to the larger distance between these nanoislands. 
Two main models were presented to model nanoparticles, i.e., Wulff structures and atomistic models. 
The former can predict the equilibrium shape of a particle with the prevalent surface facets and can 
account for realistic surface coverages, but it lacks the detail to describe specific topological surface 
atom arrangements. Atomistic models are becoming more common and provide a molecular view of 
the size-dependent catalysts surface structure. For the atomistic models, machine learning potentials 
seem to provide a new approach to simulate more realistic nanoparticles including surface coverages, 
with near-DFT accuracy. 

19 
 
2.3 Surface structures and coverages 
The dominant surface species during Co- and Ru-based FT is CO. The adsorption of CO on different 
cobalt surface facets has been studied in great detail and will be discussed in the next section. Under 
influence of the different adsorbates, the surface can restructure and form nanoislands on the cobalt 
terraces101. The formation of these nanoislands is important for the mechanism of FT reaction, since it 
is a possible source for the important B5 active sites. 
2.3.1 Overlayers of CO 
In this section, we will focus on the formation of CO overlayers on cobalt and ruthenium. The 
adsorption of CO has been extensively studied, both experimentally and theoretically, and various 
ordered CO overlayers have been proposed and characterized. Here, the discussion is limited to 
overlayers which are expected under FT conditions on metallic cobalt and ruthenium catalysts. 
Due to the profound effect of the CO* coverage on the activity and selectivity in FTS20,59,102,103, it is 
imperative to determine CO* coverages under FT conditions. Different structured overlayers of CO 
have been identified on cobalt and ruthenium catalysts. The ordered (√3x√3)R30°-CO overlayer104,105, 
with CO adsorbing on the top sites, and the (2√3x2√3)R30°-7CO structure106,107, with CO adsorbing on 
top and bridge sites, have both been observed on Co(0001) and Ru(0001). The former corresponds to 
a low coverage of 1/3 ML. Upon increasing the CO pressure, the 7/12 ML structure is formed, which 
has often been associated with the high-coverage limit during FTS. Only a limited number of ordered 
structures is observed instead of a gradual increase of CO* coverage. This suggests the existence of 
local free energy minima for certain ordered structures108. 
Heats of adsorption on cobalt particles from 5 to 17 nm range from 121 to 141 kJ/mol for coverages 
below 0.05 ML109. For CO adsorption on Co(0001), an isosteric heat of 128 kJ/mol has been reported 
for coverages up to 1/3 ML, which reduces to 96 kJ/mol for higher coverages106. Low energy electron 
diffraction (LEED) studies identified a (√3x√3)R30°-CO ordered structure, with CO* adsorbed on top, 
for an exposure of 1.2 L at 100 K and a (2√3x2√3)R30°-7CO overlayer, with one CO on top and 6 bridge 

20 
 
COs, at 100 K for a higher exposure of 2.2 L. The low coverage (√3x√3)R30°-CO structure was also 
observed at higher temperatures in LEED studies104,110,111 and the (2√3x2√3)R30°-7CO was observed at 
an exposure of 3.5 L at 180 K110. Polarization modulation reflection absorption infrared spectroscopy 
(PM-RAIRS) and high-resolution electron energy loss spectroscopy (HREELS) could identify CO* 
adsorbed on top, possibly associated with the (√3x√3)R30°-CO ordered structure112-114. Similarly, PM-
RAIRS could identify the bridge and top COs associated with the (2√3x2√3)R30°-7CO overlayer113. 
Interestingly, in a narrow pressure and temperature range, the (√7x√7)R19.1°-3CO structure has been 
observed, as a transition between the low and high coverage phase106,110,115,116. The (√7x√7)R19.1°-3CO 
ordered structure was reported to have 1 CO on a top site and 2 COs on hollow sites, resulting in all 
CO molecules at equidistant positions (Figure 5)106,108,113. Using X-ray photoemission spectroscopy 
(XPS) and IR data, Weststrate et al. suggested that 2 COs adsorb at top sites and 1 CO at a hollow site 
for this structure117. 
 
Figure 5. (left) The (√3x√3)R30° 1/3 ML CO overlayer, (middle) The (√7x√7)R19.1° 3/7 ML CO overlayer and (right) the 
(2√3x2√3)R30° 7/12 ML CO overlayer. Adapted with permission from ref.108, Copyright 2015 Elsevier. 
XPS and IR studies by Weststrate et al. confirmed that at coverages up to 1/3 ML, CO adsorbs only at 
top sites. CO adsorption is proposed to shift to hollow and bridge sites at higher coverages, due to 
lateral interactions and geometric constraints117, while Gunasooriya et al. suggest that this shift of site 
preference is driven by the increased cobalt surface charge at higher CO* coverages118. Weststrate et 
al. propose a saturation coverage of 2/3 ML at 90 K and ~10-8 mbar117. The same saturation coverage 
has been reported on Ru(0001), but here CO only adsorbs at top sites119. Advancements of 
experimental techniques have pushed the pressure-range from ultra-high vacuum (UHV) to 

21 
 
atmospheric pressures, making it possible to go beyond the typical low temperature conditions 
necessary in UHV conditions and discover high coverage structures beyond 2/3 ML119. A 0.83 ML 
coverage structure was obtained on Co(0001) (90 K and ~10-3 mbar), with CO* adsorbed at top and 
hollow sites117. The (2√3x2√3)R30° structure often associated with the 7/12 ML coverage, was found 
in electron diffraction studies starting from a coverage of ~0.5 ML up to 0.67 ML. This unit cell has been 
observed even for coverages up to 0.83 ML, suggesting that this unit cell is shared between structures 
with coverages from 0.5 ML to 0.83 ML. Surprisingly, the CO desorption temperature found in 
temperature programmed desorption (TPD) is practically the same (400 K) for different surfaces (Co 
foil, (sputtered) Co(0001)117, Co(100)120, Co(101̅0)121), despite the different CO adsorption energies that 
have been reported. The pre-factors might compensate for the difference in adsorption energies and 
this has been suggested to explain the surface-structure-independent TPD spectra. 
Using work function measurements up to 0.001 mbar, Weststrate et al. used extrapolation to LTFT 
conditions to determine CO* coverages117. Assuming Co(0001) to be representative of the surface of a 
nanoparticle and other intermediates not to affect CO* coverages, Weststrate et al. predicted CO* 
coverages between 0.45-0.5 ML which are relatively pressure-independent. These values are similar 
to the 0.43 ML obtained by den Breejen et al. and similar to theoretical predictions by Saeys and 
coworkers59,108. Further LEED, XPS and IR studies show that defective surfaces and co-adsorbates can 
significantly affect CO adsorption, e.g., CO* adsorbed on top sites exhibits an increase in stretching 
frequency upon co-adsorption of atomic species (C, H, N or O)117. In a follow-up study, new high-
coverage structures were proposed above the 7/12 ML coverage typically found under Fischer-Tropsch 
conditions (Figure 6)122. At higher coverages, the (2√3x2√3)R30° structure breaks up and boundary 
regions with higher coverage form in between, which is possible due to the formation of double bridge 
dicarbonyls. Around 0.63 ML, a phase-transition is observed by LEED, resulting in a mixture of (2x2)-
3CO structures with lower coverage boundary regions to relieve stress. These high-coverage structures 
will probably not be observed under FT conditions due to the low CO desorption barriers. Interestingly, 
scanning tunneling microscopy (STM) studies at room temperature under CO atmosphere also show a 

22 
 
phase transition at 0.63 ML, but here a Moiré-type structure is observed, suggesting a very small 
energy difference between these two structures123. In the STM study, the CO* coverage was 
investigated over a broad range of CO pressures (10-9-103 mbar) and a CO* coverage of 0.5 ML was 
proposed instead of 0.58 ML for the (2√3 × 2√3)R30° structure123. A disordered overlayer was found 
for intermediate pressures, attributed to the small stability region of the intermediate (√7×√7)R19.1° 
structure. Using extrapolation to FT conditions, CO* coverages of 0.43-0.5 ML were again predicted. 
 
Figure 6. High-coverage CO overlayers proposed by Weststrate et al.122 Adapted with permission from ref.122, Copyright 2022 
Elsevier. 
Some first principles studies assessed CO adsorption on cobalt and ruthenium102,103,108,124-126. Similar to 
the ‘pressure gap’ in surface science experiments, quantum chemical methods have shortcomings. 
Calculation of the correct site preference and accurate CO adsorption energies remain an important 
challenge for DFT127-130. The adsorption energy of CO on transition metals often suffers from 
overbinding due to an incorrect description of the metal back-donation. Moreover, many DFT 
functionals favor hollow over top sites. 
Gunasooriya et al. suggested that CO adsorption does not gradually weaken with coverage, i.e., follow 
the Langmuir adsorption isotherm, but rather forms thermodynamically stable overlayers at 

23 
 
intermediate coverages108. To accurately calculate the adsorption site of CO, the VdW-DF functional 
was used131-133. With this functional, CO adsorbs preferably at the top site, in agreement with 
experiments104, with an adsorption energy of 130 kJ/mol, which is very close to the experimental value 
of 128 kJ/mol106. The VdW-DF HOMO-LUMO gap is also larger than the PBE HOMO-LUMO gap, 
explaining the shift in site preference and adsorption energies108. 
By calculating the Gibbs free energies of CO adsorption per Co(0001) surface area (ΔGad) for varying 
CO pressures at 300 K and 500 K for different coverages, Figure 7a could be constructed108. Three stable 
phases with minimal ΔGad for a range of μCO are found: a low coverage (√3 × √3)R30°-CO phase, an 
intermediate coverage (√7/3×√7/3)R10.9°-3CO phase and a high coverage (2√3×2√3)R30°-7CO phase. 
Two regions are pointed out to show i) the conditions where the 1/3 ML structure is more stable than 
the clean surface and ii) the small stability region of the 3/7 ML structure at the transition from low 
(1/3 ML) to high (7/12 ML) coverage. The 7/12 ML coverage of the (2√3x2√3)R30°-7CO structure was 
suggested to be the saturation coverage. All other structures have a significantly higher ΔGad at all 
pressures and temperatures of interest. The obtained phase diagram (Figure 7b) corresponded well 
with experimental results. Using VdW-DF2, Ma et al. similarly predicted a site preference for top sites 
on Co(0001) with an adsorption energy of 121 kJ/mol at 1/3 ML134. Using multiplicative and additive 
corrections, vibrational spectra on Co(0001) in close agreement to experiment were obtained, linking 
structures as predicted by DFT to experimentally observed spectra. 

24 
 
a) 
 
b) 
 
Figure 7. a) Gibbs free energy of CO adsorption per Co(0001) surface area (kJ/mol Co surface atoms) as a function of CO 
pressure at 300 and 500 K for different coverages. b) First principles phase diagram for CO adsorption on Co(0001). The phase 
diagram is dominated by the low coverage (√3×√3)R30°–CO structure and the high coverage (2√3×2√3)R30°-7CO structure. 
Both structures have been observed experimentally and conditions where each structure was observed are indicated. (♦) 
(√3 × √3)R30°-CO structure at 7 × 10− 9 mbar and 300 K (Bridge et al.104). (∆) (√3 ×√3)R30°-CO structure below 1 mbar and at 
300 K (Beitel et al.113). (▲) (√3 × √3)R30°–CO structure at 100 mbar and 490 K (Beitel et al.114). (●) (2√3 × 2√3)R30°-7CO 
structure at 100 mbar and 300 K (Beitel et al.114). ( ) Typical FT synthesis conditions: 6 bar CO and 500 K. Reproduced with 
permission from ref.108, Copyright 2015 Elsevier. 
2.3.2 Surface reconstructions 
The strong adsorption of CO, C and O has been observed to reconstruct surfaces101,135-139. In the 90s, 
Wilson and de Groot performed ex situ STM studies of Co(0001) surfaces which were subjected to FT 
conditions (4 bar and 520 K)101. After FT reaction, the Co(0001) surface was transferred back to the 
STM chamber and imaged by low temperature UHV STM. They observed a massive reaction-induced 
surface reconstruction, forming triangular ~2 nm nanoislands of mono-atomic height, suggesting an 
etch-regrowth mechanism (Figure 8). The formation of these nanoislands creates defect or step sites, 
which are often linked to catalytic FTS activity140-143. Following this seminal work, it took more than 10 
years before new studies were published. EXAFS analysis by Bezemer et al. in 2006 showed a decrease 
in the mean cobalt coordination number after exposure to FT conditions which was attributed to 
surface restructuring58. Ehrensperger and Wintterlin more recently performed in situ STM studies, but 

25 
 
did not observe this surface reconstruction115. Their conditions (10 mbar of CO, 493 K and a 40:1 H2/CO 
ratio) were significantly different from the conditions used by Wilson and de Groot and by Bezemer et 
al., illustrating the importance of considering surfaces under realistic catalytic conditions. 
 
Figure 8. STM image of a) the clean Co(0001) surface showing terraces of 150 nm width and b) the reconstructed surface 
after 1 h under high-pressure CO hydrogenation conditions. The smallest observed steps in (b) are monoatomic in height 
(~0.205 nm). Reproduced with permission from ref.101, Copyright 1995 American Chemical Society. 
To explain the formation of the nanoislands, Wilson and de Groot proposed the strong adsorption of 
dicarbonyl species at defect sites as the driving force101. Other authors also proposed cobalt sub-
carbonyls (Co(CO)x) to be responsible for the surface reconstruction113,114,144. Prieto et al. on the other 
hand suggested the strong adsorption of carbon on the step-edge sites to be a driving force in the 
surface reconstruction56. Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) studies of CO adsorption were 
used to study the nature of the adsorption sites during FT conditions. An increase in low frequency 
bands of linearly adsorbed CO*, attributed to defect sites, was observed under FT conditions (493 K 
and 1 atm). The increase in defect sites suggested that a surface reconstruction occurred, which was 
proposed to be induced by carbon adatoms, originating from CO dissociation. The trend in the FTIR 
spectra was observed irrespective of particle size, suggesting surface reconstruction for all studied 
particle sizes. 
The role of carbon in surface reconstructions is in line with other studies suggesting that carbon can 
induce surface-reconstruction on Co(100), Co(111) and Co(0001) surfaces, where square planar sites 

26 
 
are formed (so-called clock-reconstructions)136,145-147. STM studies of the Co(0001) surface at pressures 
up to 0.25 mbar and temperatures up to 493 K also suggested a carbide-induced surface 
reconstruction116. Due to the surface reconstruction and expansion, excess Co atoms of the Co(0001) 
surface are ejected and added to the steps. Molecular dynamics simulations by Zhang et al. showed 
that the formation of a surface carbide induces strain in the cobalt lattice, which resulted in the 
‘popping-up’ of Co atoms to form islands and step-edges137. The nanoisland formation was only 
observed for sufficiently large terraces, suggesting a critical size at which this popping-up phenomenon 
occurs and resulting in more step-edges and active sites for larger particles, providing an alternative 
view on the origin of the particle size effect. 
Surface science studies suggest that both cobalt sub-carbonyls and carbon atoms are important actors 
for the surface reconstruction. Weststrate et al. suggest that CO is required to increase the mobility of 
Co atoms and carbon will pin down the Co atoms148. The fixed Co adatom then form a nucleus for the 
formation of the nanoislands. Banerjee et al. investigated the combined stabilization effect of CO and 
C adsorption for the formation of nanoislands using DFT149. The energy penalty to form step-edge sites 
can indeed be overcome by the strong adsorption of square-planar carbon at the newly created B5 
edge sites and by the high-coverage CO adsorption at the step-edges145. An equilibrium coverage of 1 
ML CO and 0.5 ML C on the step-edge was proposed. 
 
Figure 9. CO-covered cobalt nanoisland formed on cobalt terraces under Fischer-Tropsch conditions as proposed by Banerjee 
et al.142 The most stable nanoislands are ~2 nm and contain ~45 Co atoms. Each island is terminated by B5 edges which are 
covered with 1 ML CO and 0.5 ML C, leaving B5 site vacancies between C-covered B5 sites (black boxes) in panel b. a) top view 

27 
 
and b) side view, with the white box indicating the model used in this study142. Reproduced with permission from ref.142, 
Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society. 
In a follow-up study, the delicate balance between the energy gain of forming the C/CO covered edges 
and the energy penalty for forming corners was used to explain the specific size of the triangular 
nanoislands150. By considering i) the energy cost associated with nanoisland formation, CO desorption 
from the terrace covered by the nanoisland and formation of the three corner sites and ii) the energy 
gain from CO adsorption on the nanoisland terrace and C/CO adsorption at the step-edges, the Gibbs 
free energy for the formation of differently sized nanoislands could be calculated. Nanoislands of 45 
Co atoms (~2 nm) were found to be the most stable under Fischer-Tropsch conditions (Figure 9). In situ 
STM studies under reaction conditions (3 bar, H2/CO = 2 and 483 K) observed the formation of these 
nanoisland features, which are very mobile and short-living. Using C and CO chemical potentials, the 
phase diagram depicted in Figure 10 was constructed, which showed that under the conditions of 
Wintterlin et al. (10 mbar CO), island formation is not thermodynamically favorable116. 
The previous studies were based on thermodynamic considerations, but the formation of nanoislands 
should also be kinetically feasible. Molecular dynamics simulations of clean cobalt particles and using 
an embedded atom model did not show surface reconstructions at 500 K 95, highlighting the role of 
adsorbate species in surface reconstructions137,149. 

28 
 
 
Figure 10. Phase diagram of the cobalt surface structure (i.e., size of the most stable islands) as a function of the carbon 
chemical potential (μC) and the CO chemical potential (μCO). μC is calculated for the reaction CO + H2 + * ↔ C* + H2O, and 
depends on the CO, H2O and H2 partial pressures and the temperature. The origin (0,0) corresponds to standard FT conditions 
(20 bar, 500 K, H2/CO = 2, 60% conversion). At these conditions, Co45-B5 nanoislands are the most stable structures. The 
dotted line indicates the conditions where large islands become more stable than CO-covered terraces. Increasing chemical 
potentials stabilize ever smaller nano-islands with more B5 edge sites, up to Co45-B5, the smallest stable islands. For low carbon 
chemical potentials, nano-islands can also be stabilized by the high-coverage of CO at the edge sites, forming CO-stabilized 
nanoislands. a) CO-covered terraces with 1/3 ML coverage, b) reactive STM image of nanoisland observed by Banerjee et al. 
150, c) Co45-B5 nanoisland and d) Co45-F4 nanoisland. Reproduced with permission from ref.150, Copyright 2016 American 
Chemical Society. 
Recently, the group of Wintterlin performed operando STM studies at syngas pressures (H2/CO=2) up 
to 950 mbar151. Again, no significant changes in the surface structure were observed. The surface was 
however not static, and some morphology changes were observed. The step-edge positions fluctuated, 
indicating fast mobility of the Co atoms which detach from/attach to the steps. The absence of surface 
reconstruction might be related to the still relatively low pressure used, since the phase diagram of 
Banerjee et al. only predicts island formation above 1 bar150. Sputtering was then used to significantly 
alter the morphology and create the active sites for the CO dissociation. Higher activities were reported 
after sputtering of the surfaces. Surprisingly, after reaction, the sputtered surface was again smoother. 

29 
 
On the Co(1 0 1̅ 15) surface, which is similar to Co(0001) but contains additional step-edge sites, no 
surface restructuring was found under similar conditions152. 
New developments in characterization techniques make it possible to study catalysts at the nanometer 
scale. Moreover, operando and in situ techniques are becoming more common. In recent years, a 
similar revolution in computational catalysis, towards a more realistic molecular view of the active 
catalyst surface, is taking place. The combination of operando computational catalysis and new 
experimental techniques makes it possible to identify important surface structures, local equilibrium 
coverages and active sites. Identification of the active sites under reaction conditions is a key first step 
towards mechanistic studies of the FT reaction and brings us one step closer to understanding catalytic 
turnovers at a molecular level. 
2.3.3 Carbon deposits 
Carbon deposits during Fischer-Tropsch synthesis are inevitable. Navarro et al. used in situ STM to 
study the Co(0001) surface. They concluded that hydrocarbon chains of 14 or 15 atoms likely reside on 
the so-called terraces of the cobalt catalyst153. Other forms of carbon deposits include subsurface 
carbon, carbides and graphene146,154. 
In situ magnetometer studies suggested the formation of small amounts for cobalt carbides and 
assume the effect on catalyst deactivation is negligible155. The formation of carbides seems kinetically 
to be strongly inhibited and mainly occurs at low H2/CO ratios and at low temperatures. Cobalt carbides 
(e.g., Co2C) have been assumed to be inert156, although recent reports observed alcohol formation in 
the presence of carbides157-167. Specifically the interface between Co2C and metallic Co has been 
proposed as the active site for alcohol formation. Calculations suggest that CO adsorbs molecularly on 
Co2C, while CO insertion barriers are relatively low at the Co/Co2C interface, resulting in the production 
of alcohols157. Cobalt carbides also showed a higher selectivity to lower olefin formation166,168-174. 
Zhong et al. reported that cobalt carbide nanoprisms selectively produce C2-4 olefins, while suppressing 
methane formation168. TEM and DFT calculations suggested that the (101) and (020) facets are 

30 
 
responsible for the olefin selectivity. In a follow-up study, they showed that sodium promotion of a 
CoMn catalyst stabilizes these cobalt carbide nanoprisms169. Electron donation by Na is proposed to 
strengthen CO adsorption and facilitate CO dissociation, while stabilizing Co2C with (101) and (020) 
facets.171. This could explain the higher activity and lower methane selectivity of nanoprisms, that 
mainly contain the Co2C(020) facet, as opposed to the nanospheres exhibiting the Co2C(111) facets.  
Theoretical calculations suggest that carbides are formed more easily on the terrace sites near the 
edge site, although they are less stable than graphene147. Raybaud and Valero modeled a carbide 
reconstructed surface with surface and subsurface carbon atoms135. Similarly, the group of Kruse 
proposes subsurface carbons to appear prior to the bulk phase transformation of cobalt to cobalt 
carbides175. 
Aromatic carbon in the form of graphene is commonly found on FT catalysts. Using ethylene as a 
carbon source, Weststrate et al. investigated the fate of carbon species on Co(0001) with XPS and 
STM146. Ethylene decomposition led to the formation of atomic carbon, cobalt carbides and graphene. 
On a clean Co(0001) surface, a high coverage of partially hydrogenated species (i.e., acetylene) is 
suggested to nucleate graphene via the cyclotrimerization of acetylene, the most stable C2 species in 
the absence of CO*. When atomic carbon is present on the surface, graphene is proposed to nucleate 
more easily at the step-edges and form through the addition of acetylene, CH*, and atomic carbon. 
Weststrate et al. further concluded that atomic carbon reduces adsorption strength and saturation 
coverage of CO and H. Graphene instead more effectively blocks adsorption sites, poisoning the 
catalyst. During FT, only low coverages of acetylene will be present, and consequently graphene 
formation is suggested to be only a minor side reaction. 
Using the Boudouard reaction (CO + CO → C + CO2), Chen et al. deposited graphitic carbon on a 
supported cobalt catalyst154. During catalytic FT testing, it was found that while both CO conversion 
and CH4 formation decreased after carbon deposition, the formation rate of higher carbons remained 
nearly unaffected. Chen et al. suggested that graphitic carbon was deposited on the terraces, which 

31 
 
blocks methanation sites, and that B5 step sites were not blocked. Earlier studies however show that 
graphitic carbon nucleates and grows at extended step-edge (B5) sites147, suggesting an alternative 
explanation for the observed effect. 
To avoid the formation of carbon deposits, Saeys and coworkers investigated the introduction of boron 
promoters176,177. DFT calculations suggested that boron selective titrates step-edge sites, and hence 
slows down graphite nucleation. Experiments with a 20 wt.% Co/γ-Al2O3 catalyst showed that 
promotion with 0.5 wt.% boron reduced deactivation rates six-fold177. A somewhat similar effect has 
been reported for bismuth178. DFT calculations again suggest that bismuth binds preferentially at step-
edge sites. 
 
 

32 
 
3 Molecular views on the mechanisms of Co- and Ru-based CO-FT 
Computational and experimental studies have helped to develop a molecular view of the cobalt surface 
under FT conditions and different active sites have been proposed. The active sites can be classified 
into two groups: i) defect/step-edge (B5) sites on more open surfaces and ii) terrace-like sites (2B3) on 
close-packed surfaces. Alternative sites have been proposed, but the discussion in this chapter is 
limited to these two families. Atomistic models propose that the site composition of nanoparticles 
depends on the particle size, possibly affecting the activity and selectivity85,90. Similarly, the metallic 
surface of cobalt (or ruthenium) is covered by several intermediates, such as CO, C, hydrocarbons and 
hydrogen, which can form either structured overlayers (e.g., CO) and even induce surface 
restructuring. This molecular picture of the active sites on cobalt (and ruthenium) FT catalysts will be 
useful to study the mechanistic pathways. 
Fischer-Tropsch synthesis can best be compared to a polymerization reaction consisting of an initiation 
step, chain-growth steps and termination steps. Initiation is most often associated with CO activation 
to form a CHx* species, which is a chain initiator in the CO-insertion mechanism and a monomer in the 
carbide mechanism. Termination occurs through a series of (de)hydrogenation steps, forming olefins 
and paraffins. Via CO-insertion, oxygenates can be formed. 
A myriad of complex and less complex reaction mechanisms has been proposed over the past 100 
years, but the detailed mechanism of Fischer-Tropsch synthesis remains elusive. Table 1 summarizes 
some of the frequently encountered mechanistic questions in Fischer-Tropsch synthesis. Many of 
these questions remain largely unanswered, while many of the proposed answers spark intense 
discussions in the field. First, kinetic studies will be presented, followed by theoretical studies to 
elucidate the mechanism of CO activation and chain-growth. Finally, surface science studies focusing 
on these mechanistic questions will be discussed. 

33 
 
Table 1. Some mechanistic questions often encountered in Fischer-Tropsch studies 
1. How is CO activated (direct, H-assisted, OHx-assisted, C-assisted)? What is the active site for 
CO dissociation? 
2. What is the mechanism (and the monomer) for chain-growth? Do different mechanisms co-
exist under certain conditions? 
3. Is chain-growth fast and/or reversible? Are there many growing chains on a cobalt particle? 
4. What is the active site for chain-growth? 
5. What is the resting state of the growing chains (e.g., R-C*, R-CH*, R-CH2*)? 
6. How does the activity of the active sites compare? 
7. What are the chain termination mechanisms? 
8. Do spectator surface species (e.g., CO*) influence the reaction mechanism? 
9. Does intersite diffusion occur and how fast is it on a (crowded) surface? 
10. What is the role of water in the FTS mechanism? 
11. What is the mechanism of oxygen removal and is it rate-limiting? 
 
3.1 Experimental kinetic studies 
Two main types of kinetic models have been developed for FT: power law models and Langmuir-
Hinshelwood-Hougen-Watson (LHHW) models. While the former are based purely on empirical 
observations, LHHW models are based on mechanistic models with a rate-determining step. In 1989, 
Sarup and Wojciechowski derived a number of LHHW rate equations, which they fitted to experimental 
data179. A few years later, Yates and Satterfield compared the different proposed LHHW models using 
their experimental data as well as rate data from previous investigations180. From their analysis, they 
obtained an apparent activation energy of 93-103 kJ/mol and proposed following rate equation: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
(1 + 𝛽𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 
(2) 
The group of Bartholomew also investigated different rate equations and proposed following power 
law model and LHHW model181: 
 
𝑟 ≈ 𝑘𝑝𝐶𝑂
−0.24𝑝𝐻2
0.74;  𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
0.74
(1 + 𝛽𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 
(3) 
Kinetic studies directly measure important kinetic parameters: the apparent activation energy (Ea) and 
the reaction orders of CO and H2. All rate equations show a positive reaction order in hydrogen and 

34 
 
mostly negative reaction orders in CO. In literature, reaction orders between 0.5 and 1 are measured 
for H2 and between -1 and 1 for CO180,182,183. Apparent activation barriers vary between 90 and 110 
kJ/mol180,182,184. The variation in the parameters is attributed to the process conditions. 
Van Steen and Schulz constructed a LHHW model based on mechanistic arguments185. The simplified 
mechanism starts with CO activation to form a chain initiator/monomer (CHx*) followed by 
propagation. Using irreversible CHx* hydrogenation and chain growth and a steady-state approach, 
the rate of CO consumption is found to be equal to the rate of surface carbon hydrogenation. By 
assuming equilibrium between the surface intermediates, a rate equation was obtained that estimates 
FT reaction rates for Fe- and Co-based catalysts: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼(𝑝𝐻2
1.5𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2𝑂
⁄
)
(1 + 𝛽(𝑝𝐻2𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2𝑂
⁄
))
2 
(4) 
They were one of the first to take the FT mechanism into account to derive a kinetic equation. 
Experiments on Co-based catalysts show that increasing H2O partial pressures increases reaction rates, 
while the proposed rate equation suggests mostly an inhibition of the rate by H2O185. 
A comparison between different rate equations for alumina-supported cobalt catalysts led Botes et al. 
to suggest following semi-empirical model186: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂
0.75𝑝𝐻2
0.75 
(1 + 𝛽𝑝𝐶𝑂
0.5)
2 
(5) 
This equation showed a lot of similarities with a mechanistically derived rate equation, which assumes 
that direct CO dissociation is fast, while the C* hydrogenation to CH* and CH2* is slow. 
Ojeda et al. also investigated the kinetics of the FT reaction for Fe-based catalysts187. Their rate 
equation was based on a mechanism of elementary steps with the formation of the CH2* monomer as 
the rate-determining step, yielding the same rate equation as Yates and Satterfield: 

35 
 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 
(6) 
In the proposed scheme, direct CO dissociation has a low barrier, which might not be the case187. Based 
on this scheme, a rate equation for CO2 formation was also constructed, which suggested significant 
effects of the H2 pressure. Experimental rate data on Fe-based catalysts on the other hand showed a 
reaction order of zero in the formation of CO2, suggesting CO2 formation follows an alternative 
mechanism. Therefore, Ojeda et al. proposed an alternative mechanism, including the H-assisted CO 
activation (via HCOH*), resulting in following equation: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 + 
𝛽𝑝𝐶𝑂
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 
(7) 
The two terms are proposed to represent respectively the H-assisted and the direct CO dissociation 
pathways, but are also proposed to reflect the two possible O* rejection routes, i.e., H2O formation 
and CO2 formation. Based on a theoretical analysis, extending these equations for Fe-based catalysts 
to Co-based catalysts, they suggested that the H-assisted pathway (via HCOH*) is dominant on cobalt 
catalysts, where O* is predominantly removed as H2O. 
The influence of water on Co-and Ru-based catalysts has been originally reported to be negligible, 
while more recently, an increase in the rate with H2O partial pressure has been reported180,183,185,188. 
To account for the presence of H2O, the rate equation was adjusted188: 
 
𝑟≈ 
𝛼𝑃𝐻2𝑃𝐶𝑂+  𝛽𝑃𝐶𝑂𝑃𝐻2𝑃𝐻2𝑂
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑃𝐶𝑂+ 𝐾𝐻2𝑂𝑃𝐻2𝑂+ 𝐾𝑂𝐻𝑃𝐻2𝑂𝑃𝐻2
−0.5 + 𝐾𝑂𝑃𝐻2𝑂𝑃𝐻2
−1)
2 
(8) 
Hibbitts et al. fitted experimental data and showed a rate-enhancement of 7 ± 3 at 1 bar H2O and 463 
K. 
Using the LHHW approach, Todic et al. developed kinetic models based on both the carbide and CO-
insertion mechanism189,190. They used separate rate coefficients for C1 and C2 desorption and assumed 
a chain-length dependent α-olefin desorption. The latter assumes that hydrocarbon chains have weak 

36 
 
interactions with the catalyst surface. Using a Re-promoted cobalt catalyst under industrially relevant 
operating conditions (478-503 K, 1.5-2.5 MPa, H2/CO = 1.4 - 2.1) in a 1 L slurry reactor, the kinetic 
parameters were estimated for the model based on the carbide mechanism189. The adjustments were 
shown to be key to predict the increase in chain-growth probability and decrease in olefin/paraffin 
ratio at higher carbon number. Using the same approach, but altering the kinetic rate equations to fit 
a CO-insertion model, an improved fit was obtained. 
Another way to elucidate the reaction mechanisms governing Fischer-Tropsch synthesis is by 
performing isotope kinetic studies, for example by Kinetic Isotope Effect (KIE) studies or Steady-State 
Isotopic Transient Kinetics Analysis (SSITKA). Early SSITKA studies by van Dijk et al. suggested the 
existence of two carbon pools, both participating in the formation of methane and of long-chain 
hydrocarbons191. The nature of these two carbon pools was not identified. These early SSITKA studies 
also observed only a low concentration of growing chains during FT. 
The group of Hensen employed kinetic studies to investigate CO hydrogenation to methane192 and to 
long-chain hydrocarbons19. Using SSITKA, residence times and surface coverages of intermediates can 
be measured, leading to mechanistic proposals. An isotopic 12CO/H2 → 13CO/H2 SSITKA switch 
suggested that CHx hydrogenation is rate-limiting for methanation at 260 °C and below 1 bar192. 
Backward Chemical Transient Kinetic Analysis (CTK) suggested that the CH4 formation rate depends on 
the hydrogen coverage, which in turn depends strongly on the CO* coverage (and partial pressure). 
Forward CTK measurements suggest that O* hydrogenation to H2O is also a relatively slow step 
compared to CO dissociation. A degree-of-rate-control analysis of the microkinetic model fitted to the 
transient studies shows that C* hydrogenation is the main rate controlling step under methanation 
conditions, followed by O* hydrogenation and CO dissociation. The rate-controlling character of the 
C* and O* hydrogenation steps are proposed to lead to the positive reaction order in H2. The negative 
order in CO is then suggested to be due to the competition between H* and CO*. Using 15 nm silica-
supported cobalt catalysts at 220 °C and below 2 bar, chain-growth was studied for short hydrocarbons 

37 
 
(<C5)19. SSITKA measures higher CO* coverages (0.46 ML) than at methanation conditions (0.3 ML), 
consistent with earlier studies59,108,117. Co-feeding 13CO and 12C3H6 suggests not only that CHx* is a chain-
growth monomer but also that chain-growth is reversible, and a O-free C1 species is the main chain 
growth monomer19. This chain-growth reversibility (or chain decoupling) has been discussed earlier in 
DFT studies on metallic cobalt and ruthenium193 and in microkinetic simulations194. The transient 
responses of the FT products in a SSITKA experiment suggested that the carbide mechanism with CHx* 
as a monomer is dominant and that chain-growth is reversible19. Experiments on a C-covered (and CO-
free) surface, obtained by CO dissociation and subsequent evacuation to remove CO*, show C2 and C3 
formation upon exposure to H2, demonstrating that the carbide mechanism can occur. Upon co-
feeding 13CO and 12C3H6, 12CH4 formation was observed, suggesting chain decoupling occurs. 
Simultaneous presence of 12C and 13C in C2 species suggested that 12C can participate in chain-growth, 
while the presence of 12C in C3+ chains suggested CHx* as the monomer. Microkinetic modeling, based 
on the SSITKA and kinetic data, suggests that chain decoupling is promoted when the fraction of free 
sites is increased, resulting in a lower chain growth probability. The CO consumption on the other hand 
increases when more empty sites are present. Under FT conditions, high CO* coverages hence 
promote chain-growth and inhibit chain decoupling. 
To understand the particle size effect for cobalt, Yang et al. employed SSITKA. They showed that the 
intrinsic activity does not change with particle size and the lower TOF for smaller particles was 
attributed to surface poisoning by strongly bound C* and O* species. Further studies by the group of 
Holmen combined DFT, transient and steady-state kinetic modeling to investigate CO activation and 
methane formation195. Based on steady-state kinetic modeling, a negative reaction order for CO was 
measured, indicating strong CO adsorption and inhibition. Hydrogen on the other hand has a positive 
reaction order, which, in line with the results by Chen et al.192, suggests the rate-determining steps 
involve hydrogenation (e.g., C* + H*, O* + H*, CO* + H*). By constructing LHHW-type kinetic models 
combined with transient SSITKA and KIE studies, different reaction mechanisms were evaluated. A 
model with two carbon pools was proposed: CHx* and CH2O* (Figure 11). The chain-growth rate 

38 
 
coefficient was predicted to be two orders larger than the methane formation rate constant. The 
availability of hydrogen is the key parameter for CO reactivity and the relative contribution of the two 
carbon pools. At high CO pressures, a low H* coverage makes the CHx* route dominant. In a follow-up 
study, the group of Holmen suggested water (or hydroxyl groups) to assist in CO activation, while chain-
growth occurs via a vinylene intermediate196. The chain-growth monomer (CH*) couples with the 
alkylidene (RCH*) chain to form the vinylene (RCHCH*) intermediate, which is hydrogenated to 
alkylidene or alkene. 
 
Figure 11. CH4 formation mechanism proposed by the group of Holmen with CH2O* (Cα*) and CHx* (Cβ*) as carbon monomer 
pools195. Adapted with permission from ref.195, Copyright 2013 Elsevier. 
Kinetic and spectroscopic measurements of CO hydrogenation on ruthenium by Liu et al. show that at 
high CO* coverages, CO hydrogenation rates are underpredicted by Langmuirian models103. At low 
temperatures, co-adsorbate interactions are negligible and Langmuirian treatments are possible, but 
this model breaks down at higher pressures. To explain this rate discrepancy near monolayer CO* 
coverages, CO activation rates were estimated by assuming HCOH* formation as the kinetically 
relevant step. DFT calculations suggested a lower activation free energy for HCOH* formation when 
the CO* coverage reaches around 1 ML. The HCOH* formation transition state occupies a lower area 
than the reactants (2 CO*), which resulted in a negative activation area. Similar to the concept of 
negative activation volume in liquid phase reactions, the apparent rate constant increases when CO 
pressure (and CO* coverage) increases. This suggests that, when CO adlayers densify beyond 1 ML, an 
increase in the rate compared to Langmuirian models might be observed.  

39 
 
3.2 Theoretical studies 
Over the past 20 years, DFT calculations have become increasingly popular to evaluate mechanistic 
proposals. Surface and particle structures have been discussed in the previous chapter, suggesting 
active sites which can be investigated for both CO activation and chain-growth. The developments in 
the last 10-15 years are summarized here. 
3.2.1 CO activation 
The dissociation of CO is considered a key step in the Fischer-Tropsch mechanism, as it forms CHx* 
species, which act as monomers and/or chain-initiators. In the carbide mechanism, CHx* is the 
monomer and a low CO dissociation barrier is necessary, such that methane formation is slower than 
CO activation and chain growth is preferred15. For the CO-insertion mechanism, CO* is the monomer 
and a high CO* coverage is favored, allowing for slower CO dissociation197. Calculated barriers for direct 
CO dissociation on Co(111) and Co(0001), the dominant surfaces on cobalt particles85,90, are typically 
larger than 200 kJ/mol22,198. These barriers are too high to explain the experimental TOFs and the 
measured effective activation energies. Therefore, alternative pathways on different sites and via 
different mechanistic routes have been proposed. Direct routes, H-assisted routes and CO-insertion 
routes have been proposed (Figure 12). The CO-insertion route, where CO inserts in the growing chain 
followed by C-O dissociation, will be discussed in the section on chain-growth. 

40 
 
 
Figure 12. Some of the CO activation mechanisms. gray: direct CO dissociation; teal and gold: (O)H-assisted pathways. Further 
hydrogenation by (H* or OH*) of HCO* or COH* to HCOH* or H2CO* can occur prior to C-O scission. 
In an early study in 2006, Neurock and Ge investigated direct CO dissociation on the (0001), (112̅0), 
(101̅2) and (112̅4) cobalt HCP facets198. It was shown that increasing CO* coverages increase the direct 
CO dissociation barrier (218 kJ/mol at 1/9ML and 232 kJ/mol at 1/4ML). This value is similar to the 
barrier of 220 kJ/mol reported by Zhuo et al.22 The high CO dissociation barrier on Co(0001) was 
attributed to the repulsive interactions between the C and O adatoms in the transition state. While CO 
adsorption on the studied facets was not structure-sensitive (i.e., similar adsorption energies were 
calculated), the CO dissociation activation energies were significantly lower on more open and stepped 
surface facets. On the Co(112̅0) surface, the activation energy was 195 kJ/mol, while on the stepped 
Co(101̅2) and Co(112̅4) surfaces, barriers of respectively 123 kJ/mol and 89 kJ/mol were calculated. CO 
dissociation has a late transition state and the activation energy is largely determined by the stability 
of the products (C* and O*), which bind strongly at step-sites. 
Shetty et al. calculated a low barrier of 65 kJ/mol for the direct dissociation on Ru(112̅1)199. On the 
open Ru(101̅0) and Co(101̅0) surfaces, Shetty and Van Santen calculated low barriers for the direct CO 
dissociation of respectively 47 kJ/mol and 68 kJ/mol200. The group of Li investigated the structure 
sensitivity of the CO dissociation reaction on Co(0001), Co(101̅2) and Co(112̅0)201. On the last two 
surfaces, direct CO dissociation was preferred over CO desorption with respective barriers of 129 
kJ/mol and 134 kJ/mol. When comparing direct CO dissociation on flat and stepped Co(0001), Zhao et 

41 
 
al. found an activation energy barrier that was 96 kJ/mol lower for the stepped surface compared to 
the flat terrace202. 
Several groups have reported higher activities for the HCP phase compared to the FCC phase156,203-205. 
In an attempt to explain the higher activity of Co-HCP nanoparticles, Liu et al. performed DFT 
calculations for CO activation86. On the most abundant surfaces according to Wulff structures (Figure 
3) CO dissociation rates were calculated for low CO coverages at equilibrium (pCO=3*10-5 Pa, θCO < 0.25 
ML). It was shown that at least four facets of cobalt HCP – (112̅1), (101̅1), (101̅2) and (112̅0) – have 
higher CO dissociation rates than the cobalt FCC surface facet with the highest rate (i.e., (100)). 
Moreover, on HCP cobalt nanoparticle, the surfaces with high CO dissociation rates cover more than 
50% of the surface. 
The atomistic particle model constructed by Van Helden et al. suggested that the most common active 
sites on a nanoparticle are B3, B4, B5-A, B5-B and B6 (Figure 4)85. The B3 site can be found on the Co(111) 
surface, which has a direct CO dissociation barrier of 239 kJ/mol86. The direct CO dissociation activation 
energy on the B4 site, resembling the Co(100) surface, was calculated to be 144 kJ/mol86. This leaves 
the B5 and B6 sites as possible active sites for direct CO dissociation. Petersen et al. discussed the impact 
of step (B5) and kink (B6) sites on the CO activation143. The Co(321) and Co(221) surfaces represent the 
kink and step sites, respectively. They concluded that direct CO dissociation on FCC cobalt was 
preferred at the step and kink sites and calculated barriers of 150 kJ/mol on Co(221) and 117 kJ/mol 
on Co(321). Despite the low activation barrier on the FCC kink site (i.e., Co(321)), CO dissociation rates 
are still higher on the HCP surfaces86. Results by Saeys and coworkers show Gibbs free energy CO 
dissociation barriers of 97 kJ/mol at the B5 site of Co(211)142. The low CO dissociation barriers for these 
open surfaces (e.g., (211), (112̅1)) containing B5 sites and the increase in the fraction of B5 sites for 
particles from 1 to 6 nm is proposed to explain the increase in TOF for particle sizes in that range85. 
Using crystal orbital Hamilton population analysis, Foppa et al. investigated CO adsorption at Ru step-
edges206. In their discussion, two different step-edge sites are considered, based on the adsorption 

42 
 
mode of CO: i) flat sites (η1) , where CO adsorption is normal to the surface, similar to close-packed 
terraces, and ii) concave sites (η2), where a tilted CO molecule is almost parallel with the surface. The 
electron delocalization of the CO-metal d mixed band for CO* adsorbed on the concave site results in 
a higher electron back-donation for concave sites, increasing the CO adsorption strength. The weaker 
Ru-O interaction on smaller particles is proposed to increase CO dissociation barriers and decrease CO 
back-donation on the concave sites. Additionally, CO adsorption on the flat sites is suggested to be 
stronger due to increased σ donation. Therefore, smaller particles favor η1-bonded CO over η2-bonded 
CO. Foppa et al. propose that these trends rationalize the observed particle size dependence of CO-FT 
TOF. 
Support for H-assisted pathways has also been reported, especially on the Co(0001) surface. Inderwildi 
et al. were one of the first to calculate the energies for the H-assisted pathway24. The hydrogenation 
of CO* to HCO* and H2CO* significantly reduces C-O dissociation barriers (272 kJ/mol for CO*, 96 
kJ/mol for HCO* and 82 kJ/mol for H2CO*). However, the formation of HCO* has a barrier of 126 kJ/mol 
and is strongly endothermic. Cheng et al. found a similar barrier on Co(0001) for CO* hydrogenation 
to HCO* of 126 kJ/mol, a barrier of 53 kJ/mol for subsequent H2CO* formation and a 92 kJ/mol barrier 
for H2CO* dissociation207. DFT calculations with the PBE functional by Zhuo et al. also showed barriers 
of 90 kJ/mol for HCO* dissociation and 68 kJ/mol for the dissociation of H2CO*, both very unstable 
intermediates22. Their calculations suggested that the CO-insertion pathway is more favorable for C-O 
dissociation. The step-edge sites have also been investigated for H-assisted pathways. Van Helden et 
al. found that the HCO* (or formyl) pathway is the most likely on the Co(211) surface, exhibiting the 
B5-site208. 
DFT calculations by Liu et al. suggest that HCP cobalt catalysts prefer the direct CO dissociation route 
while FCC cobalt catalysts prefer a H-assisted route (Figure 13)86. On the other hand, Petersen et al. 
concluded that the direct CO dissociation mechanism was preferred for the step and kink sites on FCC 

43 
 
cobalt143. Inclusion of the zero-point energy correction favored the direct pathway more than the H-
assisted pathway. 
 
Figure 13. Potential energy (kJ/mol, referred to gasphase CO and ½ H2), of the transition state for CO activation via the direct 
route (CO* → C* + O*, teal) or via the H-assisted route (CO* + H* → HCO* → CH* + O*, gold) on the most common HCP and 
FCC surface facets, according to Wulff structures. Adapted with permission from ref.86, Copyright 2013 American Chemical 
Society. 
On ruthenium, where the active phase is also metallic, Shetty et al. proposed a synchronized CO 
activation and C-C coupling pathway on the Ru(112̅1) surface209. Upon CO dissociation, the formed C* 
is hydrogenated to CH* which diffuses to the top of the step-edge site, vacating the CO activation site 
at the bottom of the step-edge. The barrier to dissociate a subsequent CO* increases slightly from 65 
kJ/mol to 70 kJ/mol with the nearby CH*. The newly formed CH* can then again diffuse to the top of 
the step-edge site next to the previously formed CH* and form CHCH*. The nearby CHCH* molecule 
also barely affects the CO dissociation barrier (63 kJ/mol). In this way the self-blocking of CO activation 
sites by C* and O* is circumvented. Earlier, Liu et al. suggested a similar mechanism with CH* diffusion 
from the step sites, followed by C*+CH* coupling210. The H-assisted pathways have also been studied 
on ruthenium, providing feasible pathways on Ru(101̅0), Ru(112̅1) and Ru(0001) surfaces199,200,211. 
It seems CO activation pathways mainly occur through the H-assisted pathways on the terraces sites 
(i.e., Co(0001)), but also step-edge sites have been investigated for this route. The direct pathway 

44 
 
seems to be favored on the more open surfaces, containing low-coordinated sites. The different CO 
dissociation pathways are summarized in Figure 14. 
 
Figure 14. Summary of CO activation mechanisms, including the H2O-mediated mechanisms as proposed by Hibbitts et al.188 
and OH-mediated mechanisms as proposed by Gunasooriya et al.212 
Research by the groups of Mavrikakis and Iglesia suggested H-assisted pathways on both iron and 
cobalt catalysts187,213. DFT calculations, with the PW91 functional, on 0.5 ML CO-covered Co(0001) 
surfaces resulted in a barrier of 367 kJ/mol for direct CO dissociation, while the barrier for CO 
hydrogenation to HCO* is 138 kJ/mol187. The formation of H2CO* was also considered, but the barrier 
for C-O dissociation was too high, suggesting that it desorbs as formaldehyde. In a follow-up study, the 
KIE for cobalt was calculated for 0.5 ML CO-covered Co(0001)213. An inverse isotope effect (rH₂ < rD₂) 
was observed as a result of compensating thermodynamic (H2 dissociation to 2 H* and H* addition to 
CO* to form HCO*) and kinetic (H* addition to HCO* to form HCOH*) isotope effects, implying that a 
H-assisted pathway is followed. The proposed pathway via HCOH* differs from the H2CO* pathway 
proposed by Cheng et al.207 and Inderwildi et al.24, which was attributed to a difference in the CO* 
coverage. H-assisted pathways clearly lower the C-O dissociation barrier, but the hydrogenated 

45 
 
intermediates have relatively high formation barriers and CO hydrogenation is often very endothermic. 
This results in high overall energy barriers. 
To account for coverage effects, Loveless et al. investigated the CO-covered Ru(0001) surface and a 
CO-covered ~2 nm Ru201 nanoparticle126. While the saturation coverage on the Ru(0001) surface was 
calculated to be around 0.75 ML, the curvature of the nanoparticle and the ability of adsorbing geminal 
carbonyls increased the calculated saturation coverage to a very high 1.55 ML. DFT calculations show 
that barriers for H-assisted CO activation (via HCOH*) are significantly lower than unassisted CO 
dissociation on (111) terraces of Ru201 (322 kJ/mol compared to 165 kJ/mol). The high CO* coverage 
also increased the intrinsic barrier for direct CO dissociation significantly (280 kJ/mol vs. 227 kJ/mol). 
The high CO dissociation barrier is not consistent with the experimental rate equation, where a positive 
reaction order of H2 is measured. Low-coordinated sites were also investigated, but they gave even 
higher barriers (356 kJ/mol for the corner site and 269 kJ/mol for the step-edge site) due to repulsive 
interactions with vicinal CO*. Loveless et al. suggest that the abundance of B5 sites could account for 
the lower TOF on smaller particles, while the prevalence of close-packed surfaces on larger particles 
could explain the increase in TOF with particle size. This view is quite different from the proposals in 
the previous paragraphs. It must be noted that the step-edge used in this work has a very unfavorable 
CO dissociation barrier, even at low coverage (151 kJ/mol)28,214, significantly higher than previous 
research on other step-edge site geometries. 
Hibbitts et al. also investigated CO activation near a growing hydrocarbon chain, which is proposed to 
be an alkylidyne species215. The high coverage of CO (1.05 ML) again blocks all the active sites and, to 
activate CO, one CO molecule needs to desorb to make space for the transition state, thereby 
increasing the activation energy. The growing chain disrupts the CO-covered layer, making space for 
H-assisted CO activation (via HCOH*) without CO desorption, and lowering the effective activation 
enthalpy and the Gibbs free energy barriers to respectively 127-145 kJ/mol and 204-220 kJ/mol, 
depending on the length of the growing alkylidyne chain. The very high coverage in this work (and in 

46 
 
the work by Loveless et al.126) has been criticized since the high coverage in this model is probably due 
to the expansion and restructuring of the very small particle used in this study and likely not possible 
for the larger particles that are typically used in FT synthesis28. 
Su et al. showed that higher CO* coverages destabilize larger molecules such as HCO* and CHxCO* 
more than small molecules like CHx* and CO*, due to a combined effect of substrate mediated 
repulsive interactions and steric hindrance216. Zijlstra et al. investigated the influence of CO* coverage 
on terrace and step-edge sites on corrugated surfaces on CO activation barriers214. The barrier for CO 
activation on the terrace site significantly increases with CO* coverage. However, CO activation 
barriers on the step-edges are barely affected by higher CO* coverages. This different behavior was 
attributed to the flexibility of CO molecules to reduce repulsive electron-electron interactions on the 
corrugated surface compared to the flat terraces.  
Using ab-initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) and metadynamics, Foppa et al. simulated the dynamic 
effects of the CO adlayer on ruthenium NPs and surface slabs217. On terraces, the formyl (HCO* → 
H2CO(g) → HCOH* → CH* + OH*) pathway is followed, while on the B5 step-edges, the COH* (COH* → 
C* + OH*) pathway is preferred. The overall Gibbs free energy barriers for the formyl, COH*, and 
hydroxymethylidyne (COH* → HCOH* → CH* +OH*) mechanisms are 93, 81, and 117 kJ/mol, whereas 
the direct C–O dissociation mechanism at the step-edge requires at least 290 kJ/mol. 
On the CO-covered cobalt surface, a massive surface reconstruction under FT conditions has been 
proposed to create ~2 nm nanoislands terminated by B5 sites (Figure 9)101,150. Banerjee et al. 
investigated the reactivity of the B5 sites of these nanoislands, accounting for the coverage under 
reaction conditions, i.e., 1 ML CO and 0.5 ML C on the step-edge and ~0.5 ML CO on the nearby 
terrace142. The B5 site between two C-occupied B5 sites has a high CO dissociation barrier. To allow CO 
adsorption and dissociation, the carbon atom occupying the B5 site is first hydrogenated to CH*, which 
diffuses to the terrace. CO can then adsorb on the newly created vacancy site and dissociate directly 
or undergo hydrogenation to COH*, followed by C-O dissociation. Both pathways are competitive with 

47 
 
Gibbs free energy barriers of 145 and 140 kJ/mol, respectively. An Arrhenius activation energy of 135 
kJ/mol and a TOF of ~10-2 s-1 are consistent with experimental literature. The kinetic role of hydrogen, 
resulting in a positive order for the rate equation, is related to the creation of the C vacancy at the B5 
sites. The creation of B5 sites by surface reconstruction and their increasing concentration for larger 
particles, is suggested as an explanation for the observed particle size effect. 
Hibbitts et al. investigated the role of water as a co-catalyst in the CO activation on ruthenium and 
proposed 2 different routes, based on the assumption that the transition state of the rate-determining 
step forms from 1 CO* and 2 H* on 2 vicinal Ru atoms188. Comparison of the DFT calculations for the 
formyl route (route c in Figure 14) and the hydroxymethylidyne route (route b in Figure 14) on the 
(111) terrace of a 1.5 ML CO-covered Ru201 nanoparticle shows that the formyl route is favored with 
an effective barrier of 193 kJ/mol (322 kJ/mol for direct CO dissociation). Water can affect the formyl 
and hydroxymethylidyne pathways in different ways: as H-source, as a H2O solvent or as a proton 
shuttle. Hibbitts et al. found that, for the formyl route, the enthalpy stabilization by H2O does not 
compensate for the concomitant entropy losses. The activation free energy for the H2O-mediated 
formyl routes are even higher than for the anhydrous route. However, for the hydroxymethylidyne 
route, proton-shuttling lowers the barrier for COH* formation to 111 kJ/mol, resulting in an overall 
barrier of 129 kJ/mol (Figure 15). The entropic losses due to H2O fixation are proposed to be 
compensated by enthalpic stabilization of the transition state for COH* formation. This pathway can 
explain the experimental rate-enhancement by H2O addition. 

48 
 
 
Figure 15. Reaction energy diagram for H2O-mediated formyl and hydroxymethylidyne CO activation pathways. Energies are 
referred to a CO*-covered Ru201 nanoparticle. Adapted from Hibbits et al.188 
The high activation energies for CO* hydrogenation to COH* are proposed to result from the large 
distance between H* and the O atom in CO*. High energies are associated with breaking of the Co-H 
bond and tilting of the CO molecule. In the earlier proposed proton-shuttling pathway, H2O bridges 
this gap188. Gunasooriya et al. proposed that hydroxyl groups can also decrease this distance and 
facilitate CO activation212. Similar to the H2O-mediated pathways (hydrogenation by H2O or proton-
shuttling), hydrogenation of CO* by OH* groups lowers the hydrogenation barriers significantly and, 
as opposed to H2O-mediated routes, the entropy penalty is smaller. This resulted in a free energy 
barrier of 94 kJ/mol for the hydroxyl-mediated route as opposed to the 139 kJ/mol for the H2O-
mediated pathway. This leads to two possible pathways to form HCOH*, via COH* or HCO*. Based on 
the energy diagram in Figure 16a, these two pathways are competitive, but comparison of the Gibbs 
free energies of the respective transition states at a CO* coverage of 1/3 ML shows the route via HCO* 
and HCOH* to be the most likely (Figure 16b). The barriers for H2CO* formation and the subsequent 
formaldehyde formation are clearly higher. The regeneration of hydroxyl species from O* was 
sufficiently fast to assume that OH* formation was quasi-equilibrated. 

49 
 
a) 
 
b) 
 
Figure 16. a) Reaction energy diagram calculated by Gunasooriya et al. for the hydrogen and hydroxyl assisted CO activation. 
Values are effective energy barriers and some transition states are shown. b) Gibbs free energies for selected transition states 
along the CO activation pathway (with respect to adsorbed CO* and gas-phase H2 and H2O at 500 K, 20 bar and 60% 
conversion). Gibbs free energies include entropy contributions and can give a better representation of the kinetics of the 
reaction. The dominant pathway is indicated in green and connected by arrows. Reproduced with permission from ref.212, 
Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. 
Many surface facets, suggested by atomistic models and Wulff structures, have been investigated for 
direct CO dissociation. In Table 2, a summary of the respective active sites and activation energies is 
presented. On both cobalt and ruthenium catalysts, the step-edge sites provide low-energy pathways 
for direct CO dissociation. Both the adsorption mode, resulting in a pre-activation of the CO bond, and 
the stability of the products lower CO activation barriers on step-edges. H-assisted pathways, on 
terraces and at step-edges, are summarized in Table 3. Higher CO* coverages shift the favored pathway 
from direct CO dissociation to H-assisted pathways. H2O- and OH-mediated routes have been proposed 
to lower barriers for H-assisted pathways. Realistic surface structures, including coverages of different 
intermediates, can significantly alter the reaction mechanism for CO activation.
