52 
 
3.2.2 Chain-growth 
Various chain-growth mechanisms have been proposed, which can be discriminated by their 
monomer. FT products obey the Anderson-Schulz-Flory (ASF) distribution and there is consensus that 
the monomer is a C1 species. Two main C-C coupling pathways will be discussed here: the CO-insertion 
mechanism with a CO monomer and the carbide mechanism with a CHx* monomer (Figure 1). First, 
the resting state or nature of the growing chain will be discussed, i.e., the most stable surface 
hydrocarbon intermediate. 
Initial studies suggested CH2* as the monomer and the alkyl species (RCH2*) as the growing chain in 
the carbide mechanism12,13,15,38. More recent studies suggest chain-growth occurs on the terraces with 
alkylidyne species (RC*) as the growing chain and CH* as the monomer. In absence of adsorbed CO*, 
alkynes are the most stable hydrocarbon intermediates on cobalt terraces and conversion of alkynes 
(RCCH*) to alkylidynes (RCH2C*) is endothermic148. Based on the results of Zhuo et al.102, Weststrate et 
al. investigated the stability of C2 species in the presence of CO*. In the presence of CO*, ethylidyne is 
more stable than acetylene according to spectroscopic data218. Indeed, the relative stability of the 
reaction intermediates is severely impacted by the CO* coverage. All species are destabilized, but some 
are much more destabilized than others. Figure 17 shows the stronger destabilization of alkyne species 
as opposed to the alkylidyne species at higher CO* coverage84.  
In the alkylidyne mechanism, chains grow by coupling of CH* to RC*, forming alkynes (RCCH*). After 
several (de)hydrogenation steps, an alkylidyne with an extra CH2 group is formed. Weststrate et al. 
suggest that the high stability of the alkylidyne chains correlates with long residence times on the 
surface, allowing time to grow the chain17,218. Alkylidynes bear a close resemblance to the chain-growth 
monomer CH*. Another trend that can be observed in Figure 17 is that the relative stability of 
RCH2CO*, a key intermediate in the CO-insertion mechanism, is barely affected by the CO* coverage. 
This suggests that the subsequent C-O scission reaction is more favorable at CO* saturation coverages. 

53 
 
Studies by Hibbitts et al. also investigated the alkylidyne chain-growth mechanism. They suggest that 
on Ru218, CHx* monomers are formed near growing alkylidyne chains, which are more abundant (by a 
factor of ~108) than alkylidene and alkyl chains on (111) terrace of the Ru218 particle215. The formation 
of monomers near hydrocarbons at high CO* coverage leads to fast chain-growth and the production 
of long-chain hydrocarbons. 
 
Figure 17. Reaction enthalpies (kJ/mol) for the formation of key reaction intermediates in C−C coupling on a clean (▬) and 
a CO−saturated (▬) cobalt terrace, relative to adsorbed CO*, CH*, RC* and gas phase H2. The calculations shown here are 
for R = H, but they are comparable for R = CH3. Adapted from De Vrieze et al.84 
In the previous paragraphs, studies of chain-growth on CO-covered terraces were discussed. An 
alternative point of view suggests that chain-growth happens at the step-edges while methanation 
happens on the terrace sites18,154. To support this view, Chen et al. deposited graphitic carbon on 
supported cobalt catalysts, and evaluated its effect on the activity and selectivity154. After carbon 
deposition, the activity and methane selectivity decreased. They assumed that the graphitic carbon 
blocks the terrace sites and therefore methane formation was linked to the terrace sites. Since graphite 
formation typically nucleates at extended step-edge sites146,147, carbon deposition also affects the step-
edge sites. Experimental evidence for chain-growth at the step-edges was provided by Qin et al., who 

54 
 
synthesized nanocatalysts with either an abundance of corrugated or close-packed surfaces219. They 
found that catalysts containing more stepped sites (e.g., Co(101̅1)) produced mainly long hydrocarbon 
chains, while the catalyst exposing the Co(0001) facet showed higher methane formation. Yet, the 
catalyst exposing mainly Co(112̅0) produced the most methane. 
On ruthenium catalysts, Shetty et al. suggested CH* as the chain growth monomer, since it is the most 
stable CHx* intermediate, even in absence of CO*209. Initial chain coupling via CH* + CH* → CHCH* was 
proposed to occur at the top of the step-edge. In a follow-up study on Ru(112̅1), Filot et al. concluded 
that CH* is the chain growth monomer and that alkylidyne chains grow at the step-edge sites220. They 
calculated that CH* + RC* (R=alkyl) coupling is easier than CH* + CH* coupling because of the higher 
σ-donation by the alkyl group. CH* + CH* coupling was more favorable at step-edge sites than at 
terrace sites because of the stronger overlap of the forming π-bond with partially empty d-orbitals of 
the step-edge atoms. The calculated overall barrier for chain-growth in the CO-insertion mechanism 
via C* + CO* → CCO* was considerably higher than the barrier for the carbide mechanism, CH* + CH* 
→ CHCH*. 
Foppa et al. employed metadynamics and AIMD simulations to investigate the influence of CO adlayers 
on C-C coupling on ruthenium terraces and step-edges221. The initial coverages for the terrace and 
step-edge sites were 0.5 ML and 0.72 ML CO, respectively, and 0.24 ML H*. The H* coverage in these 
simulations is somewhat high for a CO*-saturated surface. The high CO* coverage was calculated to 
suppress methane formation by segregating CHx* groups from H* surface species. The high CO* 
coverage also reduced C-C bond formation barriers on flat surfaces and drove chain growth. Methane 
formation was slow at the step-edges irrespective of the CO* coverage. In their analysis, CH2* is the 
most stable CHx* species, and CH2* + CH2* coupling is the favored C-C coupling step in the presence of 
CO*, while CH* + CH2* and CH2* + CH3* coupling happens in the absence of co-adsorbed CO* on the 
step-edge and the terrace, respectively. Subsequent formation of the ethylidene (CH3CH*) species and 
coupling to the CH2* monomer led to propylene. This pathway is proposed to explain the selectivity to 

55 
 
olefins. While the C-C coupling Gibbs free energy barrier on the terraces is lower than on the step-edge 
(20 kJ/mol vs. 100 kJ/mol), the higher stability of CH2* at the step-edge, and the resulting higher CH2* 
coverage, allows C-C coupling also at the step-edges. The 0.24 ML H* coverage in these simulations 
corresponds to a rather high H2 partial pressure and might explain the preference for CH2* over CH*. 
 
Figure 18. The carbide (teal) and CO-insertion (gold) mechanism with CH* and CO* as respective monomers and alkylidyne 
as the growing chain. Termination steps can form either hydrocarbons or oxygenates. 
CO-insertion was originally proposed by Pichler and Schulz to explain the oxygenate selectivity8. In the 
CO-insertion mechanism, CO inserts into the growing chain (RCHx*), followed by a more facile C-O 
bond dissociation (C-assisted pathway). Weststrate et al. showed that C-O bond dissociation in ethanol 
is indeed feasible on cobalt terraces at Fischer-Tropsch temperatures, and preferred over C-C bond 
scission23. The high calculated barriers for CO insertion into RCH2*, 182-185 kJ/mol22,207, make the CO-
insertion step originally proposed by Schulz et al.8,222 highly unlikely. The strong directional RCH2-Co 
bond is responsible for this high barrier15,223. Alternative C-C coupling steps were proposed by Zhuo et 
al.22 CO insertion into RCH* has a low barrier of 80 kJ/mol on Co terraces, and CO insertion into RC* is 
also feasible with a barrier of 107 kJ/mol. RCHCO* and RCCO* are then hydrogenated to RCH2CHO* 
and undergo C-O dissociation with a barrier as low as 50 kJ/mol, similar to the C-O dissociation barrier 
in H2CO*, Table 3. In the CO-insertion mechanism, a chain initiating CHx* species is still necessary. 
Initial microkinetic simulations using the barriers calculated for empty cobalt terraces predicted a 
higher TOF (~10-8 s-1) for the CO-insertion mechanism than for H-assisted CO-dissociation (~10-10 s-1) or 

56 
 
for direct CO dissociation (~10-12 s-1) on Co terraces. Hydrogenation of RCHCO* to RCH2CO* was rate-
controlling in their CO-insertion mechanism. Zhuo et al. argued that the low calculated TOFs are related 
to the low coverages, and the resulting strong binding energies of all reaction intermediates in their 
calculations. Figure 18 illustrates the alkylidyne and CO-insertion mechanism proposed in the previous 
paragraphs. 
In a follow-up study, all barriers were recomputed for cobalt terraces with a 1/3 ML CO* coverage102. 
The CO* overlayer destabilized all reaction intermediates and transition states, and hence reduced the 
barriers for coupling reactions since the transition state was less destabilized than the two reactants. 
Concurrently, dissociation reactions -where the number of species increases- became more difficult. 
The overall barrier for the CO-insertion mechanism decreased from 175 kJ/mol on the clean cobalt 
terraces to 111 kJ/mol for the CO*-covered terraces. In the preferred mechanism, RC* is hydrogenated 
to RCH* followed by CO insertion, hydrogenation to RCH2CO* and finally C-O dissociation. Comparison 
of this C-O scission step step with RCH2CO* hydrogenation to RCH2CHO* and formation of oxygenates 
shows a slightly lower Gibbs free energy barrier for C-O dissociation. The relatively low H* coverage is 
suggested to limit oxygenate formation via RCH2CO* hydrogenation. The predicted TOF of 0.02 s-1 is 
close to experimental values. Similar to the previous work, RCHCO* hydrogenation remains the rate-
limiting step. A possible shortcoming of the proposed CO-insertion mechanism is the relatively high 
selectivity to aldehydes, somewhat similar to the high CH2O selectivity in H-assisted CO 
dissociation24,187. As with the H-assisted CO dissociation on cobalt terraces, H2O and OH-assisted 
pathways open an alternative hydrogenation step. 
As in H-assisted CO dissociation mechanism, hydrogenation of the carbon atom by surface hydrogen 
has a lower barrier than hydrogenation of the O atom. However, hydrogenation of the O atom leads 
to a more stable product (e.g., HCOH*). Gunasooriya et al. proposed proton-transfer from hydroxyl 
groups to O atoms as a new pathway with a low barrier212, expanding the earlier proposal of a water-
mediated proton-shuttle mechanism188. At a CO* coverage of 1/3 ML, Gibbs free energy barriers for 

57 
 
RCH2C-O* dissociation and RCH2C-OH* dissociation are very close, respectively 131 kJ/mol and 134 
kJ/mol, suggesting a competition between both pathways. Aldehyde formation, via RCH2CO* 
hydrogenation to RCH2CHO*, has a higher Gibbs free energy barrier of 139 kJ/mol. Microkinetic 
analysis with a RC* coverage of 0.1 ML predicts a TOF of 0.04 s-1 for chain-growth, which is similar to 
the experimental TOF (~10-2 s-1)56,58,59, suggesting chain-growth can also occur via the CO-insertion 
mechanism. 
Su et al. also found that the CO-insertion route was preferred over the carbide mechanism for low CO* 
coverages on the close-packed Co(0001) and on stepped Co, but in their calculations CH4 formation 
has an even lower overall barrier216. On the more open Co(101̅1) surface, the carbide mechanism was 
preferred and higher C2 selectivities were calculated. Increasing the CO* coverage led to a higher chain 
growth probability on Co(0001), highlighting the importance of structure sensitivity and coverage 
effects. Other carbon chain growth mechanisms were also investigated, such as the HCO* insertion 
mechanism. In this mechanism, CO* is first hydrogenated to HCO* before HCO* insertion into the 
growing chain. CO* hydrogenation is highly endothermic, resulting in very low HCO* coverages, 
explaining why HCO* is not observed in experiments. The low coverage of HCO* will also result in low 
rates for the HCO* insertion route. Calculations showed lower barriers for HCO* insertion than for CO 
insertion on Co(0001) and Rh(111)25, but this is more than compensated by the low HCO* coverages. 
Calculated C-O scission barriers are generally lower on cobalt than on rhodium, in line with the higher 
oxygenate selectivity on rhodium. 
Van Santen and Markvoort investigated the kinetics of the CO-insertion mechanism and concluded 
that quantum chemical calculations and empirical kinetic rate expressions are not consistent with the 
CO-insertion mechanism197. In the CO-insertion mechanism, CO insertion and C-O scission must be fast 
compared to chain termination and methanation, and CO dissociation has to be slow to allow a high 
CO* monomer concentration. DFT calculations at the time showed slow CO insertion rates compared 
to CO* desorption, suggesting limited chain-growth via CO insertion. Van Santen and Markvoort wrote 

58 
 
that the high CO insertion barrier is consistent with the formation of oxygenates via the carbide 
mechanism, where CHx* is the monomer. According to their analysis, chain-growth is limiting in the 
CO-insertion mechanism, and experimental kinetic rate equations are not consistent with the CO-
insertion mechanism. To properly compare the carbide and CO-insertion mechanism, detailed 
microkinetic models are necessary since rates depend on both the rate coefficient and monomer 
concentration. While the rate coefficient for RC* + CH* coupling is higher than for RCHx* + CO* 
coupling, the CO* surface coverage is at least an order of magnitude higher than the CH* coverage. 
Initial microkinetic simulations will be discussed in section 3.5. Several rates moreover govern FT 
synthesis, the rate of monomer formation or chain initiation, the rate of chain growth and the rate of 
chain termination.  
Surface coverages are moreover very important in FT synthesis, and impact both the relative stability 
of the intermediates and the activation energies. In a way, they act as a surface solvent that favors 
certain reaction steps over others. High CO* coverages present under FT conditions lower the barriers 
for C-C coupling,102,221 alter the stability of the chain growth intermediate (e.g., alkylidyne)84,215,218, and 
reduce rates for scission reactions. The active site for C-C coupling is still under debate. Many studies 
propose that chain growth happens on the CO-covered terraces102,215,221, others assume the step-edge 
as the active site18,154,220. The dominant chain-growth mechanism has not been identified either. The 
majority tends to support the carbide mechanism, with either a CH* or CH2* monomer18,154,197,209,220,221, 
but the CO-insertion mechanism also receives some support22,102,212. 
DFT calculations at low coverage do not accurately capture the complex surface structure during the 
FT reaction. To quantify the effects of more realistic surfaces and the interplay of the different 
reactions, microkinetic models based on theoretical calculations at realistic surface coverages are 
necessary.  

59 
 
3.3 Surface science and experimental mechanistic studies 
The Fischer-Tropsch mechanism on Co(0001), a model for the terraces of larger nanoparticles85,90, has 
been studied extensively by surface science. Bimolecular reactions involving hydrogen have been 
suggested to dissociate molecules with strong bonds (e.g., CO) on low-index surfaces224. The role of 
terraces in the reaction through creating specific ensembles with the adsorbed species, e.g., CO, will 
be discussed17,102,103,126,215. Defect sites, which can for example be created by sputtering, are often 
considered as the sites where π-bonded molecules, such as CO, are activated198,200,211.  
To investigate direct CO dissociation, Böller et al. performed an operando STM study on a Co(0001) 
sample151. By sputtering the sample, defect sites were created, and it was observed that the CO TOF 
increased with the defect site density, suggesting that defect sites play a key role in activating CO, and 
that the rate-limiting CO activation step is structure-sensitive. A follow-up study looked at the Co(1 
0 1 ̅15) surface, which resembles a Co(0001) surface, but contains a density of step-edge sites of 2.4 
nm-2, which is only slightly lower than values predicted by atomistic models for cobalt nanoparticles of 
~8 nm (3 nm-2)85,152. By iterative sputtering and annealing below ~700 K, a surface similar to the 
Co(10 1 ̅15) was created, although steps were not uniformly distributed as in the Co(1 0 1 ̅15) surface, 
i.e., the spacing between the steps varied. In the STM images, step bunching was observed, i.e., two 
or more steps were very close. These could be either double or multiple steps, but also resembled the 
highly stepped Co(101̅1) surface. On the other hand, some parts of the sputtered surface showed 
broader terraces than suggested by the defect density, leading to an average defect density similar to 
Co(1 0 1 ̅15). The CO-TOF for the regular Co(1 0 1̅ 15) surface was higher than for the Co(0001) and 
even the sputtered Co(0001) surfaces, again correlating with the defect site density. The measured 
CO-TOF on Co(1 0 1̅ 15) was close to values for supported cobalt nanoparticles. Weststrate et al. 
studied the direct dissociation of CO on Co(0001), in the absence of hydrogen, and observed CO 
dissociation barriers of 90-105 kJ/mol at 330 K associated with defect sites225. STM verified the 
presence of defect sites on the Co(0001) sample, while XPS confirmed the formation of C* and O*. 

60 
 
In H-assisted pathways, HCO*, COH*, HCOH* and H2CO* have been proposed as intermediates (Figure 
14). Subsequent CO dissociation can proceed more easily, since the C-O bond is weakened. In situ and 
operando DRIFTS studies by Paredes-Nunez et al. observed formates (HCOO*) during FTS, but they 
might be precursors for methanol formation226. Near-ambient pressure XPS (NAP-XPS) studies by Chai 
et al. showed that H-assisted dissociation could occur at 325 K, while direct CO dissociation occurred 
at 400 K227. Oxygen removal was proposed as the rate-limiting step. 
Some particle studies were also performed to elucidate the mechanism of CO dissociation. Using X-ray 
absorption spectroscopy (XAS), Tuxen et al. suggested H-assisted pathways, forming COH* and CHxO* 
intermediates, to occur on cobalt nanoparticles66. Upon exposing 4-15 nm metallic cobalt 
nanoparticles to CO, CoOx was observed, which was suggested to originate from dissociated CO. A clear 
increase of CoOx compared to adsorbed CO* was observed for larger particles, suggesting a higher CO 
dissociation ability of larger particles, while the smallest particle did not show any activity. Addition of 
H2 to the small nanoparticle led to the formation of CoOx, suggesting H-assisted pathways to occur. 
They proposed that the higher H* coverage on the larger nanoparticle could explain the observed 
particle size effects. While the previous experiments were performed under UHV conditions, Chen et 
al. performed isotopic 12C16O/13C18O scrambling experiments for CO pressures of 45 mbar, which is 
closer to typical partial CO pressures of 7-10 bar228. It was found that the scrambling rate without H2 
was higher than the CO consumption rate under hydrogenation conditions. This indicated that CO 
dissociation can happen sufficiently fast without hydrogen assistance. Only a small fraction of the 
surface was active in this dissociation, probably defect/step-edge sites. In situ IR spectroscopy 
confirmed that CO dissociation was not affected by hydrogen. C* and O* removal were proposed as 
rate-determining steps. 
The positive reaction order in H2 suggests hydrogen is involved in the rate-determining step180,181. This 
can be attributed to the H-assisted CO dissociation pathways, but other reaction steps in FTS can also 
be rate-determining (e.g., C* and O* hydrogenation). Using isothermal reduction experiments and a 

61 
 
kinetic model at 2.3x10-5 mbar, Kizilkaya et al. calculated barriers of ~129 kJ/mol for H2O formation on 
Co(0001) and ~136 kJ/mol for H2O formation on defect sites229. Additionally, it was found that oxygen 
removal by CO was relatively hard225. The high barriers for H2O and CO2 formation were possibly due 
to the strong Co-O bond. This suggests that not CO dissociation but oxygen removal (by CO2 formation 
or H2O formation) might be rate-determining. 
The availability of hydrogen plays a significant role in FT. The H* coverage can be influenced by the 
dissociation barrier of H2. By using a combination of DFT and TPD studies, it was shown that hydrogen 
adsorption on Co(0001)/Co(111) surfaces is kinetically impeded after reaching a coverage of 0.5 ML 
H230. Investigation of the TPD spectra of the close-packed Co(0001) and more open Co(112̅0) and 
Co(101̅2) surfaces showed that low-coordinated sites facilitate H2 dissociation, suggesting the 
importance of step and kink sites in this reaction231. Step and kink sites might increase H* coverage but 
this also results in desorption at lower temperatures, by providing a low energy path for both 
adsorption and desorption232. Comparison of the adsorption strengths on the most common cobalt 
surface facets suggests hydrogen adsorption is favored on the 3-fold hollow sites of close-packed 
terraces. Under FT conditions, other species will be present affecting this hydrogen coverage and vice 
versa. TPD experiments show that adsorbed CO* reduces the dissociative sticking coefficient of H2 
adsorption233. By inhibiting hydrogen adsorption, the hydrogen coverage is reduced by tenfold. Using 
STM at very low temperatures, Lewis et al. also found that CO and H adsorb in segregated phases on 
cobalt nanoparticles, which was confirmed by DFT234. 
Schweicher et al. used chemical transient kinetics (CTK) to argue for the CO-insertion mechanism21. 
The experiments showed that a C1 species was responsible for chain growth and that CO must be 
present for chain growth to occur. They further showed a direct link between the CO pressure and 
chain-growth probability. Further CTK studies by the group of Kruse suggested that the dearth of 
vacancies for high CO coverages favors CO insertion into O-H rather than metal-C bonds, resulting in 
the formation of an alkoxy species (H3CO*)175,235. Weststrate et al. dosed ethanol on a cobalt surface, 

62 
 
which resulted in the formation of C2Hx species, suggesting that C-O dissociation in the CO-insertion 
mechanism is feasible23. 
Results in surface science studies and molecular modeling showed that acetylenic species are most 
stable on the cobalt terraces in absence of adsorbed CO*102,148,218. DFT calculations show that, in the 
presence of CO*, the growing chains are alkylidyne species, which will stay on the surface long enough 
for chain-growth to occur. Weststrate and coworkers investigated the alkylidyne chain-growth 
mechanism depicted in Figure 19a20,148. A dual-site model was presented where CO is activated at step-
edges, followed by C* hydrogenation and diffusion to the terrace where chain growth occurs by 
insertion in the alkylidyne chain. CH* is presented as the monomer based on its stability on most flat 
metallic surfaces (e.g., Co(0001) and Ni(111)), and acetylene is formed by combination of CH* 
monomers. The conversion of acetylene (C2H2*) to alkylidyne (CH3C*) becomes much more favorable 
in the presence of co-adsorbed CO* (Figure 17). To provide evidence for the stability of the growing 
chain, Weststrate et al. studied the decomposition of ethylene into C2Hx species under UHV conditions. 
In the absence of CO, ethylene decomposed to acetylene. Introduction of CO led to the formation of 
absorption bands below 1500 cm-1, which were assigned to the C-C stretching mode and the symmetric 
CH3 absorption mode of ethylidyne. This initial study led to the proposal of an ensemble model (Figure 
19b), where step-edges activate CO* and CHx* species spill over to the terrace where 1 or 2 growing 
chains reside per ensemble. These growing chains have a high mobility and diffuse towards the 
different monomer species. 
Additional studies on the decomposition of ethylene and propylene on Co(0001) suggest that alkyne 
and alkylidyne species are the most stable species in the absence of adsorbed CO* and have long 
residence times on the surface236. Due to their role in chain-growth, the stability of alkylidyne species 
was further investigated. The surface coverage of H* is proposed to promote alkylidyne formation, 
since alkylidyne species need less adsorption sites than alkynes. Next, the influence of adsorbed CO* 
was investigated218. Upon heating of an alkyne/H*-covered surface in the presence of CO*, ethylidyne, 

63 
 
propylidyne and butylidyne are formed as confirmed by temperature programmed reaction 
spectroscopy (TPRS) and reflection absorption infrared spectroscopy (RAIRS). The presence of CO* is 
proposed to destabilize the adsorbed H*, which is suggested to further promote the formation of 
alkylidyne by hydrogenating acetylene. The lower footprint of alkylidyne compared to acetylene is also 
suggested to promote the formation of alkylidynes on ‘crowded’ surfaces (i.e., in the presence of high 
CO* coverage) and provides support for the alkylidyne mechanism (Figure 19a). 
Further support for this mechanism was provided by studying C-C coupling. In situ high resolution XPS 
shows that a high CO* coverage promotes the formation of 2-butyne by the dimerization of 
ethylidyne17. Heating an acetylene-covered surface in presence of CO* leads to the formation of 
ethylidyne species, followed by the formation of 2-butyne species as observed in XPS spectra. TPRS 
spectra also show the formation of 2-butyne. Dimerization of CH* to acetylene has been observed 
before233, showing that C-C coupling can occur even in the absence of CO*, but the stabilization of the 
growing chain (i.e., alkylidyne) requires the presence of CO*. The combination of i) the stability of 
alkylidyne in presence of CO* and ii) the CH* + CH* and RC* + RC* C-C coupling activity, were suggested 
to support the alkylidyne mechanism. The higher coverage of CH*, compared to the concentration of 
growing chains under FT conditions, was suggested to favor CH* coupling to the growing chain over 
ethylidyne coupling17. The high stability and long lifetime of the alkylidyne chain is necessary to obtain 
long chains. NAP-XPS experiments show that 2-butyne does not hydrogenate easily, indicating that 
chain termination is a slow process. 

64 
 
a) 
 
b) 
 
Figure 19. a) The akylidyne chain-growth mechanism, b) Growth ensemble with one growing chain on the close-packed 
terrace where chain-growth occurs and step-edge sites where CO activation, forming monomers, can occur. To simplify the 
picture, CO* and H* adsorbates have been left out. Reproduced with permission from ref.20, Copyright 2016 Elsevier. 
 
 

65 
 
3.5 Microkinetic modeling 
Microkinetic models, which can simulate turnover frequencies, product selectivities, preferred 
mechanistic pathways and rate-controlling steps, could be key in creating a better understanding of 
the FT reaction mechanism at the molecular level237. By including all possible reactions and important 
active sites into one large mathematical model, the different mechanistic routes affecting the activity 
and selectivity can be investigated, resulting in an unbiased evaluation of the reaction mechanism, 
resolving the long-standing debates29. 
Recent surface science and DFT studies indicate that the correct representation of the coverage and 
the nature of the active sites are crucial in the Fischer-Tropsch reaction. Microkinetic models can 
simulate the coverage of relevant surface intermediates under reaction conditions and more 
accurately calculate rates, since the rate is a function of the coverage. The first microkinetic models of 
the FT reaction have been constructed by the group of Van Santen15. Advancements in surface science 
and DFT studies, inspired a new generation of microkinetic models in the last few years. In this section, 
we will summarize the efforts to model the kinetics of Fischer-Tropsch synthesis. 
In the search for an optimally performing Fischer-Tropsch catalyst, Filot et al. developed a DFT-based 
microkinetic model for a ruthenium catalyst238. In their model, CH* was the monomer and chain-
growth follows the carbide mechanism, with alkylidyne as the growing chain, on the step-edge sites of 
Ru(112̅1). CO dissociation on terraces is found to be slow and mainly methane is formed on the terrace. 
Oxygen removal, via H2O formation, is rate-limiting on the ruthenium catalyst, resulting in a high O* 
coverage and slow CO consumption. By employing scaling relations and Brønsted-Evans-Polanyi 
relations, with the metal-carbon and metal-oxygen bond strengths as descriptors, the performance for 
different theoretical catalysts was compared (Figure 20). Three distinct reaction regions were 
identified based on a change in the rate-determining reaction, i.e., CO dissociation, O* removal or 
chain-growth termination. For each region, a dominant surface species was found, i.e., O* when 
oxygen removal is rate-limiting, CO* when CO dissociation is rate-limiting, and C* when chain-growth 

66 
 
termination is rate-limiting (Figure 20). Ruthenium is situated in the O* removal limit and the surface 
is poisoned by O*, while cobalt falls in the monomer-formation limit, where CO dissociation is limiting 
(i.e., high CO* coverage). The optimal performance, or highest CO consumption rate, was found at the 
interphase of the three regions, indicating that the current FTS catalysts perform suboptimal, i.e., the 
rate is limited by O* removal or by CO dissociation.  
 
Figure 20. The CO consumption rate (rCO) and surface coverages for different catalysts as a function of the M-O* and M-C* 
bond strength in kJ/mol (500 K, 20 bar, H2/CO=2). The three regions are i) CO dissociation, ii) chain-growth and chain-growth 
termination and iii) H2O formation. Reproduced with permission from ref.238, Copyright 2014 John Wiley and Sons. 
By introducing the degree of chain-growth probability control and the thermodynamic degree of chain-
growth probability control, Filot et al. investigated the Sabatier behavior of both chain-growth and 
reaction rate on a stepped ruthenium surface239. The degree of chain-growth probability control 
estimates how much the chain growth probably changes when a rate constant of a reaction changes. 
Analysis of the chain-growth limiting and inhibiting reactions suggest that at low temperatures, chain-
growth is limited by the lack of surface vacancies to dehydrogenate the growing chain (RCHCH* → 
RCHC* + H*) and inhibited by the RCHCH* + H* → RCHCH2* reaction. The RCHCH2* intermediate can 
easily desorb as a 1-olefin. At high temperatures, chain decoupling (or depolymerization) is proposed 
to occur due to the increased number of free sites, i.e., RCH2C* → RCH2* + C* is rate-inhibiting, while 

67 
 
RCCH* + H* → RCHCH* and OH* + H* → H2O* limit chain-growth. The thermodynamic degree of rate 
control can be used to analyze how the stability of certain intermediates can alter the chain-growth 
probability. The presence of H* is proposed to suppress chain depolymerization by limiting the number 
of free sites, while hydrogenation steps are suggested to be crucial in the interconversion of growing 
chains for the next coupling step. The balance of H* and surface vacancies was concluded to be crucial 
for chain-growth. It was also concluded that optimal rates were found at higher temperatures than 
optimal chain-growth conditions, in line with experimental results181,240-242. Microkinetic modeling can 
also be employed to improve understanding of the mechanism in SSITKA experiments26. Simulations 
of isotopic scrambling experiments suggest CO dissociation happens on the step-edges and is quasi-
equilibrated. 
To more accurately represent the kinetics of a cobalt catalyst under FT conditions, Van Helden et al. 
employed a multi-site microkinetic model243. The concentrations of the 3 different sites, called A, B 
and C, representing the Co(111), Co(100) and Co(211) surfaces, respectively, were based on earlier 
simulations of cobalt nanoparticles85. In this model CO insertion was not included, and the model was 
truncated at C2 species. By using the PW91 functional, the CO adsorption energy was overestimated. 
To obtain more realistic values, an empirical correction was applied and a coverage-dependent CO 
adsorption was introduced. The A-site has low barriers for chain-growth, but CO dissociation was 
hindered leading to a mixture of H* and CO* on the surface. On the defect sites (C-sites), the low 
barriers for H-assisted CO dissociation (via HCO*) resulted in high CH* concentrations. The B-site 
produced mainly CO2, due to the high OH* formation barriers and limited O* removal by H2O. In the 
isolated site model, i.e., without diffusion of species between sites, mainly methane is formed. When 
diffusion was included in the microkinetic model (MKM), direct application of the DFT data did not give 
satisfactory results. This is most likely due to the destabilization of certain species at high CO* 
coverages which was not accounted for in the DFT calculations. By destabilizing CHx* species, a more 
accurate prediction of the coverages was obtained, although the fundamental nature of this 
destabilization was not explored. With these corrections, the model predicted chain growth to occur 

68 
 
on the terrace sites (A-site), while CO activation happens on defect C site243. At the low CO* coverage 
conditions in this study, around 3% of the C2 species on the terrace sites were CCH3*, suggesting it 
might be a key species in chain-growth and its fraction will likely increase when more realistic CO 
coverages are considered20. While the multi-site model already shows a more realistic picture of what 
happens on the molecular scale, this investigation shows the need to perform DFT calculations under 
more realistic coverage conditions. Some initial efforts on this topic are described in work by the group 
of Saeys102, but to accurately describe lateral interactions, a large number of CO-covered surfaces will 
probably have to be investigated. The advent of new techniques such as neural networks can provide 
a solution to decrease the computational cost, at near-DFT accuracy244. 
To model coverage-dependent kinetics, Hu et al. employed an iterative self-consistency scheme, 
updating the coverages until the predicted coverages were consistent with the coverages at which DFT 
energies were obtained27. Van der Waals corrections, in the form of DFT-D3, were included with the 
PBE functional, along with a free energy correction of the gas phase molecules with B3LYP/6-311g. 
Only the energy destabilization due to CO* coverage was considered, interactions with other species 
were not considered. For the coverage-dependent model, a six-fold increase in the TOF is observed 
compared to the non-coverage-dependent model. The high selectivity towards ethylene in the C2-
truncated model indicates that long-chain hydrocarbons will likely be formed. Since only the Co(0001) 
flat terraces were considered, CO dissociation (most likely via HCOH*) has a relatively high barrier and 
CH* coverages are low, which favors the CO-insertion mechanism over the carbide mechanism. At 
higher temperatures CH4 is formed at the expense of the C2 selectivity. Below 475 K, a high selectivity 
for oxygenates was obtained. The absence of defect sites in this model explains the low overall TOF 
(10-5 s-1) and illustrates that a combination of a multi-site model with coverage dependent kinetics is 
necessary to accurately describe the FTS kinetics. 
Zijlstra et al. inluded lateral interactions into a dual-site model, including Co(112̅1) and Co(0001) 
surfaces18. The inclusion of defect sites shifted the chain-growth mechanism from the CO insertion 

69 
 
mechanism to the carbide mechanism. In this model, the DFT calculations are limited to C3 species and 
extrapolations are used for longer hydrocarbons, due to the complexity of the reaction network. 
Lateral interactions were implemented by introducing a lateral penalty for a species x, which was 
chosen such that CO* coverage does not increase above 2/3 ML. The limitation of this approach is that 
co-adsorption effects are only incorporated at a qualitative level and only repulsive interactions (i.e., 
destabilization due to CO* coverage) are considered. Experimental corrections for gas phase CO and 
CO2 were applied to correct for the overbinding of the PBE functional18. Initial analysis of the reaction 
barriers shows why cobalt is such a good FTS catalyst: while CO bond scission and chain-growth 
compete, termination to olefins has a higher barrier. Simulations at pressures below 2 bar in a model 
with only step-edge sites shows good FT activity and selectivity (CH4 selectivity < 10%), since both CO 
dissociation and chain-growth occur near the step-edges according to the proposed model. Increasing 
the CO pressure or decreasing H2 pressure increases the chain-growth probability but decreases the 
activity. The relatively high TOF (1-10 s-1) is attributed to the overestimation of the number of active 
sites. Chain-growth follows the carbide mechanism with alkylidynes as growing chains, while direct CO 
dissociation is preferred, and oxygen is removed as H2O via O* hydrogenation and OH* 
disproportionation. The subsequent addition of Co(0001) terrace sites to the model led to an increase 
in CH4 selectivity. The higher CH4 selectivity was proposed to originate from CHx* hydrogenation on the 
terrace sites. Modeling with 10% step-edges and 90% terrace sites (consistent with values proposed 
by Van Helden et al.85) showed that chain-growth occurs on the terrace sites under methanation 
conditions. In the dual-site model, chain-growth probability decreased and CH4 selectivity increased at 
higher H2 pressures. The chain-growth mechanism and CO dissociation followed similar routes as in 
the single-site model, but CH3* hydrogenation is contributing significantly to the degree of rate control, 
degree of selectivity control and the degree of chain-growth control. The microkinetic model was also 
employed to investigate the applicability of Co-based catalysts in CO2 hydrogenation to liquid 
hydrocarbons (CO2-FT). The conditions correspond to a situation with high hydrogen partial pressures, 
i.e., a high H2/CO ratio. This corresponds to methanation conditions in CO-FT and chain-growth was 

70 
 
observed at the terrace sites. Liu et al. suggested chain-growth to occur on step-edge sites, reporting 
a higher selectivity towards C2+ on a stepped Co(101̅1) surface245. The discrepancy in chain-growth 
mechanisms in the different microkinetic models illustrates a long-standing debate among different 
authors28. 
Since the FT reaction network contains thousands of species and reactions, the combinatorial 
complexity can easily grow exponentially when investigating reaction pathways. Studying the 
complete reaction network is computationally costly. Additionally, microkinetic models are not easily 
transferable to new systems. In reality, only a few reaction pathways are important, i.e., only a few 
reactions will be rate-controlling, and less accurate surrogate models can be used to reduce 
complexity. To decrease the computational expense, Ulissi et al. suggested an alternative approach to 
calculate the preferred reaction pathways for a reaction network (Figure 21)246. The more accurate and 
computationally expensive DFT calculations are only performed for the important and rate-limiting 
steps. Other steps are calculated using physics-based approximations such as group additivity and 
linear scaling relations, which are sufficient to exclude the unimportant pathways in the reaction 
network. To bootstrap the process, a few DFT calculations which are important for the reaction (e.g., 
adsorption) are performed. By predicting the rate-limiting steps in each iteration and adding the DFT 
calculated energies for this step, the model can be iteratively refined with fewer calculations than 
would be necessary to study the entire network. By propagating uncertainty, the residual error for the 
final reaction mechanism can be calculated. Experimentally observed reaction orders and apparent 
activation energies could reduce the computational burden even more, by restricting potential 
pathways. This procedure was tested for ethanol formation on Rh(111), and the same rate-limiting-
step as in literature (i.e., HCOH* → CH* + OH*) was predicted after calculating the energetics of only 
5% of the transition states and 40% of the surface intermediates. At the typical levels of DFT 
uncertainty, many pathways become competitive, indicating the limits of DFT-based microkinetic 
modeling. This framework can be a tool to reduce the computational burden of microkinetic models 
for complex reaction networks with a large number of possible reaction pathways, e.g., Fischer-

71 
 
Tropsch synthesis. Further improvements in this approach could include the addition of multiple active 
sites and realistic coverages. 
 
Figure 21. a) Reaction network for CO hydrogenation to oxygenates, CO2, methane and H2O. Even for this limited reaction 
network, thousands of pathways are possible for a single active site, making materials discovery prohibitively expensive. b) 
Reduced reaction network for CO hydrogenation on Rh(111). Reproduced with permission from ref.246, Copyright 2017 
Springer Nature under the CC BY 4.0 license (http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/). 
While surface science and computational studies are promising to uncover the nature of the active 
sites and create a molecular view of the possible reaction mechanisms, microkinetic models are crucial 
to evaluate preferred pathways in a complex reaction network. Microkinetic models should be able to 
resolve mechanistic discrepancies, but inaccuracies in the model assumptions (e.g., CO* coverage, 
incomplete network) have led to conflicting conclusions. A clear molecular movie of the catalytic 
surface will help in creating a unified view.  
 

72 
 
4 Molecular views of the active site and structure of Fe-based FT 
catalysts 
Cobalt is well-known for its ability to produce long-chain hydrocarbons. Iron similarly can produce long-
chain hydrocarbons and, by altering process conditions, short-chain hydrocarbons as well. Additionally, 
iron has a lower methanation activity than cobalt and produces more oxygenates and olefins. This shift 
in the product selectivity is probably related to the lower hydrogenating ability of iron48. Another 
difference between iron and cobalt catalysts is their active phase. While Co-based catalysts are mainly 
metallic under Fischer-Tropsch conditions, Fe-based catalysts can be a mixture of iron carbides and 
oxides247,248. Because iron is an earth-abundant material, it is more easily available than cobalt and 
cheaper. Together with its ability to catalyze the (R)WGS, which makes it more tolerant to feedstocks 
with different H2/CO ratios, it is a subject of intensive interest.  
In this chapter, we will discuss the active phases of Fe-based catalysts under FT conditions. In Fe-based 
catalysts different phases, including iron oxides and iron carbides, develop dynamically248,249. The focus 
will be on recent experimental and theoretical research of FeCx bulk phases and surfaces, in order to 
gain a better understanding of possible active sites. To start, studies of a particle size effect on the 
activity and selectivity of Fe-based catalysts are discussed. 
4.1 Particle size effect 
Motivated by the strong effect of the particle size on the activity and selectivity of cobalt catalysts, 
researchers have compared activity and selectivity for iron particles of different sizes. While cobalt and 
ruthenium nanoparticles exhibit a clear particle size effect, for Fe-based FT catalysts some 
contradictory activity and selectivity trends have been reported. Initial studies in the 1980s for 
unpromoted carbon-supported catalysts by Jung et al. showed that smaller nanoparticles (<1.6 nm) 
exhibited lower TOF, higher O/P ratio and lower CH4 selectivity under 1 atm, 548 K and H2/CO = 3250. 
Jones et al. found similar trends for CH4 selectivity and TOF on iron (carbide) particles (1 atm, 473 K 

73 
 
and H2/CO = 2). However, a higher O/P ratio was observed for larger particles251. This discrepancy in 
O/P ratio is probably linked to the different reaction conditions. 
Experimental work by Mabaso et al. showed that reduced iron oxide particles smaller than 7-9 nm, 
prepared by the reverse micelle technique and supported on carbon or alumina, displayed lower TOF, 
higher methane selectivity and lower chain-growth probability than larger particles (30 bar, 543 K and 
H2/CO = 2)252. However, the results could be clouded by the presence of the potassium promoter and 
the observed effects cannot be completely attributed to a particle size effect. Later studies by Park et 
al. with Fe-based catalysts supported on δ-Al2O3 confirmed these trends253. The TOF increased with 
particle size (2.4 to 6.2 nm) and then remained constant up to 11.5 nm (10 bar, 280-300 °C and H2/CO 
= 2). On smaller reduced FeOx particles, more short-chain hydrocarbons, including CH4, were formed. 
The lower TOF for smaller particles was attributed to the formation of aluminates or silicates, which 
are difficult to reduce and possibly limit the formation of carbides254. 
Torres Galvis et al. investigated both unpromoted and promoted catalysts (2 to 7 nm) for Fischer-
Tropsch to Olefins (FTO)60. To limit metal-support interactions, the catalysts were supported on carbon 
nanofibers (CNF). For the unpromoted catalyst, the catalytic activity, expressed as moles of CO 
converted per gram of iron (FTY), increased with decreasing iron carbide particle size, while methane 
selectivity and olefin selectivity remained unaffected. Iron promoted with sodium and sulfur on the 
other hand displayed a similar increase in activity for decreasing particle size, together with an increase 
in methane selectivity and a decrease in olefin selectivity. These different activity trends in the previous 
studies were attributed to support effects. Using alumina, silica or activated carbon likely resulted in a 
lower degree of carburization. The difference in selectivity for the promoted and unpromoted catalyst 
suggests a promoter effect. It was proposed that corner and edge sites, which are more prevalent on 
small particles, are responsible for the increased activity of smaller nanoparticles. The authors 
proposed that the corner and edge sites are especially active for CH4 formation, while the terraces are 
responsible for olefin formation. It was further suggested that sulfur suppresses methane formation 

74 
 
on terrace sites, but not on edge and corner sites, consistent with the increased CH4 selectivity for 
smaller particles. The increase in the light olefin selectivity for promoted catalysts was attributed to 
the promoting effect of sulfur. 
Follow-up studies by Xie et al. showed a decrease in FTY with particle size for the unpromoted catalyst, 
supported on CNF (Figure 22a)255. The decrease in FTY with particle size can be quite well fitted by a d-
1 curve, suggesting a constant TOF. The increased activity over time can be linked to increased 
carburization (Figure 22a). For the Na- and S-promoted catalyst, an increase of the FTY was observed 
for decreasing particle size (Figure 22b) . An increase in particle size was observed over time (i.e., 
sintering) and was linked to a loss in activity over time. 
a) 
 
b)
 
Figure 22. Catalytic activity as a function of average iron oxide particle size of a) spent unpromoted catalysts (red-outline □) 
the initial period, TOS = 4–10 h, and (red ■) TOS = 100 h b) spent promoted catalysts (blue-outline ◇) at the initial period, 
TOS = 4–10 h, and (blue ◆) at TOS = 100 h at steady state, (340 °C, 20 bar, H2/CO = 1, GHSV = 54 000 h–1). Lines were added 
to guide the eye. The trend line in b) is fitted to y α d–1 for all data points except the first point. Reproduced with permission 
from ref.255, Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. 
In a SSITKA study under methanation conditions, it was shown that the CHx* coverage decreases with 
increasing particle size, and that Na- and S-promoted catalysts have higher CHx* coverages256. Smaller 
particles were proposed to contain more highly active low-coordinated sites, which produce the C-H 
bonds. The TOF was found to be proportional to the CHx* coverage, and this study found that the TOF 
increases for smaller particles and is higher for promoted catalysts. The hydrogen coverage increased 
as well for decreasing particle size, and was lower on the promoted catalyst. Methane selectivity was 

75 
 
proportional to the hydrogen coverage, and light olefin selectivity was inversely proportional to the 
hydrogen coverage. This suggests that both the promoter and the particle size affect the activity and 
selectivity by adjusting both the CHx* and the H* coverage. Studies of the particle size effect on iron 
are somewhat less conclusive due to the complex phase behavior of Fe catalysts under FT conditions. 
 
 

76 
 
 
4.2 Active phases: carbides and oxides 
4.2.1 Bulk phases 
Already since the earliest reports of FTS, it has been observed that the catalyst consists of a mixture of 
iron oxides, metallic iron and iron carbides under catalytic conditions248,257-263. Iron carbides are often 
reported to be the active phase during FTS47,263. The search for the phases which are present under FT 
conditions is still very actual. Paalanen et al. recently identified different iron carbides under FT 
conditions using Mössbauer absorption spectroscopy and X-ray powder diffractometry264. 
Additionally, they calculated the formation temperatures at which the carbide phases are stable. To 
have consistent naming throughout this review, we use the following bold notations for the iron 
carbides47: 
• 
hexagonal ε-Fe3C, P6322, 
• 
hexagonal ε-Fe2C, P63/mmc, 
• 
hexagonal ε’-Fe2.2C, P63/mmc, 
• 
orthorhombic η-Fe2C, Pnnm, 
• 
monoclinic χ-Fe5C2, C2/c (the Hägg carbide), 
• 
orthorhombic θ-Fe3C, Pnma (cementite), 
• 
orthorhombic θ-Fe7C3, Pnma (the Eckström-Adcock carbide). 

77 
 
 
Figure 23. Structures of the triangular prismatic (TP) θ-Fe3C and χ-Fe5C2 carbides, and unit cells of the octahedral (O) ε-Fe2C 
carbides. Fe: orange, C: gray. Reproduced with permission from ref.50, Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society.  
The iron carbides can be classified according to their coordination pattern, i.e., the sites occupied by 
the interstitial carbons248. θ-Fe3C, χ-Fe5C2 and θ-Fe7C3 belong to the class of trigonal/triangular 
prismatic (TP) carbides, while ε-Fe3C, ε-Fe2C, ε’-Fe2.2C and η-Fe2C belong to the class of octahedral (O) 
carbides. For the former, the carbon atoms occupy the triangular prismatic interstices and for the latter 
the octahedral interstices are occupied (Figure 23). A review by Paalanen and Weckhuysen suggested 
that the ε-Fe2C (with space group P63/mmc)259 and ε-Fe3C (with space group P6322)265 are the same 
carbide and the different stoichiometry was attributed to an inaccurate determination47. Here, ε-Fe2C 
will be used to avoid confusion, as it is more often used in literature, despite the more accurate naming 
of ε-Fe3C. A difference between the η-Fe2C, proposed by Hirotsu and Nagakura266, and ε-Fe2C (or ε-
Fe3C) structures can be seen in Figure 24. ε-Fe2C and η-Fe2C have similar crystal structures, but ε-Fe2C 
has some empty octahedral interstitial sites. 

78 
 
a)
 
b) 
 
Figure 24. a) Hexagonal ε-Fe2C (or ε-Fe3C) viewed along the c-axis259,265, b) Orthorhombic η-Fe2C viewed along the b-axis266. 
The unit cells are indicated in teal, dashed lines. Fe: orange, C: gray.  
The calcined Fe-based FT catalyst is a mix of oxide phases (α-Fe2O3/Hematite, Fe3O4/Magnetite, 
FeO/Wüstite)248. Upon reduction with H2 or CO, metallic α-Fe is formed. During FTS, carburization 
occurs47. For reduction with H2, temperatures above 350 °C are required267. Above 570 °C, a two-step 
α-Fe2O3 → Fe3O4 → Fe reduction is observed; at lower temperatures the intermediate formation of FeO 
(Wüstite) is observed267. During reduction with CO, carburization can occur at the same time. The most 
stable carbide phase (θ-Fe3C) is only stable below 500 °C47. Reduction with CO above 500 °C will thus 
lead to the formation of metallic iron -no iron carbides will be formed- but below 500 °C, carburization 
can occur47.  
The (partly) reduced Fe can be carburized to FeCx by (H2/)CO and carburization ability of the reduced 
iron oxides and of metallic iron was found to be Fe > FeO > Fe3O4268. The iron carbides and Fe(II) oxides 
are proposed to reach a balanced state during FTS, with increasing amounts of FeCx on the surface. 
The unreduced catalyst can also be directly exposed to the reaction mixture (H2/CO)263,269,270. This 
results in the simultaneous formation of metallic iron and iron carbides. 
Riedel et al. investigated the phase evolution of the iron catalyst under FT conditions271. Several 
“episodes of formation”, related to compositional and structural changes of the catalyst, each with 
their own kinetic regimes, were observed, i.e., different reactions (carbon deposition, RWGS, FT) were 
dominating during each episode due to the different catalyst composition. Aside from iron carbides, 

79 
 
other forms of carbon were observed as well, i.e., atomic carbon, amorphous hydrocarbons and 
graphitic carbon were found under reaction conditions272. The strong correlation between the amount 
of atomic carbon and the catalytic activity suggests the importance of surface atomic carbon in Fe-
based FTS. 
The complex phase evolution of iron carbides makes it difficult to identify the active phases under FT 
conditions. Due to the presence of carbon in the iron lattice, the thermodynamic stability of the 
different iron carbide active phases is sensitive to the carbon chemical potential (μC), which depends 
on the temperature and the gas-phase composition under FT conditions (e.g., CO, H2, C2H4). For 
example: 
 
𝜇𝐶 =  2𝜇𝐶𝑂 − 𝜇𝐶𝑂2  
 (2CO → C + CO2) 
(9) 
 
𝜇𝐶 = 𝜇𝐶𝑂 + 𝜇𝐻2  − 𝜇𝐻2𝑂 (CO + H2 → C + H2O) 
(10) 
 
𝜇𝐶 = 
1
2 𝜇𝐶2𝐻4  − 𝜇𝐻2   (½C2H4 → C + H2) 
(11) 
Using statistical thermodynamics, the chemical potentials of CO, H2, H2O, CO2 and C2H4 can be 
calculated, from which the different carbon chemical potentials are then obtained. Reaction (9) gives 
high carbon chemical potentials around -6.4 eV, reaction (10) carbon chemical potentials around -6.8 
eV and reaction (11) low carbon chemical potentials around -7.4 eV. DFT calculations can help to 
evaluate the stability and reactivity of the different structures. De Smit et al. performed a set of DFT 
calculations for the different carbide phases and found that the enthalpy of formation of various 
carbide phases is very close (i.e., within ~0.1 eV/C atom) under FT conditions270. The ε-Fe2C, ε’-Fe2.2C 
and η-Fe2C were found to be the most stable carbides, but in experiments θ-Fe3C, χ-Fe5C2 and θ-Fe7C3 
are more commonly observed. It was suggested that the formation of ε-Fe2C, ε’-Fe2.2C and η-Fe2C 
carbides is entropically and kinetically less favorable. The lower deformation energies, the lower 
carbon diffusion limitations, and the lower vibrational entropy of θ-Fe3C, χ-Fe5C2 and θ-Fe7C3 compared 
to ε-Fe2C, ε’-Fe2.2C and η-Fe2C were suggested as reasons for the observation of θ-Fe3C, χ-Fe5C2 and θ-
Fe7C3 carbides in experiments. Assuming the formation of ε-Fe2C, ε’-Fe2.2C and η-Fe2C is inhibited, the 

80 
 
formation of the three trigonal prismatic (TP) carbides (θ-Fe3C, χ-Fe5C2 and θ-Fe7C3) is highly dependent 
on μC. DFT calculations suggest that at high temperatures (>623 K) in pure CO (low μC), θ-Fe3C is 
formed, while at lower temperatures and/or higher CO pressures, χ-Fe5C2 is more stable. At very low 
temperatures, θ-Fe7C3 becomes the most stable phase. It is likely that the formation of θ-Fe7C3 is 
limited by competition with the formation of ε-carbides. The influence of the carbon chemical potential 
was then tested experimentally in the pretreatment of the catalyst. By changing μC of the 
pretreatment, the carbide phase could be tuned. A catalyst pretreated at low μC contained θ-Fe3C, χ-
Fe5C2 and amorphous FexC, while a catalyst formed at higher μC contained mainly χ-Fe5C2. Upon further 
increasing μC, ε-carbides were formed. For the catalyst pretreated at low μC, a phase transformation to 
χ-Fe5C2 was observed during FTS.  
This kinetic limitation in the formation of ε(’)-carbides was further investigated by Chai et al.273 By 
adjusting the H2/CO ratio, they could control the formation of different carbide phases. At low H2/CO 
ratios, χ-Fe5C2 is preferably formed. When the ratio is increased, O* removal via H2O is faster and more 
vacancies are formed where CO dissociation can happen. This increases the C* coverage and results in 
a higher carburization rate, which leads to the formation of the more carbon-rich ε(’)-carbide. 
Recently, the group of Liu computed the relative stability of different FeCx phases50. Using a machine 
learning potential and a stochastic surface walking (SSW) global optimization, they explored the 
potential energy surface efficiently and constructed the convex hull for the different carbide phases at 
different μC (Figure 25). μC = -6.6 eV corresponds to the low conversion limit with only CO, H2 and H2O 
present in the surrounding gas mixture (CO + H2 → C + H2O). When conversion increases, the carbon 
chemical potential decreases to -7.43 eV since the gas mixture at high conversion consists of ethylene 
and hydrogen (½C2H4 → C + H2). At this higher conversion (μC = -7.43 eV), the convex hull diagram 
shows that carbides with a high Fe:C ratio are no longer convex points, and the most stable phase 
becomes η-Fe2C. This phase is only stable by ∆Gf = -0.01 eV/C atom, suggesting that at lower carbon 

81 
 
chemical potentials, the carbides will transform to the metallic iron and graphite. A new carbide phase, 
θ-Fe7C3-II, with an energy very similar to θ-Fe7C3, was discovered by the simulation (Figure 25b). 
a) 
 
b) 
 
Figure 25. a) Thermodynamic convex hull diagram for bulk FeCx with respect to BCC Fe at μC = −6.60 eV, i.e., low conversion. 
(b) Structures of θ-Fe7C3-II (Pnma, a = 4.96 Å, b = 16.19 Å, c = 4.54 Å) and θ-Fe7C3 (Pnma, a = 4.51 Å, b = 6.84 Å, c = 11.74 Å). 
Fe: orange, C: gray. Different layers of Fe atoms in θ-Fe7C3-II and θ -Fe7C3 are labeled with different colors (yellow, blue, green, 
red, cyan, and purple). Reproduced with permission from ref.50, Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society. 
4.2.2 Surfaces 
Next, we discuss the surface structure of the different iron carbides. Experimentally identifying the 
surface structure of Fe-based catalysts is quite complicated, due to the structural similarity of different 
surfaces274. Additionally, it is difficult to reconcile results obtained from UHV surface characterization 
studies (<1 mbar) with FT conditions (>10 bar). In TEM studies under FT conditions, lattice fringes 
corresponding to χ-Fe5C2(510), θ-Fe3C(111) and ε-Fe2C(101) have been observed275-277, but the focus in 
this section will be on theoretical studies.  
To create a surface, the bulk carbide structure needs to be cut. The resulting slab can be symmetric, 
i.e., have the same surface structure for the top and bottom layer, but this is not always possible. In 
addition, in some cases, e.g., for carbon-terminated surfaces, the Fe:C ratio of the slab differs from the 
bulk ratio. The stability of such non-stoichiometric slabs is a function of the carbon chemical 
potential278. The surface energy equation should then be balanced with the chemical potential of 
carbon to account for the non-stoichiometric surface: γ= (∆Gs-∆Gb-xμC) 2A
⁄
 with ∆Gs and ∆Gb the 

82 
 
Gibbs free energy of respectively the surface slab and the bulk phase, while x is the number of 
additional/removed carbon atoms, compared to the stoichiometric slab, and A the surface area.  
Figure 26 shows how different surface terminations can be created279. By taking the origin where the 
two vectors describing the surface plane cross, and using fractional distances of the unit cell, a 
consistent naming for the different surfaces can be maintained.  
Initial studies focused on stoichiometric and symmetric slabs. Sorescu suggested that asymmetric 
surfaces can have low surface energies280, however, for asymmetric slabs the calculated surface energy 
is the average for the top and the bottom surface termination.  
 
Figure 26. Illustration of the notation for the different surface terminations of the (001) surface. The subscript indicates the 
fractional distance from the origin. Reproduced with permission from ref.279, Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society 
under the CC BY-NC-ND 4.0 license (https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/). 
A systematic DFT study by Steynberg et al. investigated all symmetric and stoichiometric low Miller 
index surfaces of χ-Fe5C2281. They found that the (010)0.25 surface was the most stable, and the (101)0.0 
surface was the least stable. The relative surface energies of different surfaces were however very 
close, which suggest that different surface terminations might be present under FT conditions. Broos 
et al. constructed Wulff equilibrium shapes using several low-index χ-Fe5C2 surfaces, but included 
several low surface energy asymmetric surface facets in the construction (i.e., (100)0.0 and (100)0.287)279. 
These two surfaces exhibit surface topologies very similar to the B5 sites discussed for Co and Ru 
(Section 2.2). When constructing the Wulff particle, only the most stable Miller index plane was 

83 
 
considered. For example, the (111̅)0.5 and (100)0.287 surfaces were not taken into consideration, but for 
certain chemical potentials they become more stable. The (010)0.25, (111̅)0.0, and (100)0.0 facets made 
up nearly two thirds of the surface of the Wulff shape. Previous studies did not consider surfaces with 
high Miller indices, which can however also be relatively stable. Pham et al. considered some high 
Miller index surfaces and in that study, the χ-Fe5C2(510) facet was the most prominent facet in the 
Wulff shape282. 
The stability of the low-index facets of θ-Fe3C ((110), (01̅1), (101), (010), (111), (001), (011), (100) and 
(11̅1)), cleaved at the origin, was investigated by Broos et al.283 The (110), (01̅1), (101) surfaces 
occupied almost 60% of the Wulff particle, while the (111) and (010) surfaces occupied more than 20% 
of the surface. For the high-index surfaces, the θ-Fe3C(031) surface was found to be the most stable, 
taking up almost 30% of the surface284. 
By combining in situ XPS and ab initio thermodynamics, de Smit et al. found that the C-rich χ-Fe5C2(100) 
facet is dominant at low temperatures (high μC), while BCC Fe and (sub)surface carbon are more stable 
at higher temperature (low μC)285. Zhao et al. studied different bulk-truncated surfaces of the most 
common carbides (χ-Fe5C2, ε-Fe2C, θ-Fe3C, Fe4C) for various carbon chemical potential (μC)286,287. By 
varying μC, different reaction conditions can be simulated and the Wulff equilibrium shape can be 
constructed. For example, a pure CO environment promotes the formation of C-rich surfaces, while an 
increasing H2 concentration decreases μC and favors C-poor surfaces (Figure 27). The carburization 
ability of a gas hence depends on its carbon content. Low temperature and low pressure can lead to 
C-rich surfaces as well. The Fe4C(100), θ-Fe3C(010), χ-Fe5C2(100) and ε-Fe2C(12̅1) were calculated to be 
the most stable at μC = -7.1 eV. Yin et al. constructed a Wulff particle at 1 atm, 550 K and 
PH2/PCO = 2.5288. They found χ-Fe5C2(111), χ-Fe5C2(100) and χ-Fe5C2(111̅) to make up more than 50% of 
the surface. 

84 
 
 
Figure 27. The morphology of an Fe-based FTS catalyst changes with reaction conditions (varying μC). Adapted from Zhao et 
al.287 
By combining a stochastic surface walking (SSW) algorithm with a neural network potential, Liu et al. 
found the most stable iron carbide surfaces from millions of structure candidates50. The most stable 
structures not only contain bulk truncated structures, but also include surface geometries with 
additional C atoms. Such surface reconstructions lead to surface Fe:C ratios around 2 or slightly below, 
suggesting some surface reconstruction occurs to reach this low ratio (Figure 28a). For χ-Fe5C2(510), 
extra C atoms occupy the 4-fold sites, resulting in the sinking of adjacent C atoms. On the θ-Fe7C3-
II(010) surface, C atoms were calculated to pop up to create a more C-rich surface, while on the η-
Fe2C(111) surface, the C atom moves from a 3-fold hollow site to the vacant 4-fold site. The limited 
stability of surface Fe atoms makes it unlikely that B5 sites, consisting of only Fe atoms, are formed 
under FT conditions. The most stable surfaces at μC = -6.9 eV are θ-Fe3C(010), χ-Fe5C2(100), θ-Fe7C3-
II(010), η-Fe2C(011), η-Fe2C(110), χ-Fe5C2(510), η-Fe2C(111) and χ-Fe5C2(111) (Figure 28b). The χ-
Fe5C2(510) surface resembles a clock-reconstructed surface, similar to the Co2C clock-reconstruction 
modeled by Nandula et al.145 and more importantly, it resembles the active site for CO activation at 
the edges of the cobalt nano-islands proposed by Banerjee et al. (Figure 9)142. Interestingly, Liu et al. 
found that the most stable FeCx surfaces mainly have Fe-C coordination numbers (CN) between 2.2 
and 3, which were suggested by Wang et al. to be optimal for FTS274,289. 

85 
 
 
Figure 28. a) Surface reconstruction of some of the most stable surface facets (side view). b) Top views of the 8 most stable 
surfaces (θ-Fe3C(010), θ-Fe7C3-II(010), and η-Fe2C(011) are the same as χ-Fe5C2(100)). The green dotted lines label the 3-fold 
hollow sites on these surfaces. Fe: orange, C: gray (upper) and yellow (lower). Reproduced with permission from ref.50, 
Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society. 
Experimental identification of the surface structure of Fe-based catalysts has been challenging. 
Recently, Li et al. prepared flat model surfaces290. STM studies at low pressures showed that iron 
carbide layers can be easily formed from Fe/Au(111) films by ethylene decomposition and C insertion 
in the metal Fe interstices. The carbide film was however unreactive towards hydrogenation. The low 
C content of the carbide was proposed to explain the low reactivity. Subsequent exposure to CO at low 
temperatures (<300 K) increased the ‘Fe oxide’ and ‘Fe carbide’ peak in the XPS spectra, suggesting 
that CO activation occurs at low temperatures on the carbide surface, as opposed to metallic Fe films. 
It was suggested that at higher pressures, the additional C atoms resulting from CO dissociation may 
act as active species and boost C-C coupling. 
While a lot of progress has been made in creating a molecular picture of the catalytic surface structure 
for pure Fe carbide phases, the Fe-FT catalyst under reaction conditions is likely even more complex. 

86 
 
Experimental TEM studies by Yang et al. suggest the formation of a core-shell structure, with a carbide 
core and an amorphous graphitic carbon shell275. XPS spectra confirmed the presence of both Fe3O4 
and χ-Fe5C2. Graphitic carbon was also observed using Raman spectroscopy by de Smit et al., who 
found that, depending on the pretreatment procedure, the amount of graphitic carbon increased 
during FTS270. When the catalyst was pretreated at low μC (623 K in 1% CO/H2), significant amounts of 
graphitic carbon were formed, while at high μC (553 K in pure CO) no additional graphitic carbon was 
formed. More recently, Raman spectroscopy measurements by Paalanen et al. suggested the 
formation of carbon-nanofiber-like carbon deposits on a Fe-based catalysts during Fischer-Tropsch 
synthesis291.  
All the previous studies focused on clean iron carbide surfaces. Since CO adsorbs dissociatively on these 
surfaces, low CO* coverages are expected256. This is markedly different from cobalt and ruthenium. 
Low coverages have been observed for other reaction intermediates as well, except for C* and O*.  
In an earlier study by de Smit et al., the nanoparticle and bulk iron oxide were exposed to different 
conditions and investigated by in situ XPS, showing the formation of carbonates, atomic carbon and 
hydroxyls on the catalyst surface285. These experiments further illustrate the complexity of the Fe-
based catalyst surface. 
Modeling of the nanoparticles combined with experimental studies can help in understanding the 
surface and bulk structure. New techniques, such as machine learning potentials employed by Liu et 
al., seem promising to begin to elucidate the catalyst structure. Recently, Niu et al. showed that an 
Fe5C2@Fe3O4 catalyst is formed from an iron nanoparticle upon exposure to a CO atmosphere292. NAP-
XPS and environmental TEM (ETEM) showed that carbon diffuses into the core of the particle to form 
a carbide, while oxygen oxidizes the shell. ReaxFF MD simulations for a ~5 nm iron particle surrounded 
by CO molecules showed that, after CO dissociation, carbon diffuses into the particle, while oxygen 
remains near the surface. The presence of oxides on the surface will lead to the formation of CO2 via 

87 
 
CO oxidation. By varying the H2 amount, the oxide layer thickness could be regulated, which in turn 
changed the initial CO2 selectivity. 
The stability of different surface phases is strongly dependent on the carbon chemical potential and 
the conditions of the pretreatment procedure determine the surface composition and hence the 
reactivity. An overview of the different stable surface facets that have been reported for varying 
carbon chemical potentials is given in Table 4.

4.3 FT activity 
The Hägg carbide (χ-Fe5C2) is one of the main active phases in the Fe-based FT catalyst and it has been 
reported to have a high selectivity to C5+ hydrocarbons270,275. Other phases such as θ-Fe3C and ε'-Fe2.2C, 
have also been proposed as the active phase273,283,296. An account of experimental studies is presented 
here to identify the most important phases during Fischer-Tropsch synthesis. 
De Smit et al. found that a catalyst pretreated at high μC conditions contained more χ-Fe5C2 than a 
catalyst treated at low μC conditions270. The former catalyst (high μC) exhibited a high activity and 
selectivity in the FT reaction, while the latter (low μC) was more susceptible to the build-up of 
carbonaceous species. The high selectivity towards carbonaceous species was attributed to the 
metallic character of the θ-Fe3C phase. Herranz et al. also pretreated catalysts under different 
atmospheres and found χ-Fe5C2 to be more active than θ-Fe3C269. Similarly, Yang et al. synthesized pure 
χ-Fe5C2 nanoparticles and found this catalyst to perform a lot better than the conventional catalyst 
reduced from Fe2O3275. More recently, Zhao et al. managed to synthesize FeCx nanoparticles of ~18 nm 
with different carbide phases297. They found χ-Fe5C2 to be the most active phase, while the α-Fe and θ-
Fe7C3 catalysts were transformed into χ-Fe5C2 under reaction conditions and their final activity was 
close to that of the χ-Fe5C2 catalyst. Carburization during LTFT of rapidly quenched skeletal iron, 
featuring nanocrystalline dimensions and low coordination numbers, formed the ε-Fe2C phase298. Xu 
et al. found that ε-Fe2C was more active than χ-Fe5C2, and showed a high C5+ selectivity of 73% at 423 
K. The difference in the activity for the ε-Fe2C phase reported by Xu et al. and by Zhao et al. likely 
results from differences in particle size, reaction conditions and preparation method274. Interestingly, 
Chang et al. found the activity for χ-Fe5C2 and ε-Fe2C to be very similar, while a higher activity was 
attributed to the θ-Fe7C3 phase276. The ε-Fe2C phase on the other hand exhibited the lowest CH4 
selectivity and the highest C5+ selectivity. More recently, Lu et al. suggested ε-Fe2C as the main active 
phase during FT, with a higher activity than χ-Fe5C2 and θ-Fe3C299. 

89 
 
Despite the strong support for iron carbides as the active phase, Yang et al. investigated the possibility 
of iron oxide as the active phase for the FT reaction300. Scanning transmission electron microscopy 
(STEM) and X-ray absorption fine structure (XAS) showed sub-nanometer iron oxide clusters to be 
stable and active in FTS. In particular, finely dispersed iron oxide clusters consisting of partly reduced 
Feδ+ (δ=2.6-2.9) species deposited on ceria nanorods were reported to be active in FTS. The activity 
was associated with the local coordination of the active sites. 
While iron carbides seem to be responsible for the FT activity, the active phase responsible for the 
WGS activity of Fe-based FT catalysts is not yet identified301. Since it is well known that magnetite 
(Fe3O4) catalyzes the WGS reaction302, some suggest that iron oxides are responsible for WGS activity 
during the FT reaction. Others propose that the formation of magnetite, through oxidation of the 
carbide phase by CO2 or H2O303,304, is responsible for the deactivation of FT catalysts305.  
 
 

90 
 
5 Molecular views on the mechanisms of Fe-based CO-FT 
5.1 Experimental kinetic studies 
Early kinetic studies of promoted Fe-based catalysts resulted in power law rate equations and LHHW 
models. In 1984, Huff and Satterfield proposed to include a water inhibition term306: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
2
𝑝𝐻2𝑂+ 𝛽𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
 
(12) 
Van Steen and Schulz obtained the following rate equation, assuming a similar mechanism for Fe- and 
Co-based catalysts185: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼(𝑝𝐻2
1.5𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2𝑂
⁄
)
(1 + 𝛽(𝑝𝐻2𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2𝑂
⁄
))
2 
(13) 
Based on a review of the kinetic literature, Botes suggested that the effect of H2O is most likely related 
to the WGS activity of Fe-based catalysts and that the assumption that CO2 or H2O inhibits the FT 
reaction is incorrect307. This resulted in the following rate equation for the FT reaction, with no H2O or 
CO2 inhibiting terms: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂
0.5𝑝𝐻2
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 
(14) 
Ojeda et al. also investigated the kinetics of the FT reaction on Co- and Fe-based catalysts187. Their rate 
equation, based on the formation of the CH2* monomer as the rate-determining step, is similar to rate 
equations for cobalt180,240: 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 
(15) 
The high activation energy for direct CO dissociation on metallic Fe(110) (189 kJ/mol), led Ojeda et al. 
to propose the H-assisted CO dissociation path (via an HCOH* intermediate) also on Fe-based 
catalysts187. To experimentally discriminate between direct and H-assisted CO dissociation, the oxygen 

91 
 
rejection pathways were compared. In the direct CO dissociation pathway, O* can form either H2O or 
CO2 under FT conditions. In the H-assisted pathway, OH* is proposed to only form H2O. This led to the 
same rate equation as for Co-based catalysts, which accounts for H2O formation (left term) and CO2 
formation (right term): 
 
𝑟 ≈
𝛼𝑝𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐻2
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 +  
𝛽𝑝𝐶𝑂
(1 + 𝐾𝐶𝑂𝑝𝐶𝑂)2 
(16) 
The H2O/CO2 ratio is then indicative of the pathway that is followed. In experiments, a high H2O/CO2 
ratio was found under industrially relevant conditions (>0.2 MPa), suggesting that the H-assisted route 
is also dominant on Fe-based catalysts. On Fe-based catalysts, the WGS reaction is active, which could 
significantly impact the H2O/CO2 ratio. In a follow-up study, an inverse kinetic isotope effect was found 
for the FTS, supporting the proposed prevalence of the H-assisted route for Fe-based catalysts213. Most 
kinetic studies report apparent activation barriers between 80 and 105 kJ/mol240. Reaction orders in 
the range of 0.6 to 1.4 for H2 and -0.4 to +0.5 for CO have been measured308. 
Mechanistic SSITKA studies were also performed, although the number is smaller than for Co-based 
Fischer-Tropsch. Govender et al. proposed two carbon pools in the formation of methane, which could 
also contribute to the formation of C2+ hydrocarbons309,310. They also suggested that olefins and 
paraffins were formed on different catalytic sites or do not share a surface intermediate. Isotopic 
surface 13C atoms, deposited via the Boudouard reaction, were suggested to participate in the reaction, 
with CCH* as the dominant surface intermediate on the carburized iron catalyst311. 
Chai et al. investigated the involvement of Mars-van Krevelen mechanisms in Fe-based FT and 
proposed two main CH4 formation pathways after initial carburization of the iron catalyst312. The slow 
reaction pathway, accounting for 10% of the total activity, goes via a Mars-van Krevelen mechanism 
on sites covering 90% of the surface. The fast reaction pathway follows a Langmuir-Hinshelwood 
mechanism on sites that only cover 10% of the surface. Isotope-labeling studies suggest that bulk 

92 
 
carbon atoms can participate in the FT reaction by diffusing to the surface and that the two outer layers 
of the Fe-based catalyst participate in the reaction. 
While initial LHHW models assumed metallic iron as the active phase, more recent studies tend to 
support a Mars-van Krevelen mechanism for CO activation on FeCx catalysts313. We see opportunities 
to develop kinetic models that account for these recent mechanistic insights. 
5.2 CO activation 
The comparable stability of different iron carbide phases during FTS, each with different possible 
surface terminations, has motivated numerous DFT studies of CO activation on all these different 
facets. At each of these types of sites, direct, H-assisted and CO-insertion mechanisms have been 
considered (Figure 29). Despite the diversity of possible surfaces for these three carbides, recent 
studies suggest some sort of convergence towards a common surface active site geometry50. In the 
next sections, we give an overview of DFT studies that have focused on CO activation. This leads to 
many options and the choice of the surface in these calculations is not always fully rationalized. 

93 
 
 
Figure 29. In Fe-based FT, mainly θ-Fe3C and χ-Fe5C2 have been suggested as active bulk phases under FT conditions. 
Subsequent surface analysis proposed a range of stable surfaces. In the search for the active sites, different CO dissociation 
mechanisms were evaluated on the most stable surface.  
Initial DFT studies were performed for metallic BCC iron surfaces, and the most important findings are 
summarized here. Similar to the low CO activation barriers at the kink sites of Co surfaces143, Sorescu 
found relatively low barriers of 64-87 kJ/mol for CO dissociation on the kinked Fe(710) and Fe(310) 
surfaces314. The high barriers for direct CO dissociation on close-packed Fe(110) (189 kJ/mol) led Ojeda 
et al. to propose a H-assisted CO dissociation path via a HCOH* intermediate187. DFT calculations on 
Fe(110) covered with 0.5 ML CO show that the barriers for the H-assisted dissociation pathway are 
significantly lower than for the direct CO dissociation pathway. On the stepped Fe(111) surface, direct 
CO dissociation and H-assisted CO dissociation via HCO* have similar activation barriers of about 165 
kJ/mol, while further hydrogenation to H2CO* and HCOH* is thermodynamically and kinetically 
unfavorable315. Further comparison between H-assisted and direct CO dissociation pathways on 
metallic iron has been performed for Fe(100)316. Here, direct dissociation has a lower barrier than H-
