assisted dissociation (via HCO*), but the latter is expected to dominate under high-coverage conditions 
due to the absence of free sites. On the Fe(310) surface, Elahifard et al. compared both routes and 

94 
 
concluded that, due to the endothermicity of HCO* and COH* formation, the direct route is the most 
viable317. Wang et al. found molecular CO adsorption to be possible at higher CO* coverages, while CO 
adsorbs dissociatively at low CO* coverages318. At medium coverages, both molecular CO adsorption 
and dissociative CO adsorption occur. 
First DFT studies for CO adsorption and dissociation on Hägg carbide (χ-Fe5C2) were reported by 
Sorescu280. The low Miller index surfaces proposed by Steynberg et al.281 were considered ((010)0.25, 
(111̅)0.0, (110)0.0, (111)0.0, (111̅)0.5, (110)0.5, and (100)0.0)). Most of these surfaces are symmetric and 
stoichiometric, only the (100)0.0 is asymmetric, but it was included because it has a low surface energy. 
Three types of active sites were distinguished based on the atom to which CO is bound: Fe sites, Fe-C 
sites and C sites. While CO adsorption on the Fe-site was the most stable (>180 kJ/mol), the CO 
dissociation barrier on mixed Fe-C sites was significantly lower. Barriers as low as 65 kJ/mol were 
calculated on the (111̅)0.00 surface when CO was bound to 3 Fe atoms and 1 C atom (Figure 30). This is 
not the most stable CO adsorption site on this surface, and the barrier increases to 153 kJ/mol when 
it is referenced to the most stable CO adsorption site on this surface. 
IS 
TS 
FS 
 
 
 
Figure 30. CO dissociation on the (111̅)0.00 surface, showing the initial state (IS, 3Fe-1C configuration), transition state (TS) and 
final state. Fe: orange, C: green, O: red. Adapted with permission from ref.280, Copyright 2009 American Chemical Society. 
Based on DFT calculations for CO hydrogenation on χ-Fe5C2(100)-0.05, Gracia et al. proposed that 
methane is formed via a Mars-van Krevelen mechanism on iron carbides313. The active participation of 
Fe carbides in the FT reaction has a long history that goes back to the original work of Fischer and 
Tropsch2. In 1948, Kummer et al. for example demonstrated that the carbide phase actively 
participates in the FT reaction and that the FT mechanism occurs via a carbide intermediate319. In the 

95 
 
molecular mechanism proposed by Gracia et al., surface C (Cs) atoms actively participate in the 
mechanism. After hydrogenation of the surface carbon, a C vacancy is formed where CO adsorbs 
(Figure 31). Subsequent CO activation occurs via a COH* intermediate, in contrast to the commonly 
preferred HCO* route. χ-Fe5C2(001) was investigated as a possible surface for CO activation by Cao et 
al.320 To refill the C vacancies, they suggested a formyl (HCO*) pathway and HC-O dissociation. 
Including the endothermicity of HCO* formation however increases the overall barrier for this route 
from 112 to 206 kJ/mol. 
 
Figure 31. Scheme of the Mars-van Krevelen (MvK) mechanism for Fischer-Tropsch synthesis on χ-Fe5C2(100)-0.05. Adapted 
with 
permission 
from 
ref.313, 
Copyright 
2009 
Springer 
Nature 
under 
the 
CC 
BY-NC 
2.0 
license 
(https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/2.0). 
A catalytic cycle between a C-rich (or C-saturated) surface where surface carbon hydrogenation is 
preferred, and a defect (C-poor) surface where CO activation restores the carbide structure, was also 
proposed by Niemantsverdriet and Ozbek (Figure 32)321. The carbon-terminated χ-Fe5C2(001)−0.05 was 
used to represent a surface with maximum carbon content. When individual surface carbon atoms are 
hydrogenated or react with CO, C vacancies are created. On this partially vacant carbide surface, H-
assisted and direct CO dissociation are energetically feasible. It was further suggested that 
hydrogenation of CO* to HCO* can lead to oxygenates, H2CO. Both on the partially vacant surface and 

96 
 
on the C-saturated surface, surface carbon hydrogenation is the preferred pathway. Only when the 
surface is almost C-free, direct dissociation becomes favored, and the C vacancies are again filled.  
 
Figure 32. Scheme of the MvK mechanism on FeCx including the intermediate formation of metallic iron and recarburization 
to vacant FeCx. Adapted with permission from ref.321, Copyright 2014 Elsevier. 
Since previous work suggested that θ-Fe3C(001)293, η-Fe2C(011)294, Fe4C(100)295 and χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25281 
are the most stable low index surfaces of the respective carbides (Figure 33), Huo et al. proposed to 
investigate these surfaces for direct CO dissociation322. They suggested that surface C atoms are 
hydrogenated to CH4 (or hydrocarbons) to create C vacancies. While the saturated FeCx surface does 
not dissociate CO easily, these newly created C vacancies provide a low energy pathway for CO 
activation. CO adsorption is somewhat enhanced on these sites, but the CO dissociation barriers are 
reduced from more than 250 kJ/mol on the saturated FeCx surface to less than 100 kJ/mol for the 
surface containing C vacancies. CO dissociation barriers at the C vacancy sites for θ-Fe3C(001), η-
Fe2C(011), Fe4C(100) and χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25 are respectively 88, 81, 90 and 103 kJ/mol. The CH4 formation 
barriers, i.e., the vacancy formation barriers, for θ-Fe3C(001), η-Fe2C(011), Fe4C(100) and χ-
Fe5C2(010)0.25 are 191, 131, 187 and 149 kJ/mol, respectively, suggesting that η-Fe2C(011) and χ-
Fe5C2(010)0.25 surfaces more easily form C vacancies. 

97 
 
 
Figure 33. Top and side view of the a) η-Fe2C(011), b) χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25 c) θ-Fe3C(001), d) Fe4C(100). Reproduced from ref.322, 
Copyright 2009 American Chemical Society. 
Huo et al. also found a linear relationship between the reaction energy to hydrogenate surface carbon 
to CH4 and create a C vacancy and the charge on the surface C atom (Figure 34)322. This relation could 
help identify promising surfaces for CH4 formation (and C vacancy formation), as well as potential 
promoters. 
 
Figure 34. Correlation between the reaction enthalpy (ΔEr) of CH4 formation and Mulliken charge (q) of the surface Csurf atom. 
Adapted with permission from ref.322, Copyright 2009 American Chemical Society. 

98 
 
Petersen et al. also investigated CO activation on the χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25 surface323. Barriers for direct and 
H-assisted CO dissociation (via HCO*) on C vacancies of 137 and 136 kJ/mol, respectively, were 
calculated. Interestingly, barriers for CO activation on non-vacancy sites were only 10 kJ/mol higher. 
In an earlier study, Petersen et al. investigated CO activation at step sites of various χ-Fe5C2 surfaces324. 
The barrier for CO activation on step-like sites of χ-Fe5C2(110)0.0 and χ-Fe5C2(110)0.8 was 30 - 60 kJ/mol 
lower than on Fe5C2(010)0.25, possibly due to the pre-activated adsorbed state of the CO molecule at 
the step-like sites. 
Using Wulff equilibrium shapes, Pham et al. identified χ-Fe5C2(510) as the dominant surface 
termination282. On this terrace-like surface, direct CO dissociation was favored with a barrier of 127 
kJ/mol, while H-assisted pathways were calculated to be preferred on the step-like χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25, χ-
Fe5C2(100)-0.05 and χ-Fe5C2(001)313,320,322. More recently, Broos et al. compared not only the direct and 
H-assisted CO dissociation pathways but also considered C-assisted pathways (CO-insertion) in a 
comprehensive study considering different surface facets of the Hägg carbide279. In C-assisted 
pathways, a C atom is coupled to CO (possibly followed by hydrogenation steps) prior to C-O bond 
dissociation. The most stable surfaces, including (010)0.25, (111̅)0.0, (100)0.0, (111̅)0.5 and (100)0.287, were 
considered for CO activation. Kinetic models predict the highest rate for the (111̅)0.5 surface, followed 
by the (100)0.0 (Figure 35). Only on the (010)0.25 surface, the H-assisted activation is preferred. It is 
interesting to note that high Miller index surfaces like (510)0.0 favor the direct pathway (Figure 35)282. 
Both (010)0.25 and (510)0.0 are terrace-like facets and mainly differ in their distribution of carbon atoms, 
which seems to affect the CO activation routes279. When B5-type sites are present, as on the (100)0.0, 
(111̅)0.5 and (100)0.287 surfaces, direct dissociation is the preferred pathway, while the presence of 
interstitial C atoms below the surface shifts the preference to H-assisted pathways (e.g., (010)0.25). 
Higher concentrations of subsurface C atoms weaken CO adsorption and increase the direct CO 
dissociation barrier, leading to a preference for H-assisted pathways. In line with results obtained by 
Chen et al.325, Broos et al. find that C atoms withdraw charge from the surface Fe atoms, leading to 
higher CO activation barriers. Petersen and Janse van Rensburg found similar barriers for H-assisted 

99 
 
and direct CO dissociation pathways on χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25, but they introduced a C vacancy, which led to 
a shift in the corresponding barriers279,323. With a C vacancy, the χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25 surface favors the 
direct pathway279. On (111̅)0.0, C-assisted pathways are competitive. The difference in CO activation 
mechanism between (111̅)0.5 and (111̅)0.0 illustrates the importance of surface termination. Note that 
the rates for CO activation on the various χ-Fe5C2 facets are all lower than for the stepped Co(112̅1). 
 
Figure 35. Reaction rates for direct CO dissociation (red) and H-assisted CO dissociation (blue: CHO*/HCO* pathway, green 
COH* pathway) on different χ-Fe5C2 facets. The rates are normalized to the rate of direct CO dissociation on Co(0001). The 
rates on Co(112̅1) are given as a reference. CO dissociation on the (010)0.25 surface with a C vacancy is denoted by (010)*0.25. 
The rates for the C-assisted pathway (CCO*) on the (111̅)0.0 are also calculated. The data for the χ-Fe5C2 (510)0.0 surface are 
taken from Pham et al.282 and values on cobalt are obtained from Liu et al.86 Reproduced with permission from ref.279, 
Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society under the CC BY-NC-ND 4.0 license (https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-
nd/4.0/). 
In a follow-up study, Broos et al. discussed CO dissociation mechanisms on the low-index cementite 
(θ-Fe3C) surface facets283. Cementite can form at high temperatures and low carbon-pressures, i.e., 
under HTFT conditions270. Deng et al. already investigated the θ-Fe3C(100) surface and found a H-
assisted dissociation barrier (via HCO*) of about 121 kJ/mol326, while Huo et al. reported a barrier of 
88 kJ/mol for direct CO dissociation on the θ-Fe3C(001) surface322. Broos et al. explored nine facets for 
CO adsorption and CO dissociation ((110), (01̅1), (101), (010), (111), (001), (011), (100) and (11̅1)), 
which were all cleaved at the origin283. On the (111) surface, a very low direct CO dissociation barrier 

100 
 
of 40 kJ/mol was calculated, strongly resembling the mechanism on α-Fe(111) which has a barrier of 
57 kJ/mol. The low barrier was associated with the B5-site configuration on these surfaces, similar to 
results for cobalt and ruthenium surfaces. The barrier on other corrugated surfaces, like (11̅1), is 
significantly higher (107 kJ/mol). On terrace-like surfaces, high direct dissociation barriers (up to 315 
kJ/mol for (011)) were found. CO dissociation, on the prevalent surface facets of the cementite phase, 
clearly has lower barriers than on the surface facets of the Hägg carbide. Cementite is hence suggested 
to be more active for CO dissociation. On surfaces without B5-sites, the H-assisted mechanism was 
calculated to be preferred. For the (100), (010), (01̅1) and (001) surfaces, barriers around 100 kJ/mol 
or lower were calculated. The formyl (HCO*) pathway was mostly followed, except on the (010) surface 
where the COH* pathway competes with the HCO* pathway and with direct CO dissociation. On the 
(011) surface, the C-assisted pathway was energetically feasible283. Microkinetic models of the various 
CO activation pathways confirmed that (111) and (11̅1) are indeed more active for the direct 
dissociation, while (01̅1) and (001) are most active for H-assisted CO dissociation, and (011) favors the 
C-assisted pathway. Further analysis showed that CO conversion rates multiplied by the relative 
contribution of each surface in a Wulff shape, are very comparable for (01̅1) and (111), with the direct 
CO dissociation on the (111) surface becoming dominant at realistic FTS pressures. In general, this 
study concluded that B5-sites are responsible for direct CO dissociation, and the most active surfaces 
(i.e., (111), (11̅1) and (01̅1)) make up 31% of the Wulff particle.  
Liu et al. also investigated the FT activity of the most stable surfaces50. On the most stable surfaces, CO 
adsorbs preferably at the Fe sites at hollow, bridge or top sites (∆Gad = -55 to -102 kJ/mol). Hydrogen 
adsorption is a crucial step in FTS. Therefore, the mobility and stability of H* on the most stable 
surfaces was computed. Only the χ-Fe5C2(510), the χ-Fe5C2(111) and η-Fe2C(111) surfaces adsorb H 
exergonically (∆Gad < 0 kJ/mol) and have a flat potential energy surface (PES) for H diffusion. The χ-
Fe5C2(510) was found to have the lowest Gibbs free energy barrier for CO activation (107 kJ/mol at 523 
K, 25 bar and H2/CO=2, Figure 36a)50. The direct dissociation at a surface C vacancy is favored over H-
assisted or C-assisted pathways, which had overall Gibbs free energy barriers of more than 200 kJ/mol. 

101 
 
CO dissociation is hence considered a two-step process (Figure 36b). First, Cs is hydrogenated to create 
a C vacancy. In a second step, CO adsorbs and dissociates to restore the carbide surface (Figure 36a). 
CH* hydrogenation is found to be the rate-determining step with a free energy barrier of 91 kJ/mol. 
Barriers larger than 170 kJ/mol for CH4 formation were computed, rendering methane formation less 
likely than vacancy formation via CH* diffusion, with Gibbs free energy barriers around 90 kJ/mol. 
Interestingly, CO dissociates on a 4-fold Fe site between two carbon atoms. This is very similar to the 
reaction site proposed by Banerjee et al. on cobalt catalysts, where nanoislands create B5 sites covered 
with carbon atoms in 4-fold sites (Figure 9)142. Similar to the work of Banerjee et al., a carbon vacancy 
needs to be created by hydrogenation and CO dissociates with a low barrier at the vacancy site.  
 
Figure 36. a) Gibbs free energy diagram for the lowest energy CO activation pathway mediated by the surface carbon vacancy 
(523 K, 25 bar, H2/CO=2). Cv denotes a carbon vacancy. b) molecular view of the active site during the reaction. Fe: orange, 
C: gray, H: white, O: red. Reproduced with permission from ref.50, Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society. 
In Co-based FTS, carbides are often associated with deactivation and methane formation, while iron 
carbides are considered the active phase under FT conditions. Cheng et al. used first principles 

102 
 
calculations (DFT) to rationalize this different behavior (Figure 37)327. Calculated CO dissociation 
barriers are higher on carbides than on metals. The CH4 formation barrier on Co2C(001) is lower than 
on stepped Co(0001), suggesting that cobalt carbides show high methane selectivity. These activity 
and selectivity trends can be rationalized in terms of C* and O* binding strength. Binding energies 
decrease from cobalt to cobalt carbides and from iron to iron carbides. On Fe-based catalysts, C* 
hydrogenation was proposed as the slow step. This step benefits from weaker adsorption. For Co-
based catalysts CO dissociation was considered as the slow step. Weaker carbon adsorption then 
reduces the activity.  
 
Figure 37. FT activity results from a balance of CO dissociation and C* and O* hydrogenation. While weaker binding improves 
the activity for iron carbides, it reduces activity for cobalt carbides. The volcano-shaped curve illustrates the effect of carbide 
formation on activity, with C* and O* binding strength as a descriptor. Reproduced with permission from ref.327, Copyright 
2010 American Chemical Society. 
Summarizing, four reaction pathways have been discussed for CO activation on Fe-based catalysts: 
• 
Direct CO dissociation at Fe sites is associated with relatively high barriers. It has been 
proposed on low-index surfaces with B5 sites279,282,283,323,327. Surfaces with Fe-B5 are rather 
unstable according to Zhao et al. and Liu et al.50,287. On the χ-Fe5C2(510) surface, direct CO 
dissociation can occur on the 4-fold Fe sites50,282,325,328,329; 

103 
 
• 
C-vacancy-mediated CO dissociation, with C vacancies created by surface Cs hydrogenation 
(Mars-van Krevelen mechanism) or by C-CO coupling, has been proposed for several 
surfaces313,320-323,326. Both direct CO dissociation and H-assisted CO dissociation are considered; 
• 
H-assisted CO dissociation via an HCO* or a COH* intermediate279,283,313,320,323,326, where 
generally the HCO* pathway is preferred, can occur on θ-Fe3C(01̅1)283, θ-Fe3C(001)283, θ-
Fe3C(100)326 and χ-Fe5C2(010)0.25279; 
• 
C-assisted CO dissociation via a CCO* intermediate, which undergoes hydrogenation steps and 
before C-O dissocation279,280,283, is related to the CO-insertion mechanism. On θ-Fe3C(011)283 
and χ-Fe5C2 (111̅)0.0279, the CO-insertion route can be competitive; 
5.3 Chain-growth 
Early DFT investigations by Li et al. evaluated chain-growth on metallic BCC Fe(111) and found CH* + 
CH* coupling to be kinetically favored with a barrier of 52 kJ/mol315.  
To predict the selectivity between methane and C-C coupling, Cheng et al. proposed the difference in 
effective barrier for C-C coupling and for methanation as a descriptor (∆Eeff)330. Smaller or negative 
values for ∆Eeff result in a high CH4 selectivity. Surfaces with a large ∆Eeff are therefore preferred for 
chain-growth. Interestingly, the selectivity towards CH4 decreases when the adsorption strength of C 
and H increases (i.e., ∆Eeff correlates with ∆HC+4H). In a follow-up study, the CH4 selectivity of carbides 
and metallic phases was compared for cobalt and iron327. On Fe5C2(100)0.287, the lowest barrier for C-C 
coupling was for C* + CH3*, with an effective barrier (i.e., including the thermodynamics of prior 
hydrogenation steps) of 187 kJ/mol. A ∆Eeff of -5 kJ/mol was calculated for this surface, and CH4 
formation is hence preferred over C-C coupling. Pham et al. used ∆Eeff as a descriptor to evaluate the 
selectivity for the χ-Fe5C2(510) surface331. The lowest effective barriers were found for C* + CH* and 
CH* + CH*. CO-insertion pathways were ruled out due to their high barriers. A high ∆Eeff (70 kJ/mol) 
and corresponding low CH4 selectivity were found for the χ-Fe5C2(510) surface331. On χ-Fe5C2(010) the 
effective activation energy barrier for methane formation is only 149 kJ/mol322, while on χ-

104 
 
Fe5C2(100)0.287 this barrier equals 182 kJ/mol327. These two surfaces with high methane selectivity have 
a more step-like character (Figure 38). These calculations are hence in line with the high methane 
selectivity of corners and edges proposed by Torres Galvis et al.60 
 
Figure 38. Top and side view of the χ-Fe5C2(100)0.287 surface with a Fe-B5 step-site (red). Fe: purple, C: gray. Reproduced with 
permission from ref.327, Copyright 2010 American Chemical Society. 
Cao et al. proposed a carbide mechanism for the formation of C2+ species on χ-Fe5C2(001)0.0320. Chains 
initiate with the formation of CCO* species or by the hydrogenation of surface carbon to CH* (Figure 
39). In a first path, C* couples with CCO* to form CCCO*. In other pathways, a series of hydrogenation 
steps followed by C-O dissociation form CCH2* or CHCH*. In the proposed cycle, both the carbide and 
CO-insertion mechanism are operative. Comparison of the CCO* formation barrier on more stable FeCx 
surfaces shows higher activation energies, i.e., the mechanism on more stable surfaces might be 
different. 

105 
 
 
Figure 39. Reaction energy diagram illustrating possible mechanisms for C2 and C3+ formation on Fe5C2(001)0.0 as proposed by 
Cao et al.320 Starting from CCO* + CH*, 4 C-C coupling options are considered. In the first pathway, CH* dehydrogenation to 
C* is followed by C* coupling to CCO* forming CCCO*. For the 3 other options, CCO* undergoes a few hydrogenation steps 
before C-O scission, forming CHCH* or CCH2*, which can couple with CH* in a following step. Note that these are not 
elementary reactions, but the highest barrier along this path is depicted. (X* + Y*) means both species are next to each other. 
The (a) and (b) subscripts refer to different adsorption sites, i.e., CCH2(b) has a different adsorption configuration than CCH2(a) 
Adapted from Cao et al.320 
The presence of adsorbed hydrogen can impact the preferred reaction mechanism. On χ-Fe5C2(510), 
the presence of hydrogen was proposed to shift the preferred CO adsorption site from the 4-fold site 
to the top site329. CO dissociation on this site will be increasingly difficult and consequently different 
CO activation pathways were considered. CHO insertion was proposed as the most likely coupling 
pathway, forming CHCHO* with a barrier of 106 kJ/mol, which is lower than the barrier for direct CO 
insertion (126 kJ/mol). On the other hand, the formation of methane only has an overall activation 
energy of 77 kJ/mol, which the authors link to the high methane selectivity during FTO. 
Yin et al. recently investigated the competition between CH4 and C2 formation on χ-Fe5C2288. Based on 
Wulff equilibrium shapes, the most stable surface facets were selected and investigated for the relative 
rates of C2 and CH4 formation. Two less common surfaces, (010) and (101̅), were also included as they 

106 
 
could have a high catalytic activity. The (111) and (101̅) surfaces were found to have high C2 formation 
rates. On these surfaces, C-C coupling is calculated to follow the carbide mechanism and occurs via the 
coupling of H-deficient species (e.g., C* + C*). The coupling of C* + C* has a calculated barrier of 122 
kJ/mol on Fe5C2(101̅) at very low hydrogen coverages (θH/θ* ~10-4). On the other hand, the (010), (110) 
and (111̅) are responsible for more than 96% of the methane formation, but comprising less than 25% 
of the Wulff particle surface. 
For cementite, Deng et al. proposed the formation of the CCO* species on θ-Fe3C(100)326 via adsorption 
of CO on surface carbon. Barriers of more than 150 kJ/mol were calculated for C-O scission, preventing 
chain growth. Hydrogenation is calculated to lower the C-O scission barrier to below 105 kJ/mol. In the 
proposed mechanism, C vacancies are formed, which might be beneficial for CO activation322. 
The θ-Fe3C(031) surface was found to be the most stable surface284. The ∆Eeff descriptor indicated that 
this surface is selective for C-C coupling. Coupling of CH* + CH* and CH2* + CH2* were found to be the 
dominant chain growth pathways. The effective barrier for C-C coupling is 96-97 kJ/mol, while the 
barrier for CH4 formation is 221 kJ/mol. The ∆Eeff descriptor is hence 124 kJ/mol. 
Oxygenates can also be formed during Fe-based FTS and Ozbek et al. suggested the possibility of 
forming higher alcohols by HCO* insertion into the growing chain (Figure 40a)332. To produce alcohols 
on an Fe-based catalyst, Chen et al. suggested a synergistic effect between the carbide phase and the 
oxide phase (Figure 40b)333. CO dissociation is proposed to occur on χ-Fe5C2 or ε-Fe2C, while CO adsorbs 
molecularly on Fe3O4. DFT calculations suggested that both H-assisted and direct CO dissociation 
mechanism prevail on ε-Fe2C(101) (∆Ga, direct = 195 kJ/mol and ∆Ga, H-assisted= 196 kJ/mol), while direct CO 
dissociation is favored on χ-Fe5C2(510) (∆Ga, direct = 111 kJ/mol). Free energy barriers for CO dissociation 
on Fe3O4(311) exceeded 400 kJ/mol. The proximity of the carbide and oxide phases is proposed to 
create an interface where CO* can insert in the growing chain. 

107 
 
a) 
 
b) 
 
Figure 40. a) Scheme of the MvK mechanism for the formation of alcohols and hydrocarbons. Adapted with permission from 
ref.332, Copyright 2015 Elsevier. b) Illustration of the formation of alcohols at the interface of FeOx and FeCx as proposed by 
Chen et al.333 On FeCx, CO adsorbs dissociatively and chain growth can happen, while on FeOx CO adsorbs molecularly.  
When determining the rate-limiting step of a proposed kinetic model, all reactions in the mechanism 
should be considered (i.e., CO activation, C-C coupling, C and O hydrogenation). In general, C 
hydrogenation has relatively low barriers compared to the other steps, i.e., C-C coupling and CO 
activation, and should not be rate-determining50,329. O* removal steps can have relatively high barriers 
and can therefore compete with CO activation and C-C coupling, similar to Co-based catalysts225. For 
example, Ozbek et al. found that O* removal has a significant barrier for H2O formation of about 100 
kJ/mol332 and Liu et al. found barriers of 200 kJ/mol for H2O formation on χ-Fe5C2(510)334. Liu et al. 
found that CO2 is the favored O* rejection pathway on χ-Fe5C2(510) (Ea = 134 kJ/mol). Chai et al. 
suggested O* removal as H2O as the rate-determining step, which could be accelerated by increasing 
H2 pressure273. 

108 
 
A next step in the modeling of Fe-based catalysts should include microkinetic models to account for 
the various possible elementary reaction steps in an unbiased way. Such models should also include 
realistic coverages of adsorbates, reaction intermediates and surface carbon. This requires the 
identification of the most important surface facets and active sites under reaction conditions. The 
complexity and phase evolution of the oxide and carbide phases is an important obstacle towards this 
goal, and might require the use of novel and efficient modeling techniques.  
5.4 Promoters in Fe-based Fischer-Tropsch 
To improve activity, selectivity and stability of Fe-based FT catalysts, structural and chemical promoters 
are often introduced48. Structural promoters, such as SiO2, Al2O3 and MgO, suppress sintering, stabilize 
the active phase or improve mechanical strength, but also limit reduction and reduce activity. Chemical 
promoters can improve reduction and carburization of the catalyst and enhance activity and 
selectivity. 
Alkali metals are often used as chemical promoters, since they enhance carburization, chain growth 
probability, suppress methane formation and inhibit secondary hydrogenation47,263,335-337. Na and K are 
the most common alkali metal promoters in FTS305. The lower hydrogenation ability, which increases 
olefin content, is attributed to a higher C:H surface ratio47. Alkali promotion also increases WGS rates 
and promotes carbon deposition via the Boudouard reaction336,338-340. Recently, Cheng et al. 
investigated the promoting effect of potassium on reduced graphene oxide supported Fe-based 
catalyst and observed a volcano behavior as a function of K loading341. Potassium was proposed to 
increase carburization rates, thereby increasing activity at lower loadings. At higher K loadings, a 
decrease in activity was observed, which was attributed to the role of K in increasing the size of the 
Hägg carbide particles, and to K-induced active site blocking by carbon deposition. 
Another model system that has been extensively studied is Fe-Na-S47. Sulfur modification of an 
unpromoted Fe catalyst lowers CO conversion, O/P ratio and chain-growth probability342. The 
combination of Na and S reduces CH4 selectivity, decreases chain-growth, increases olefin selectivity 

109 
 
and increases activity291,343-345. While Na and K are electropositive elements, S is electronegative, 
affecting the electron density of the Fe-based catalyst differently47. It is proposed that electron back-
donation towards surface intermediates is increased (for alkali metals) or decreased (for S), and the 
surface intermediates are stabilized or destabilized, respectively. This suggests increased dissociative 
adsorption of H2 and CO on alkali-promoted surfaces and decreased dissociative adsorption on S-
promoted surfaces. The unique properties of both promoters lead to an ‘alkali-like’ increased olefin 
selectivity and CO conversion and a ‘sulphur-like’ decreased α. The reduced CH4 selectivity is attributed 
to the combined ‘alkali-S’ effect256,344. This dual decoration of Fe-based catalysts seems to be a 
successful way to develop FTO catalysts. A more elaborate overview on FTO catalysts can be found in 
a review by Torres Galvis and de Jong46. 
It has been reported that the addition of moderate amounts of Mn enhances the activity, increases C2-
C4 selectivity, hydrocarbon formation and catalyst stability346-348. Addition of small amounts of Mn also 
enhances carburization rates and basicity346. The increased basicity is proposed to facilitate chain 
growth and improve dissociative CO adsorption. Using Mn-modulated Fe2O3 nanorods, the effect of 
the initial crystal phase (γ-Fe2O3 vs. α-Fe2O3) on the carburization and activity was investigated349. γ-
Fe2O3 preferentially forms C-poor carbides (θ-Fe3C) with a high selectivity for light olefins. Ding and 
coworkers developed a core-shell Fe3O4@MnO2 catalyst and suggested that electron transfer from the 
Mn oxide to the iron oxide could facilitate CO activation, while hydrogen spillover to MnO2 enhanced 
C-C coupling350. The lower hydrogenation ability significantly improved olefin selectivity. 
Cu addition to a Fe-Mn catalyst lowered chain-growth probability and favored formation of short-chain 
hydrocarbons, which was attributed to a decrease in surface basicity351. Weakened basicity was 
proposed to decrease the CHx* coverage and increase the H* coverage, resulting in a lower chain-
growth probability. Cheng et al. investigated a Fe-Mg-K catalyst supported on reduced graphene oxide 
and suggested that the synergy of Mg and K increased CO dissociation352. Addition of Mg inhibits 
magnetite formation, limiting WGS activity and CO2 formation. Ma et al. proposed that Mg facilitates 

110 
 
the dissociative adsorption of CO and hydrogen adsorption296. Interestingly, Mg also enhances α-Fe2O3 
dispersion and inhibits carburization, resulting in reduced chain growth, while Li promotes ε’-Fe2.2C 
formation. ε’-Fe2.2C was proposed to have a higher activity and a better C-C coupling ability than χ-
Fe5C2. 
Recently, the group of Khodakov has proposed mobile promoters such as Pb, Sb, Bi and Sn to enhance 
stability and reduce sintering of supported Fe-based FT catalysts353. These promotors all have a low 
melting point and therefore expected to be highly mobile under FT conditions. Soldering metals, i.e., 
Bi and Pb, are reported to increase olefin formation for silica supported iron catalysts354. A 2-3 fold 
increase in TOF and 10-fold higher yield to light olefins was observed for Fe-based catalysts supported 
on carbon nanotubes and promoted with Bi and Pb355. An important aspect in this study was the 
nanoconfinement of the Fe catalyst by the carbon nanotubes, which was suggested to enhance the 
effect of the promotors by the increased contact with the iron carbide. On silica-supported iron 
catalysts, a 7-10-fold increase in the TOF was reported with Sb and Sn promoters356. 
5.4.1 Molecular views of the role of promoters in Fe-based Fischer-Tropsch 
Computational studies have been used to begin to develop a molecular view of the role of promoters. 
To explain the reduced CH4 selectivity for Fe-Na-S catalysts, Xie et al. studied the effect of the 
counterion of Na256. Early on, it was thought that the alkali promoters are oxidized under FT conditions, 
leading to O as the counterion (Na2O)357. These electronegative counterions reduce charge donation 
from the electropositive promotor to the catalyst. DFT modeling suggested that the less 
electronegative S atom (in Na2S) draws less charge from Na and thereby increases charge donation to 
the catalyst256. In addition, the binding geometry of Na2S is proposed to improve charge donation to 
the C atoms. Charge donation is found to enhance H adsorption at the Fe site but weaken H adsorption 
at the C site. The combined effects reduce the H* coverage, especially for Na2S. Earlier experimental 
studies by Benziger and Madix indeed showed that O and S inhibited H2 dissociative adsorption, with 

111 
 
S being more effective357. The synergy between Na and S results in a lower CH4 selectivity and higher 
olefin selectivity. 
The charge of the Fe surface atoms affects the FT reaction and the addition of promoters, such as K, 
can alter the charge significantly. By investigating CO activation with DFT, Chen et al. correlated the 
activation energy with the charge of the involved Fe atoms325. Lower activation barriers were 
computed for less cationic Fe species (Figure 41b). Potassium donates electrons and hence reduces 
the charge on the Fe atoms. Interestingly, a similar correlation between charge and barrier is found for 
non-stoichiometric surfaces and for C vacancies (Figure 41b). C atoms withdraw electrons from the Fe 
atoms, increase the positive charge and increase the CO dissociation barrier.  
a) 
 
b) 
 
Figure 41. a) Structure of a typical χ-Fe5C2 surface (Fe atoms in blue and C atoms in gray) including a (non-)stoichiometric 
termination, a B5 site (orange), a carbon vacancy (yellow), and a promoter (purple). Reproduced with permission from ref.325, 
Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. b) Correlation between the direct CO dissociation barrier (Ea) and the average 
Mulliken charge (qM) of the surface Fe atoms involved in the CO activation for different Hägg carbide facets. Adapted from 
Chen et al.325 
DFT calculations by Petersen et al. showed that K significantly stabilizes O and CO adsorption on the χ-
Fe5C2(100)0.0 surface358. The high stability of oxygen hampers its removal. Zhao et al. found that the 
addition of K2O to the χ-Fe5C2 (100), χ-Fe5C2(111) and χ-Fe5C2 110) surfaces influences surface C* 
hydrogenation barriers359. Potassium alters the charge of the H* and surface C atoms and thus impacts 
the CH4 formation rate. 

112 
 
To understand the promoting effect of Na, Fe5C2(111) and Na2O/Fe5C2(111) were compared with 
DFT360. The addition of Na2O weakened propylene adsorption (from -79 kJ/mol to -53 kJ/mol), while 
the hydrogenation barrier to C3H7* remained very similar (65 kJ/mol vs. 64 kJ/mol). This suggests that 
propylene favors desorption over hydrogenation on Na2O/Fe5C2(111) (64 kJ/mol > 53 kJ/mol). The 
presence of Na2O suggests an electronic modulation, suppressing the hydrogenation of olefins. Liu et 
al. suggested that electron donation from Na2O to Fe atoms favors β-hydride abstraction, increasing 
olefin selectivity361.  
Using DFT calculations, Park et al. proposed an optimal K/Fe ratio of 0.075 for K-doped iron carbide 
catalysts362. On Fe5C2(010)0.25, the lowest CO dissociation barrier was found for a K/Fe ratio of 0.075, 
which exhibited the highest activity on a ~18 nm iron carbide catalyst. 
Computational studies indicate that MnO promotes the reduction of the iron oxides phase by spillover 
of oxygen from FeOx to oxygen vacancies in MnO361. Electron donation from Na2O to Fe3O4 enables 
oxygen vacancy formation, facilitating reduction.  
The activity, selectivity and structure of Fe-based FT catalysts can be heavily tuned by promoters. 
Electropositive or electronegative promoters are proposed to alter the C:H surface ratio, which 
controls methane and olefin selectivity. Additionally, the surface charge of the Fe atoms plays an 
important role in the selectivity and activity. The precise nature of the promoters remains to be 
identified, i.e., DFT models use both atomic promoters and oxides. Iron phase evolution, i.e., reduction 
and carburization, is enhanced by the addition of electron donating promoters or through oxygen 
spillover. The pronounced influence of alkali promoters on the carburization and the basicity of the 
catalytic surface and the corresponding phase evolution, will play an important role in developing 
catalysts for CO2-based Fischer-Tropsch (CO2-FT) synthesis. 
 

113 
 
6 CO2-FT: Revival of the century-old Fischer-Tropsch process? 
Recently, the European Commission presented its Green Deal to become the “first climate-neutral 
continent”363. Similar initiatives are proposed in the US364. While electrification of the economy is a key 
component of the Green Deal, direct electrification is not the best option for some sectors. Sustainable 
alternatives for fossil fuels remain necessary. One of the possible routes to sustainable alternatives for 
fossil fuels is the chemical valorization of CO2 with green hydrogen, which can be transformed to e-gas 
(methanation) or e-liquids (Fischer-Tropsch) for long-range transport and air travel. This Power-to-X 
(P2X) scheme uses renewable electricity to generate various chemical building blocks. The integration 
of electricity and CO2 in the current industry is therefore an important R&D direction365. The selective 
hydrogenation of CO2 to long-chain hydrocarbons is a challenging catalytic reaction, which requires 
multi-functional catalysts. Several routes to convert CO2 into valuable chemicals have been studied in 
recent years, ranging from traditional heterogeneous catalysis to electrocatalysis, homogeneous 
catalysis and photocatalysis366-383. Among them, the selective conversion of CO2 to fuel or olefins with 
a high carbon-efficiency (i.e., a high fraction of carbon ending up in the final product) has been gaining 
attention44,384-391. 
In this chapter, we provide a brief overview of the current research in CO2 hydrogenation to 
hydrocarbons (olefins and fuels), using the same catalysts as in FTS, i.e., Co, Fe and Ru. A handful of 
reviews focus more elaborately on the formation of hydrocarbons from CO2392-394. The review by 
Ronda-Lloret et al. focusses on olefin production395, while Ma and Porosoff highlighted the use of 
tandem catalysis in CO2-FT396. 
There are two main pathways to produce long-chain hydrocarbons: a direct route and an indirect 
route. In the indirect route, CO2 is converted into a more reactive intermediate such as CO or MeOH 
which is subsequently converted into hydrocarbons. This can be achieved by employing two different 
catalysts, either in a one-step or two-step reactor system. By combining the Fischer-Tropsch synthesis 
with the reverse water-gas shift reaction in a single catalytic process, both reactions are kinetically 

114 
 
coupled and much higher carbon-efficiencies can in principle be obtained, i.e., the direct route (Figure 
42)44. This route is generally assumed to be more economical and environmentally acceptable since it 
avoids using multiple steps, thereby reducing the CAPEX and OPEX. High CH4 selectivities and low 
chain-growth probabilities currently hamper the economic efficiency of the integrated process. 
 
Figure 42. Tandem CO2-FT mechanism on Fe and Co, coupling RWGS for CO2 activation with C-C coupling via FTS to form long-
chain hydrocarbons (i.e., the direct route). Mn and K are possible promoters that can facilitate this reaction44,386,397. 
One of the indirect routes goes via ‘oxy’ intermediates, often methanol, and is typically performed on 
metal oxide/zeolite catalysts (Figure 43)384,388. By employing catalysts with two distinct active sites, i.e., 
metal oxides and zeolites, methanol formation is coupled to the methanol-to-gasoline (MTG) or 
methanol-to-olefin (MTO) reaction. In 2016, Jiao et al. reported this tandem reaction over a catalyst 
system combining ZnCrOx with a mesoporous SAPO zeolite398. In the following years, many researchers 
have tried to use this tandem reaction to circumvent the ASF distribution observed for the direct CO2 
hydrogenation route, and thereby increase the selectivity towards fuels and light olefins384,385,388,399-406. 
 
Figure 43. The two routes to form hydrocarbons from CO2. 
Instead of using zeolites as C-C coupling catalysts, metal oxides can be used for both CO2 activation and 
C-C coupling. Geng et al. reported the combination of such a Fe-based tandem catalyst with zeolites 
to obtain branched hydrocarbons399. The iron carbide and oxide sites form paraffins and olefins directly 

115 
 
from CO2 hydrogenation, while the acid sites in the zeolite are proposed to increase the gasoline 
fraction in the product distribution by hydroisomerization. In the next sections, we will focus on the 
first part of this process, where CO2 is hydrogenated directly to hydrocarbons. 
Both Fe- and Co-based catalysts are excellent catalysts for the CO-FT reaction. Since Fe-based catalysts 
show both RWGS and FT activity, they are promising candidates for CO2-based FT. While CO activation 
and C-C coupling steps occur on the iron carbide sites (i.e., χ-Fe5C2, θ-Fe3C; Chapter 5)270,407, the sites 
responsible for the RWGS activity are not yet identified. Fe3O4 is active in the RWGS and has often been 
proposed as the active site for CO2 activation in CO2-FT302,408, but iron carbides can also activate CO2271. 
6.1 Co- and Ru-based CO2-FT 
The first catalyst of interest is cobalt, due to its excellent chain-growth capabilities. On cobalt, high CO 
partial pressures are necessary to induce chain-growth, as shown by DFT calculations by the Saeys 
group102, the Iglesia group215, and experiments by the Meunier group409 and the Weststrate 
group17,20,218,236. While the groups of Iglesia and Saeys mainly focussed on CO-FT, the group of Meunier 
investigated the surface of the cobalt catalysts under CO2 hydrogenation conditions. Using operando 
IR spectroscopy, they found that the selectivity towards methane is related to the CO* coverage409. 
When shifting from a CO-rich to a CO2-rich feed, the CO* coverage decreases and methane selectivity 
increases. A possible explanation for the increased methane yield is the higher H* coverage when CO* 
coverage decreases. Since cobalt can easily hydrogenate species, a larger amount of methane is 
formed when either the formation of monomers or chain-growth is too slow. Methane selectivities 
over 90% have been reported for CO2 hydrogenation on cobalt catalysts397,410. The dependence on the 
CO* coverage and consequently partial pressure of CO puts a clear limit to the selective conversion of 
CO2 to long-chain hydrocarbons for Co-based catalysts. 
Prieto et al. proposed that the limited CO* coverage on Co-based CO2-FT catalysts is related to 
thermodynamic bounds imposed by the RWGS reaction411. The thermodynamic equilibrium of the 
RWGS puts a limit on the CO partial pressure that can be obtained. In Figure 44, the chain growth 

116 
 
probability is plotted for different temperatures and H2/CO ratios. The CO-FT regio is indicated, as well 
as the RWGS equilibrium. In an ideal scenario, chemical equilibrium of the RWGS is reached and a 
relatively high partial pressure of CO is achieved, making both carbon oxides kinetically 
indistinguishable. When H2/CO2 = 3, the equivalent H2/CO ratio is always above 10 and the CO partial 
pressure below 1.8 bar (Figure 44). The corresponding chain growth probability of below 0.5-0.6 under 
these conditions results in a C5+ selectivity below 20%. Decreasing the H2/CO2 ratio from 3:1 to 1:1 was 
proposed to increase the expected C2+ selectivity (Figure 44)410.  
To increase the selectivity towards long-chain hydorcarbons, Riedel et al. added MnO as an active 
RWGS catalytic species397. Even then, high CH4 yields (95%) were observed.  
 
Figure 44. Contour plot of the chain growth probability (α), based on a single α model and parameters by Vervloet et al.412, 
as a function of temperature and H2/CO ratio (PCO). The white lines indicate the thermodynamic equilibrium limit for the 
RWGS at different feed conditions (H2/CO2=1 or 3) based on the Soave-Redlich-Kwong equation-of-state and Gibbs free 
energy minimization. The black box indicates typical FTS operating conditions.Reproduced with permission from ref.411, 
Copyright 2017 John Wiley and Sons. 
Recent microkinetic simulations also predict a high CH4 selectivity on cobalt catalyst for CO2-FT18. It 
remains a point of debate whether methane is formed on specific methanation sites154 or by the 
redirection of a common monomer CHx* pool towards methane rather than to long-chain 
hydrocarbons410. To mitigate excess methanation, (alkali) promoters can be added413,414. The 

117 
 
suppression of CH4 formation increases the C5+ selectivity, but the chain growth probability still does 
not exceed 0.55-0.65 due to the low equivalent CO partial pressure (Figure 44)411. Membrane reactors 
have been proposed to increase CO partial pressures415. On cobalt catalysts, high CO partial pressures 
and high CO coverages seem to be required to facilitate chain growth, yet, thermodynamically CO2-FT 
to long-chain hydrocarbons is favorable. Alternative mechanisms, facilitated by the selection of 
alternative promotors, could therefore possibly enable the formation of long-chain hydrocarbons. 
Building on mechanistic studies for ruthenium CO-FT catalysts, Mansour and Iglesia investigated the 
kinetic links between CO-FT and CO2-FT416. Kinetic and isotopic evidence revealed that the difficulty of 
CO2 hydrogenation does not lie in the chemical inertness of CO2, but rather in the intermediate 
formation of CO, and its strong C-O bond. Near-saturation CO adlayers are measured by IR 
spectroscopy and the similar coverage under CO-FT and CO2-FT conditions suggests that CO 
adsorption/desorption is quasi-equilibrated. For a CO2 partial pressure of 5 kPa, the same CO* 
coverage could be obtained during CO2-FT as during CO-FT with an equivalent CO partial pressure of 
0.2 kPa. Kinetic analysis suggested that CO2 formation is quasi-equilibrated and that the kinetically 
relevant transition state for CO2 consumption involves one O* and one H*, i.e., H2O formation is rate-
limiting with OH* formation as the kinetically relevant TS. While CO2 easily dissociates, CO activation 
was proposed to follow a H-assisted route, via HCOH*, similar to CO-FT (Figure 45). Chain-growth steps 
were not discussed.  

118 
 
 
Figure 45. Elementary reactions associated with CO2 hydrogenation forming CO, CH4 and H2O on ruthenium catalysts. 
Adapted from Mansour et al.416 
Despite the low chain-growth activity of Co-based catalysts in CO2 hydrogenation, a C5+ selectivity of 
over 50% was reported for a Co6/MnOx catalyst.386 This catalyst system was suggested to avoid the 
formation of CO intermediates and instead directly convert CO2 into the CHx* monomers. This was 
achieved by enhancing CO2 adsorption and weakening H adsorption, resulting in high C:H surface 
ratios.  The group of Weckhuysen investigated the effect of the support on CO2 hydrogenation over 
cobalt catalysts417. Metallic cobalt and cobalt oxides were found to both be active in CO2 hydrogenation 
and for the TiO2 support, cobalt oxide was reported to be more active than metallic cobalt for CO2 
hydrogenation. Again, limited chain growth was observed.  
On Co-based FT catalysts, high chain-growth probabilities seem to require high CO* coverages, and 
such high coverages are difficult to achieve during CO2-FT. If the necessity of high CO* coverages can 
be overcome, promoted cobalt catalysts could facilitate chain-growth during CO2-FT. Through the 
addition of suitable supports and promoters, new active sites could be introduced on Co-based 
catalysts, which might increase the C2+ selectivity418. By combining iron and cobalt, methane formation 
can be suppressed and olefin selectivity increased419-424. It is however very likely that the presence of 
Fe in bimetallic Fe-Co catalysts is the main reason for the higher selectivity towards C2+ products. 

119 
 
6.2 Fe-based CO2-FT 
The RWGS activity of Fe-based catalysts is often proposed to make them more suitable for CO2 
hydrogenation to long-chain hydrocarbons395. Recent studies however show that Co-based catalysts 
also readily convert CO2 to CO, so the RWGS activity of Fe-based catalysts is not the only reason for 
their higher chain-growth  probability during CO2-FT. Different bulk and surface phases are present 
under FT conditions, including metallic iron (α-Fe), iron carbides (χ-Fe5C2, θ-Fe3C) and iron oxides (α-
Fe2O3, Fe3O4)248. The composition of the catalyst is highly dependent on the structure of the precatalyst 
and on the carbon chemical potential under reaction conditions. The chapter on Fe-based FT discussed 
the importance of the iron carbides for the FT reactions270. The active phase for the RWGS reaction has 
not yet been identified. Fe3O4 is an effective catalyst for the high temperature (R)WGS and can coexist 
with FeCx species408,425. Kinetic modeling by Botes suggested that the WGS reaction and the FT reaction 
occur on different sites426. Botes did not identify the nature of the active sites, but suggested that the 
active site for the WGS reaction has a high affinity for water and hydroxyl groups. Similarly, Lox and 
Froment suggested a dual-site mechanism427. On the other hand, carbides have been proposed as the 
active phase for the RWGS activity271, and Davis and coworkers suggested FT and RWGS occur on the 
same type of site428. 
Riedel et al. tested Fe-based catalysts by varying the CO2 fraction in the syngas feed and found that 
conversion and selectivity were barely affected even when H2/CO2 was used instead of H2/CO397. 
Increasing the fraction of CO2 in the feed, at the same total pressure, resulted in a similar chain-growth 
probability and a CH4 selectivity below 15%. This early result indicated the potential for Fe-based 
catalysts in CO2 hydrogenation to hydrocarbons.  
Different from cobalt (and ruthenium) catalysts, FeCx catalysts typically have low CO* coverages256. 
This implies that a high CO* coverage is not required to induce chain-growth on Fe-based catalysts, 
alleviating the need for high CO partial pressures during the reaction. C5+ selectivity above 50% have 
indeed been obtained397,399. Schulz et al. showed that when H2/CO2 is used instead of H2/CO, the 

120 
 
development of FT activity is noticeably slower429. The limited concentration of the carburization 
agent, CO, for a H2/CO2 feed likely hampers carburization of the Fe-based catalyst411. To promote CO2-
FT reaction, promoters that accelerate carburization are likely necessary. Alternatively, the catalyst 
needs to be carburized prior to the CO2-FT reaction. 
The complex phase behavior in Fe-based CO-FT is also expected for Fe-based CO2-FT catalysts. Indeed, 
Riedel et al. identified different ‘episodes of synthesis’ during the CO2-FT reaction, with strong 
compositional and structural changes in each episode271. The changing composition during each 
episode resulted in different dominant reactions. At the start of the reaction, mainly carbon deposition 
was observed. At a later stage, the RWGS became dominant. The FT activity only increased once iron 
carbides were formed from metallic iron.  
Skrypnik et al. more recently investigated the structural changes of an unpromoted Fe-based catalyst 
during CO2 hydrogenation430. While the fresh catalyst was mainly a mixture of metallic iron and iron 
oxides, Mössbauer spectroscopic analysis of the spent catalyst bed suggested that iron carbides (e.g., 
χ-Fe5C2, θ-Fe3C) were formed mainly in the upstream layer of the catalyst bed. At higher CO2 
conversions, i.e., downstream in the catalyst bed, the fraction of carbides decreased and a larger 
fraction of Fe3O4 was observed, suggesting that iron carbides were converted back into Fe3O4 by 
product H2O. The spatial composition of the catalyst bed was also dependent on the initial catalyst 
composition, i.e., the pretreatment procedure. The differences in the spatial composition of the fresh 
catalyst were proposed to lead to different kinetics for the formation of carburizing CO and oxidizing 
H2O, which was suggested to determine the final steady-state composition of the catalyst bed. The 
structural evolution under CO2 hydrogenation conditions of α-Fe2O3 and γ-Fe2O3 into iron carbides was 
further investigated by operando Raman spectroscopy and XRD431. Starting from α-Fe2O3, mainly χ-
Fe5C2 was formed, while starting from γ-Fe2O3, θ-Fe3C was formed. The latter catalyst had superior 
chain-growth capabilities, while the former mainly produced light olefins. 

121 
 
By the addition of promoters, the selectivity can be directed towards olefins or jet fuel. Yao et al. used 
the Organic Combustion Method to prepare a Fe-Mn-K catalyst with a jet fuel selectivity of 47.8% at 
38.2% CO2 conversion44. Using different promoters, Barrios et al. found that the combination of an 
alkali metal with a complementary promoter increases the selectivity towards light olefins432. 
Additionally, promoters can help accelerate the carburization rate of the catalyst433.  
Recent results show the potential of Fe-based FT catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation into C2+ 
hydrocarbons. To obtain the desired selectivity towards fuels or olefins, modifications, i.e., promoters, 
are necessary, especially for Co-based catalysts. While initial efforts focused on the creation of active 
Fe-based CO2 hydrogenation catalysts, a clear molecular view of the active sites is still missing.  
6.3 Promoters in CO2-FT 
Several promoters have been proposed for Fe-based FT, affecting both the nature of the active phase 
(oxide and carbide phase stability) and the rate of the kinetically relevant steps (CO activation, C-C 
coupling, hydrogenation). To obtain high olefin and long-chain hydrocarbon yields in CO2-FT, 
promoters will be crucial. The direct CO2-FT route most likely follows a tandem mechanism where CO2 
is first activated, e.g., via the RWGS reaction, to form the monomer which then undergoes chain-
growth. 
Alkali metal promoters such as Na and K enhance the surface basicity and the carburization of Fe-based 
catalysts, which has been linked to a higher chain growth probability385,434,435. Surface basicity enhances 
CO2 adsorption, while carburization facilitates the formation of the carbide phases which are active for 
COx hydrogenation and C-C coupling435,436. Microcalorimetric studies showed that addition of 1 wt.% 
Na increased the initial heat of CO2 adsorption and CO2 saturation coverage by a factor of 5.7436. 
Enhanced CO2 adsorption can increase CO2 conversion, but also reduce the hydrogenation ability of 
the catalyst and decrease methane selectivity437. Potassium decreased the methane selectivity and 
increased C5+ selectivity fivefold433. Potassium may further hinder readsorption of olefins (both are 
electron donors) and consequently limit the formation of saturated hydrocarbons437. 

122 
 
Since carburization is more difficult under CO2-FT conditions429, promoters need to increase the 
carburization rate. Fischer et al. showed, using operando magnetometry under industrially relevant 
conditions, accelerated carburization of highly alkalized Fe-based catalysts in CO2-FT433. This 
acceleration is probably related to the increased rate of the RWGS reaction, which increases the carbon 
chemical potential411. The carburization rate for CO-FT increased by the basicity of the alkali oxide or 
salt (unpromoted = Li < Na < K < Rb < Cs)438. The presence of alkali metals is suggested to weaken the 
C-O bond by back-donation from the alkali metal and increases the Fe-C bond strength. 57Fe Mössbauer 
spectroscopy showed that potassium promotion of a Fe-based catalyst increased the Hägg carbide 
content in the catalyst by almost 50%439. Moreover, synergy between the honeycomb-structured 
graphene support and the potassium promoter was proposed to stabilize an extra phase, ε’-Fe2.2C, 
which has been reported to be active for FT273,296. 
Whether Na and K promotion favor light olefins or long paraffins depends on several factors, such as 
loading and the presence of other promoters. One of these additional promoters is Mn. Fe/Na catalysts 
decorated with Mn showed increased light olefin over C5+ selectivity440. XRD and XPS measurements 
showed a strong interaction between Mn and Fe, which was reported to weaken CO adsorption. CO* 
is a possible intermediate during CO2 hydrogenation, and the reduced concentration of CO* was 
proposed to reduce the coverage of C* necessary for chain-growth. Additionally, Mn was proposed to 
facilitate carbide (χ-Fe5C2) formation. 
Another popular promoter is Cu. Copper has a positive effect on both the reduction of FeOx and the 
subsequent carburization to the Hägg carbide441. A delafossite (CuFeO2)-derived Fe-Cu catalyst gave a 
high C5+ selectivity of 66.3% and 31% C2-C4 (O/P=7.3) at 573 K, 1 MPa, H2/CO2=3 and conversion of 
17.3%379. Addition of copper to form a Fe-Cu alloy supported on Al2O3 enhanced the C2-C7 hydrocarbon 
formation and suppressed methane formation391. 
Zn has been proposed to enhance light olefin production in CO2-FT389,442. The formation of the 
irreducible ZnFe2O4 spinel structure improves the stability of the catalyst389. On the other hand, Choi 

123 
 
et al. reported that a ZnFe2O4 spinel structure containing Na residue is an excellent catalyst precursor 
for CO2 hydrogenation443. Here, the ZnFe2O4 structure is reduced at 400 °C with H2 to form iron particles 
covered with ZnO, which acts as a structural promoter. While Zn is an excellent structural promoter360, 
some electronic effects were also observed. Falbo et al. showed that the addition of Zn increases the 
surface basicity and increases CO2 adsorption and conversion444. The Zn-promoted catalyst produced 
more heavy and less-saturated hydrocarbons, which the authors attributed to the high C:H surface 
ratio. 
Despite the obvious advantages of promoters, an unpromoted iron-based catalyst has been developed 
for CO2 hydrogenation, with a selectivity of 12% for CH4, 40% for C5+ and 36% for C2-C4 hydrocarbons 
at 36% conversion445. More recently, another unpromoted Fe-based catalyst was synthesized by 
controlled iron oxalate decomposition430. This catalyst had a CH4 selectivity below 10% and C2+ 
selectivity of 50% at 25% CO2 conversion. 
An impressive number of studies has been presented on CO2 hydrogenation to hydrocarbons. To 
predict which promoters increase selectivity for light olefins, Yang et al. performed a statistical analysis 
of the available literature data446. Based on this analysis, they synthesized a Fe-Mn-K catalyst with a 
light olefin selectivity of 30.4% at 42.3% CO2 conversion. They proposed a dual-site mechanism with 
Fe3O4 as the active site for the RWGS reaction and the χ-Fe5C2 as the active site for the FT reaction. The 
χ-Fe5C2 phase is formed during catalyst activation and Fe3O4 is formed in situ by CO2/H2O oxidation 
during the first hours of the reaction. 
The support also influences catalyst activity and selectivity, e.g., via oxygen spillover. Huang et al. 
investigated ZrO2 supports for CO2 hydrogenation over Fe-based catalysts and found a higher activity 
and selectivity for light olefins for supports with a higher surface area and a higher basicity447.  
Parastaev et al. used a ceria-zirconia (CZ) support to investigate the effects of metal-support 
interactions between cobalt and ceria448. The interactions were controlled by adjusting the size of the 
support, which could be manipulated with the calcination temperature. XRD, scanning transmission 

124 
 
electron microscopy-energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (STEM-EDX) and TEM suggested that 
supports have to be sufficiently large to facilitate strong metal-support interactions, but not too large 
to maintain a sufficiently large surface area. IR spectroscopy, NAP-XPS and XAS confirmed the 
formation of oxygen vacancies on the support during reduction treatment by reverse oxygen spillover 
at low T (<400 °C) and by hydrogen spillover from the catalyst particle to the support at high T (>400 
°C). In both cases, oxygen was removed as H2O. After exposure to CO or CO2 at 50°C or 300°C, oxidation 
of the support occurred via oxygen spillover from the catalyst particle to the support. By exploiting the 
rich chemistry of the Co-CZ metal-support interactions, metal particles could be stabilized while CO2 
activation could be enhanced. 
Strong metal-support interactions can also be detrimental for catalytic activity, as they sometimes 
impede the carburization of Fe-based catalysts449. To avoid strong metal-support interactions, 
chemically inert supports based on carbon, such as carbon nanofibers, carbon nanotubes or graphene, 
can be used. For example, graphene is an interesting support because of its high surface area, 2D 
structure and moderate metal-support interactions341. The moderate metal-support interactions 
increase the degrees of reduction and carburization. Another carbon-based support, honeycomb-
structured graphene, was synthesized and tested as a support for CO2-FT439. This structure combines 
the interesting properties of graphene with a 3D porous structure, which limits the agglomeration of 
catalyst particles and provides efficient heat dissipation. An Fe-based catalyst supported on 
mesoporous carbon with an average pore size of 6.9 nm converted CO2 into C5+ hydrocarbons with a 
selectivity of 44.5% at 50.6% conversion450. This was attributed to the easy formation of iron carbides 
within the pores of the support, which was proposed to be enhanced by weak metal-support 
interaction. 
6.4 Molecular views on the CO2-FT mechanism 
Lee et al. proposed a mechanism which starts with CO2 adsorption on an Fe atom, followed by 
hydrogenation steps451. The main proposed pathway forms CH2*, while side-reactions form CO, 

125 
 
formate, methanol and formaldehyde. In a next step, either methane is formed from CH2* or CO2 
couples to CH2*. Subsequent hydrogenation steps form C2H4*, which can desorb as ethylene, be 
hydrogenated to ethane, or undergo a second CO2 insertion. No experimental or theoretical evidence 
was given for this CO2-insertion mechanism. Most evidence however suggests that the CO2-FT reaction 
mechanism resembles the CO-FT mechanism, i.e., the RWGS reaction is coupled with the CO-FT 
reaction. 
For CO2 hydrogenation, monomer formation (either CO* or CHx*) is an important step, which likely 
involves the reverse water-gas shift reaction408. An experimental kinetic analysis for a Fe-based catalyst 
found that the FT reaction is slower than the RWGS step452, but this is not necessarily always valid. 
Recently, kinetic LHHW models are also being constructed for CO2-FT on Fe-based catalysts453. DFT 
calculations for CO2-FT are limited, therefore studies on metallic Fe will also be considered.  
Wang et al. considered CO2 activation on metallic iron454. The Fe(111) facet was suggested as the most 
active facet, due to its superior ability to adsorb and activate CO2. In their mechanism, CO2 activation 
preferably goes via a HCOO* intermediate with an activation energy of 52 kJ/mol. DFT calculations on 
FeCu(100) and Fe(100) showed different pathways for CO2 activation on monometallic iron and 
bimetallic FeCu455,456. For bimetallic catalysts, the formation of HCOO* (Ea= 38 kJ/mol) was preferred, 
while direct CO2 dissociation was preferred on Fe(100), with an activation energy of 77 kJ/mol. On Fe-
Cu, CO* is thus not necessarily formed and CH* might be formed via HCOOH* and HCO* intermediates. 
Further chain-growth was also investigated for these surfaces456. On Fe(100), CH4 formation was 
kinetically favored, while on Fe-Cu(100), C2 formation via CH* + CH* coupling was found to be 
kinetically favored.  
Han et al. compared direct and H-assisted CO2 dissociation pathways on Fe, FeOx and FeCx surfaces457. 
The calculations favored direct CO2 dissociation on these surfaces, while OH* removal was found to be 
rate-limiting. The highest CO formation rate was calculated for the Fe3O4 surfaces, suggesting Fe3O4 as 
the active phase for the RWGS reaction. Nie et al. also investigated CO2 adsorption and activation on 

126 
 
Fe(100), χ-Fe5C2(510) and Fe3O4(111)458. Low activation energies (<55 kJ/mol) for direct CO2 
dissociation were found on K-promoted Fe(100) and χ-Fe5C2(510). Potassium promotion increased CO2 
adsorption on these surfaces and reduced the CO2 dissociation barrier on Fe(100) to 53 kJ/mol. On 
Fe3O4(111), high CO2 dissociation barriers were calculated regardless of the addition of K.  
Recently, a theoretical study of CO2 activation on iron carbides has been performed459. On the Hägg 
carbide (χ-Fe5C2), the (100), (111̅), (101̅), (510), (111) and (110) were considered. While the (510) facet 
was found to have a low activation energy of 23 kJ/mol for direct CO2 dissociation, the H-assisted 
pathway (via HCOO*) was preferred on (111) and (100), with activation energies below 30 kJ/mol for 
HCOO* formation. 
The activity of Ru(0001) in the CO2-FT reaction was investigated by Zhang et al. using DFT 
calculations460. They proposed that adsorbed CO2* is first hydrogenated, forming HCOO*, which 
dissociates to HCO* and O*. Further dissociation to CH* produces the monomer necessary for chain-
growth or CH4 formation. The selectivity was dependent on the balance between CH4 formation and 
chain growth, i.e., the competition of the CH* + H* and CH* + CH* reactions. Zhang et al. suggested 
that normal pressures and high H2/COx ratios lead to methane while high pressure and low H2/COx 
ratios lead to chain-growth. 
Descriptor-based analysis can be used to evaluate the effect of promoters. By employing a microkinetic 
model and CO2* and OH* adsorption as descriptors, Zn and Cu were proposed as promoters to 
accelerate the RWGS reaction on the Fe3O4 active phase by facilitating OH* removal457. Using the Allen 
scale of electronegativity as a descriptor, Yang et al. investigated the activity and selectivity of CO2 
hydrogenation on Fe-based catalysts461. Promoters were suggested to affect the local electronic state 
of iron in iron carbides, which in turn affects the adsorption and dissociation of the important 
intermediates. Promoters with low-energy valence electrons hinder the adsorption of CO and H2 but 
increase CO2 adsorption and dissociation. Such descriptor-based analysis might help tailor catalysts for 
CO2 hydrogenation. 

127 
 
The field of CO2-FT has grown exponentially in the last five years, resulting in a large number of 
experimental studies. Theoretical studies have not been able to keep up, and a large knowledge gap 
remains for a molecular view on CO2-FT over Co- , Ru- and Fe-based catalysts. 
 

128 
 
7 Conclusion and outlook 
Since its discovery in 1923, the Fischer-Tropsch reaction has attracted wide academic interest. 
Research was initially centered in Germany, focusing on the rapid development of commercial plants. 
After World War II, the FT process attracted international interest, and especially in South-Africa, 
SASOL build several FT plants in the 50s. At that time, the first mechanistic studies also appeared, and 
various surface polymerization mechanisms were proposed: several version of the carbide 
mechanisms, the CO-insertion mechanism and the hydroxycarbene mechanism. The 80s and 90s saw 
a new wave of FT plants. Concurrently, detailed kinetic studies were performed, and the first 
mechanism-based LHHW models appeared to better describe the FT activity and selectivity. 
In recent years, a molecular bottom-up approach has emerged to begin to unravel the complex FT 
mechanism. Surface science and DFT studies put the focus on a molecular view of both the active sites 
and the reaction mechanism, bringing us one step closer to creating a molecular movie of this catalytic 
reaction. This review provides an overview of these recent developments and focuses on creating a 
molecular view of the active sites and the mechanism for Co- and Fe-based Fischer-Tropsch synthesis. 
Catalyst nanoparticles react dynamically to the reaction environment and active sites often only form 
under reaction conditions. Efforts to understand and quantify this dynamic behavior are emerging both 
experimentally and computationally. Theoretical models have been proposed to construct 
nanoparticles, ranging from Wulff equilibrium shapes to atomistic models. While Wulff structures 
identify the most stable surfaces on large nanoparticles (>10 nm), most catalytic nanoparticles are 
smaller than 10 nm. Modeling nanoparticles containing up to ~10 000 atoms is beyond current DFT 
and alternative methods have been proposed, ranging from broken bond models to machine learning 
potentials. Two types of active sites emerge in these atomistic models of Co and Ru nanoparticles: 
terrace sites and B5 step-edges. New developments in characterization techniques make it possible to 
image individual catalyst particles with nanometer scale resolution. Moreover, in situ and even 
operando techniques are becoming common. A similar revolution is taking place in computational 

129 
 
catalysis. In the short-term, it is expected that dynamic simulations using dedicated machine learning 
potentials will be able to accurately model the distribution of active sites for realistic catalyst 
nanoparticles under virtual reaction conditions, so-called operando computational catalysis.  
Surface science studies and DFT calculations suggest that the Co(0001) terrace reconstructs under 
reaction conditions, while operando TEM images of the working catalyst and Wulff particles based on 
DFT calculations show limited changes on the particle level. Additionally, CO* seems to form stable 
overlayers on cobalt terraces under FT conditions, resulting in a high saturation coverage. SSITKA 
studies find a reversibly bound CO* coverage of ~0.4-0.5 ML under FT conditions. A molecular view of 
the Co or Ru FT catalyst will need to account for this extra layer of complexity. 
Two important reaction steps have been investigated: CO activation and C-C coupling. Most studies 
have proposed that C-O activation is rate-limiting. Recent research suggests that oxygen removal or 
carbon hydrogenation could control the reaction rate as well. On cobalt catalysts, step-edge and 
terrace sites have been proposed as the active site for CO activation, and two main mechanisms have 
been proposed as well: direct and H-assisted CO activation. At the terrace sites, the high CO* coverages 
impact the CO activation mechanism. 
Theoretical studies and surface science experiments do not yet agree on a definitive mechanism for 
chain-growth. The majority tends to support the carbide mechanism, while the CO-insertion 
mechanism still receives some support. Recent reports agree that alkylidyne species are the resting 
state of the growing chain, especially at high CO* coverage conditions. Microkinetic models were 
proposed to resolve this long-standing debate, but results depend heavily on the underlying DFT 
methods and the completeness of the mechanism. Flexible machine-learning methods can reduce the 
computational burden to construct these models by reserving the most expensive calculations (or 
experiments) for the kinetically most relevant steps. 
The dynamic phase evolution of iron oxides and iron carbides during FT complicates the development 
of a molecular view of the active sites and mechanism of Fe-based catalysts. There is some consensus 

130 
 
that the active phase is a mixture of carbides. This is a first step towards the identification of the active 
site. For every carbide, however, many surface terminations are possible, dramatically increasing the 
number of active site candidates. Methods that can handle this combinatorial complexity will be 
needed to identify the active sites in Fe-based FT. While most studies of iron carbide nanoparticles 
have used the Wulff approach, recent studies again showed the potential of dedicated MLPs to create 
atomistic models. Large-scale dynamic simulations using machine learning potentials can begin to fill 
this important knowledge gap, while operando experimental characterization techniques and surface 
science studies on model carbides provide a molecular view of surface of iron carbides under FT 
conditions.  Several experimental studies indicate that the surface of Fe-based carbide catalysts might 
be partially covered by carbonaceous deposits, but, in general, the coverage of reaction intermediates 
is often expected to be low for Fe-based FT.  
The mechanistic view of Fe-based Fischer-Tropsch is less intensively developed than for cobalt and 
ruthenium systems. While initial LHHW models assumed metallic iron as the active phase, most DFT 
studies now point towards a Mars-van Krevelen-type mechanism, where surface carbon is 
hydrogenated to create a C vacancy. CO dissociation is proposed to occur faster on this C vacancy. 
Interestingly, this mechanism is very similar to one of the mechanisms proposed for CO activation on 
carbon-covered cobalt edge-sites, suggesting that the active site on both catalysts might not be so 
different.  Studies of the C-C coupling mechanism are limited, and a molecular view of the mechanism 
for Fe-based FT catalysts is not yet available. We see opportunities to develop microkinetic models 
that account for these recent mechanistic insights, and that allow to compare various mechanistic 
proposals in an unbiased way. These efforts will be greatly facilitated by recent surface science studies 
on iron carbide systems and by mechanistic studies using SSITKA and/or CTK at FT conditions. Such an 
approach might help prevent the heated mechanistic debates that we have seen for Co- and Ru-
systems. 

131 
 
The last chapter discusses the potential of reviving this 100-year-old reaction to produce sustainable 
fuels. CO2 hydrogenation, with green hydrogen, to fuels or olefins provides an opportunity to close the 
carbon cycle. Initial research shows promising results with conventional Fe-based catalysts, which 
might be further improved by the selection of promotors and supports. Since Co-based catalysts 
require high CO coverages to drive chain growth, CO2 hydrogenation on cobalt is hampered by the 
thermodynamics of the RWGS reaction. CO2-FT could be beneficial for hard-to-abate sectors, which 
remain heavily dependent on fossil fuels, and cannot make a complete transition to a hydrogen-based 
economy. 
 
 

132 
 
Author Biographies 
Konstantijn Rommens obtained his Master’s Degree in Chemical Engineering in 2019. He is currently a 
PhD student at the Laboratory for Chemical Technology at Ghent University, under the supervision of 
professor Mark Saeys. His PhD concerns the development of machine learning potentials for Co- and 
Fe-based catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation to jet fuel. 
Mark Saeys obtained his PhD from Ghent University in 2002. From 2003 to 2014, he was a professor 
of chemical engineering at the National University of Singapore. Since 2014, he is a full professor at 
the Laboratory for Chemical Technology at Ghent University, Belgium. In 2013, he received the 
Odysseus Award from the Research Foundation-Flanders to establish a research program on modeling-
guided catalyst design at Ghent University. His research combines modeling-guided catalyst design 
with experimental kinetic validation and state-of-the-art characterization to unravel and optimize 
catalytic processes.

for financial support. This work received funding from the Flemish Research Foundation under the 
Odysseus grant (FWO G0E5714N).
